PREFACE

Koch’s treatise on melody is the ideal counterpoise to a previous volume of
this series, Johann Philipp Kirnberger’s The Art of Strict Musical Composi-
tion. Whereas Kirnberger concentrated on harmonic progression within the
strict contrapuntal style, Koch confronts the elusive art of writing melody, and
that mostly from the standpoint of instrumental music. He demonstrates that
it is no less amenable to step-by-step instruction.

Somewhere between the strenge Satz and the freie Satz (Kirnberger’s
terms) Koch recognized a singbare Satz begging for pedagogic attention. It
was a manner of writing associated with the Italian composers, of simplified
harmonic syntax and subdued contrapuntal activity, which allowed the lis-
tener to focus his attention on the progress of melodic ideas. By the time Kirn-
berger was completing the part of the treatise translated in our series—1771 to
1776—this new style had triumphed, having been assimilated into the practice
of composition we know as the classic style. Melodic and motivic invention
and elaboration were now the crux of the creative act, the driving force for
which harmony served as a vehicle.

Melody was neglected in the pedagogical scheme of figured-bass instruc-
tion combined with strict counterpoint that dominated seventeenth-century
Italian and eighteenth-century German training of musicians. Johann Matthe-
son, himself an author of a thorough-bass treatise, set out to fill the gap with
his Kern melodischer Wissenschaft (1737), later incorporated into Der
vollkommene Capellmeister (1739). He anticipated Koch in pursuing the anal-
ogy between a melodic composition and a well-elaborated, properly punctu-
ated speech. It was inevitable that a linear view of music should lead to a rhet-
oric of periodicity, subject-predicate, main and subordinate ideas, invention,
elaboration, and execution, and all the other marks of a literalness of thinking
that, if they never altogether fit the multilayered texture of music, made a
deep impression on how people created and experienced it. Koch reminds us
that this literal-mindedness was more than an analogy. To think about music
rhetorically must have seemed then as natural to composers as developing a
verbal argument was to the educated man. One could not begin a composi-
tion, any more than a speech, without a subject, and from its choice flowed the
obligation to stick to it and develop it as fully as the subject and occasion de-
manded.

A product of the Enlightenment and of Sulzer’s aesthetic and encyclopedic
program, Koch could not domesticate his novel conceptions to the technical

xO)


============================================================
PAGE 11
============================================================

x 19 PREFACE

vocabulary of his time. The wide frame of reference of his terminology con-
stantly tests the translator’s ingenuity and breadth. It would be easy to render
itin a musical jargon, but this would damp its resonance. Anlage is drawn from
the visual arts; Redetheile, Perioden, Cdsur are verbal references; Ruhepunkte
des Geistes awakens the image of a breathing organism; and many more exam-
ples could be cited. Nancy Kovaleff Baker has lovingly listened for these reso-
nances and echoes. She has inhabited Koch’s terminological environment for
about ten years, since preparing her study of Koch’s theoretical work as a dis-
sertation, which I had the privilege of overseeing. In the course of these years
she has settled into a congenial family of terms, which, even if some strike one
at first as alien to music theory, give off many of the same vibrations as the
original language.

Koch can serve as a corrective to the current fascination with systems of
tonal and harmonic analysis and put us in touch once more with the character-
istic musical oratory of the classic period. Although he always kept an ear to
the tonal goals and harmonic implications—for die Melodie harmonisch zu
denken was rooted in his musical personality—he was intent on inventing and
elaborating logically articulated successions of finely honed melodic state-
ments, which alone possessed the uniqueness to embody individual expres-
sions of feeling, for him the noblest goal of musical art.

This is the second translation in the series that owes to the National Endow-
ment for the Humanities Translations Program prestigious and material sup-
port. It is our first to enjoy the precisely elegant computer-generated notation
of musical examples, programmed and designed by Thomas Hall of A-R Edi-
tions.

CLAUDE V. PALISCA
Editor, Music Theory Translation Series

INTRODUCTION

The Versuch einer Anleitung zur Composition of Heinrich Christoph Koch
(1749-1816) is only now beginning to arouse the interest it deserves. Pub-
lished in three volumes over an eleven-year period, in 1782, 1787, and 1793, it
is notable both for its range of material and for its singleminded aim—
pedagogy.! Although Koch’s purpose was to instruct the beginner in the art of
composition, the historian of music will find the treatise instructive in a
broader sense. Koch was, in the words of Francois Joseph Fétis, ‘“‘an educated
musician who united a perfect knowledge of practice with theory.’’* He drew
upon the contemporary repertoire to formulate his theories and also to illus-
trate his treatise. Because of its close connections to current practice,

Composition, was published by Adam F. Bohme of Leipzig; the first volume was printed in
Rudolstadt and the remainder in Leipzig. Georg Olms Verlag issued a facsimile edition of the
Versuch (Hildesheim, 1969), from which the present translation was made. Throughout this
work, those footnotes which specify volume numbers for the Versuch always refer to the Ger-
man version; where no other specific work is indicated, it is understood that the reference is to
the Versuch. The first volume of Koch’s work was reviewed in Carl F. Cramer, ed., Magazin der
Musik (Hamburg, 1783-86; facs. ed., 2 vols. in 4, Hildesheim, 1971), 7 December 1783,
2:1304-08. The same review also appeared the next year in Johann N. Forkel, ed., Musikali-
scher Almanach fiir Deutschland (Leipzig, 1784), pp. 1-4. The principal sources which discuss
Koch in varying degrees of detail are: Peter Benary, Die deutsche Kompositionslehre des 18.
Jahrhunderts (Leipzig, 1961); Arnold Feil, ‘‘Satztechnische Fragen in den Kompositionslehren
von F. E. Niedt, J. Riepel und H. Chr. Koch” (Diss., Ruprecht-Karl University, Heidelberg,
1955); Leonard G. Ratner, ‘‘Harmonic Aspects of Classic Form’’ (Ph.D. diss., University of
California at Berkeley, 1947), ‘‘Harmonic Aspects of Classic Form,” Journal of the American

Structure,’ Musical Quarterly 42 (1956): 439-54; Fred Ritzel, Die Entwicklung der ‘‘Sonaten-
form” im musiktheoretischen Schrifttum des 18. und 19. Jahrhunderts, 2d ed. (Wiesbaden,

nal of the American Musicological Society 24 (1971): 85-95, and ‘“‘Theme, Harmony, and Tex-

phony,” Studi musicali 9 (1980): 303-16.

2. Francois Joseph Fétis, Biographie universelle des musiciens et bibliographie générale de la
musique, 8 vols. (Paris and Brussels, 1835-44), ‘‘Koch (Henri-Christophe),”’ 5:366: ‘‘un musi-
cien instruit qui unissait une parfaite connaissance de la pratique 4 la théorie.”’

Sf

therefore, the Versuch is of great historical significance; it provides valuable
perspective on the manner in which the art of composition was then regarded
and it gives numerous insights into the musical styles and ideas of the eight-
eenth century.

Heinrich C. Koch was born on October 10, 1749 into an old and established
family of Rudolstadt, a small town now in Thuringia, East Germany.’ Be-
cause his father and grandfather had served as instrumentalists in the court
chapel of Rudolstadt, Koch’s future seemed automatically settled. At the age
of fifteen, he entered the service of the chapel as a second violinist. He was
soon promoted to first violinist and became a court musician—Hofmusikus. A
few years later, he was elevated to the rank of chamber musician—Kammer-
musikus.‘ It was at court that he began to receive formal musical education
from the Rudolstadt Kapellmeister Christian Scheinpflug, many of whose
compositions later served as examples in the Versuch.> Koch’s musical
studies, subsidized by his patrons, Prince Johann Friedrich and then Prince
Ludwig Ginther, took him to Berlin and Dresden in 1773, and subsequently
to Weimar, where he had violin lessons for six months from Carl G. Gopfert.®
With the exception of a trip to Hamburg, however, Koch spent the rest of his
life in Rudolstadt, teaching, writing, composing, and fulfilling his duties as a
member of the orchestra.

In 1792 Koch was appointed Kapellmeister, a post which he apparently did
not enjoy. With the death of Prince Friedrich Carl and the resultant reorgani-
zation under a new ruler, he petitioned to return to the orchestra as first violin-
ist, and his request was granted. He wished to spend his time on the theoretical
works by which he is now known. In addition to the Versuch, these include

3. The principal sources of information on Koch’s life are: Hans Heinrich Eggebrecht,
“Heinrich Christoph Koch,” in Die Musik in Geschichte und Gegenwart, 14 vols. (Kassel,
1949-68), 7: cols. 1296-99; Peter Giilke, Musik und Musiker in Rudolstadt (Rudolstadt, 1963),
pp. 31-34; Gotthold Sobe, ‘‘Heinrich Christoph Koch, Lebensbild eines Musikers der Goethe-
zeit,” in Beitrage zur Heimatkunde des Kreises Rudolstadt (Rudolstadt, 1958), pp. 168-73; and
two articles in the Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung: ‘‘Nachrichten,”’ 18 (1816): cols. 302—04 and
‘““Necrolog,” 22 (1820): cols. 133-37.

4. There is some discrepancy in the dates given for Koch’s promotions. According to the
“Nachrichten” in the Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung 18 (1816): col. 302, he became first vio-
linist and court musician in 1768 and chamber musician in 1777. Eggebrecht and Sobe, who both
used archival materials, give June 17, 1772 for Koch’s first promotion and July 1, 1778 for his
second.

5. Christian Gotthelf Scheinpflug (1722-1770) was a minor German composer who became
Kapellmeister at the court of Rudolstadt in 1754. He wrote overtures, partitas, symphonies,
cantatas, and incidental music as the court demanded. None of his works was ever published
and most are to be found in the archives of Rudolstadt.

6. Carl Gottlieb Gépfert (1733-1798) was a virtuoso violinist, famed for the strength and
fullness of his tone. He began his career in Leipzig as an orchestra player in the theater and
occasional soloist in the Gewandhaus concerts; after some travelling, he settled in Weimar as
concertmaster, a post which he obtained around 1770.

INTRODUCTION Is Xi

four books and numerous articles which touch on all aspects of music; two
other studies unfortunately remained unfinished when he suffered a stroke
and died on March 19, 1816. There is little known about Koch’s personal life;
he was an honest and self-effacing man who spoke mainly through his writ-
ings. These earned him election to the Royal Swedish Academy of Music.
That association, however, had not been informed of his death, and the honor
was posthumous, coming in 1818.

Koch’s compositional output was not great and nearly all of it is lost. We
know something about the nature of his works, however, through contempo-
rary reports in the Allgemeine musikalische Zeitung and Gerber’s Neues
historisch-biographisches Lexikon der Tonkiinstler.’ A large proportion of his
works were occasional pieces: five large cantatas for court festivities, four
smaller birthday cantatas, and two mourning cantatas in memory of Princes
Ludwig Gunther and Friedrich Carl. He wrote one drama, Die Stimme der
Freude in Hygeens Haine (1790), and a chorale book for the court chapel. In
addition, he composed numerous instrumental works and much church mu-
sic. None of these is extant in its entirety, but excerpts are quoted in his theo-
retical works. In the archives of Rudolstadt there are seven symphonies in
manuscript, formerly belonging to the court chapel, which bear the name
“Koch” on the title page; however, they cannot be attributed with certainty to
Heinrich Christoph Koch.

Of Koch’s literary works we can be more certain. He wrote reviews and a
few articles for the Speyerschen musikalische Zeitung in 1788-91, for the Je-
naischen allgemeine Literaturzeitung in 1804-11, and for the Allgemeine musi-
kalische Zeitung in 1807-11. Just how many he wrote is difficult to determine,
since they often appeared without a name. Koch himself tried to establish a
periodical of which he was editor, but, after only two issues in 1795, his Jour-
nal der Tonkunst was discontinued. He was not situated centrally enough in
Rudolstadt and, more important, current events had so seized people’s atten-
tion that they wished to read political rather than musical papers.” If the time
and place had been different, the Journal would probably have been a success,
for Koch offered a smorgasbord of topics designed to please the reading public
of amateurs.

A most useful book for amateur and professional alike, Koch’s Musikali-
sches Lexikon was published in Frankfurt in 1802.!° This, and not the Versuch,

was considered his main work, and it was widely read at the time. There was a
need for a work such as the Lexikon, for, as the reviewer in the Allgemeine
musikalische Zeitung observed, Johann Walther’s Musikalisches Lexikon of
1732 was outdated by that time, Jean Jacques Rousseau’s Dictionnaire de mu-
sique (1767) was not comprehensive enough and often presented distorted
ideas, and Johann Sulzer’s Allgemeine Theorie (1771-74) was of more help to
the aesthetician than to the practicing musician. This writer did not even men-
tion James Grassineau’s A Musical Dictionary (1740), which was also out-
dated and perhaps unknown to him. Koch exhaustively treats all aspects of
music, presenting different viewpoints and referring to an amazing wealth of
sources. In 1807 he made an abridged version of the Lexikon, the Kurzge-
fasstes Handworterbuch der Musik, which included some different terms.!! He
omits many of the technical discussions contained in the earlier work and ad-
dresses himself to practicing musicians and those with only a general knowl-
edge of the art. Extracts from the Lexikon were translated into Danish by H.
C. F. Lassen in his Musikalsk haand-lexicon ... Udtog af H. C. Kochs
musikalske Encyclopaedie (Copenhagen, 1826); later in the century, the Lex-
ikon was extensively revised and enlarged by Arrey von Dommer, who enti-
tled his work H. Ch. Koch’s Musikalisches lexicon (Heidelberg, 1865).

Koch revised the first volume of the Versuch when it went out of print, and
the new edition appeared as Handbuch bey dem Studium der Harmonie (Leip-
zig, 1811).!? In this work, he readjusted his system to conform with recent in-
vestigations of the physical basis of sound, and advanced a method of chord
classification that, he hoped, would enable the beginner to deal with the grow-
ing number of dissonant combinations of tones. .

Koch’s last completed work was his Versuch, aus der harten und weichen
Tonart . . . auszuweichen (Rudolstadt, 1812).'4 It is a study of enharmonic

11. Kurzgefasstes Handworterbuch der Musik fiir praktische Tonkinstler und fiir Dilettanten

13. Although in the Versuch Koch had explained consonance and dissonance using feeling
as the criterion, by the time he wrote the Handbuch he had learned, through the writings of the
acoustician Ernst Chladni (1756-1827), that the coincidence of vibrations was the determining
factor. He now had a scientific basis for his classification of consonance and dissonance. In addi-
tion, he now recognized difference tones and used them in his explanation of supposition; he
thus no longer needed to justify the lower added note as the ‘‘natural” root of the upper notes,
which were its overtones. Another change Koch made was to explain chords by combining the
essentially incompatible theories of Friedrich W. Marpurg and Johann P. Kirnberger in a mixed
system. This approach had a precedent in the conflicting theories advanced by Daniel Gottlob
Turk in his Kurze Anweisung zum GeneralbaBspielen of 1791, with which Koch might have
been familiar. For a brief discussion of Koch’s revisions in the Handbuch, see Baker, ‘‘From
Teil to Tonsttick,” pp. 24-28.

14. Versuch, aus der harten und weichen Tonart jeder Stufe der diatonisch-chromatischen

INTRODUCTION AS xv

modulation, taught through examples. At the time of his death, he was in the
middle of two works, one a comparison of harmonic systems, occasioned by
the appearance of the theories of Jér6me Joseph de Momigny in 1806, the
other a study of the physical and mathematical aspects of music.!> We can only
guess what Koch thought on these matters, for the manuscripts have not been
found.

The Versuch einer Anleitung zur Composition touches on nearly every
topic treated in the writings listed above. It is a comprehensive study of music
which begins with the origins of tones and works up to a description of com-
plex musical forms. I have translated only the part of the treatise which treats
the mechanical rules of melody, for that is the more original and historically
important portion. Because the Versuch offers a cumulative course of instruc-
tion, with each section building on what preceded, I shall take the opportunity
to give a brief précis of the ideas contained therein. In the appendix is a table
of contents for the entire treatise, omitted in the original, which gives an idea
of the scope of the Versuch and the methodical way in which Koch approached
his subject matter.

The relationships among the tones of the scale are inherent in the process
by which they arise, namely string division and the overtones. Koch takes the
entire length of a string and two-thirds and three-quarters of it to obtain the
fundamental and what he calls the two principal tones (Haupttdnen); these
will be the first, fourth, and fifth degrees of the scale. Each of these three
pitches produces overtones of a major third and perfect fifth (actually their
replicates at higher octaves); this is a natural, vertical phenomenon which will
later be important in the accompaniment of a melody. All of these tones are
derived from a common fundamental; together they form the major mode of a
given key and, when arranged within an octave, the major scale. Key or mode
is the primary matter of music, the Urstoff der Musik, and it plays a significant
role in Koch’s theory.

Koch aims to define the term harmony in a way that obviates the current
disputes as to the priority of harmony or melody and sheds some light upon
the nature of music itself. In volume 1 and in his refinements of the definition
in volume 2, he designates key or mode as simple harmony (einfache Har-

Tonleiter vermittelst des enharmonischen Tonwechsels in die Dur- und Molltonart der ubrigen

rebuttal by Koch and a rejoinder by the reviewer, 15 (1813): cols. 1-9. (In this translation,
Koch’s Versuch will henceforth refer only to the Versuch einer Anleitung zur Composition.)

XVi 1 ( INTRODUCTION

monie). If the tones occur simultaneously, they are being used harmonically;
if, on the other hand, they are heard in succession, they are being manipulated
melodically. Therefore both harmony and melody share their origin in the pri-
mary matter of music, the specific pitches of a key defined through string divi-
sion and the overtones. The corollary to this is that harmony and melody are
of equal importance in Koch’s view. Thus while the first part of his treatise
discusses harmonic matters, the latter portion deals with the mechanical rules
of melody and how sections may be joined together. At all times, however,
one must be conscious of the basic simple harmony underlying a musical struc-
ture. This must inform one’s treatment of both harmony and melody, for it is
the essence of music.

After his explanation of the origins of tones, Koch proceeds to a discussion
of harmony in the usual sense, the intervals and chords of music and their pro-
gressions. For this, he is greatly indebted to Friedrich W. Marpurg (1718-
1795), specifically to his Herrn Georg Andreas Sorgens Anleitung zum
GeneralbaB und zur Composition, mit Anmerkungen von Friedrich Wilhelm
Marpurg (1760) and his ‘‘Untersuchung der sorgischen Lehre von der Entste-
hung der dissonirenden Satze”’ (1761).!© Although Koch’s basic orientation is
the same, and definitely anti-Kirnberger, he has not taken over Marpurg’s
ideas without some changes. He does not subscribe to the theory of the under-
tones, he derives the seventh in an original manner, and he generally sim-
plifies Marpurg’s theories, omitting complicated explanations which would be
confusing to a beginner.

One of Koch’s aims was to fill a certain gap in instruction and alleviate the
uncertainty of the beginner when first trying to compose: he may know the
individual chords, but their connection into a unified whole is a mystery to
him. Koch breaks down composition into five methods or species of counter-
point which are graded in harmonic difficulty. These species demonstrate how
the notes of a cantus firmus contain in themselves the basis of a varied har-
mony and are predicated upon the way in which tones and keys arise.!’ They

INTRODUCTION 4 es XVil

are, of course, important preparation for the creation of a good melody which
is capable of varied harmony.

Although Koch systematically presents rules which he feels are the essence
of music theory, there are certain matters connected with the “inner nature”’
of music for which there are no rules. This is what he considers to be the higher
study of the art, which he discusses at the beginning of volume 2. Only genius
can explain how a beautiful melody is created; only taste, the ultimate
eighteenth-century arbiter, can be the final judge of what is beautiful. Yet
there are guidelines applicable to the fine arts, and Koch outlines them before
continuing with the “external nature” of music. In this he is heavily indebted
to Johann G. Sulzer (1720-1779), whose Allgemeine Theorie der schénen
Kiinste (1771-74) is the source of all of his aesthetic pronouncements.!® This
work drew on the talents of Johann Kirnberger (1721-1783) and his pupil Jo-
hann A. P. Schulz (1747-1800) for the musical articles, but Sulzer alone was
responsible for the aesthetic sections.!? In these articles he stresses the neces-
sity of unity (Einheit) and variety (Mannigfaltigkeit) and the importance of ex-
pressing one particular feeling. In music, of course, such expression is best
accomplished in conjunction with a text, whereas instrumental music, because
of its more undefined character, is rated rather low. Though Koch marveled at
how many instrumental works actually could arouse a specific feeling, he took
over Sulzer’s ideas without questioning them and, in effect, served as one of
the last exponents of the eighteenth-century theory of art as imitation. Of
more interest is Koch’s attempt to apply to musical composition Sulzer’s three
stages of creation: the invention of the plan (An/age) or sketch of the entire
work; its realization (Ausfiihrung); and its elaboration (Ausarbeitung). It is a
basic impossibility to find musical equivalents for certain of these terms as Sul-
zer defines them. Yet the mere fact that Koch made the attempt shows that he
was not concerned with aesthetic systems per se but sought to apply them to
musical reality. He was most interested in the art of camposition and the

monic accompaniments which are graduated in their difficulty, but in this context he ignores the
horizontal element completely. See his Die Kunst des reinen Satzes in der Musik aus sicheren
Grundsdtzen hergeleitet und mit deutlichen Beyspielen erldutert, 2 vols. (Berlin and K6nigsberg,
1771-79; facs. ed., 1 vol., Hildesheim, 1968), 2, part 1, pp. 3-40; translated as The Art of Strict
Musical Composition, trans. and ed. David Beach and Jurgen Thym (New Haven, 1981), pp.
283-313.

XVili \\, INTRODUCTION

means, whether theoretical or aesthetic, by which a student might better un-
derstand and thus practice this art.

Koch’s teachings on the ‘“‘external nature”’ of melody, when it is objectively
considered, form the most significant portion of the treatise. Under the head-
ing ““The mechanical rules of melody,” he discusses in turn modulation, me-
ter, melodic sections, and the connection of these sections into a whole. Mod-
ulation, of course, is a change in key or in the simple harmony that is the basic
material of music. It may occur harmonically or melodically if certain tones
belonging to a new key are used, but significantly it is in this portion on melody
that modulation is discussed. The horizontal continuity provided by melodic
modulation lays the foundation for Koch’s later treatment of forms. It is un-
derstood, however, that harmony plays an equal role; this is a cooperative
process, and the composer’s aim should be ‘‘to conceive of melody harmoni-
cally.’’20

Koch describes the smallest melodic units, which are the true building
blocks of music, and joins them together; through this process he abstracts
rules of composition. This aspect of music theory was virtually untouched un-
til Koch’s work, attention previously having been focused on the bass and its
harmonies. The only other theorist to discuss phrase technique in this manner
was Joseph Riepel (1709-1782), who in his Anfangsgriinde zur musikalischen
Setzkunst (1752-68) dealt with the smallest elements of music and their combi-
nation.*! This was a pioneering work which Koch both knew and respected,
crediting it with shedding the first rays of light over this theoretical darkness.”
It is, however, the first attempt of its kind and suffers from uncertainty in ter-
minology and general disorganization. By contrast, Koch’s presentation is ex-
tremely intelligible and clearly organized and builds further upon the basis
outlined by the earlier theorist.

The two means by which a section of music can be distinguished are its end-

INTRODUCTION a y XIX

ing and its length. The ending, or means by which a resting point is articulated,
is called its melodic punctuation (melodische Interpunction) because of its re-
semblance to the punctuation of speech. Here again melody and harmony co-
operate, for the melodic punctuation is created in large part by the accompa-
nying harmony; together they convey varying degrees of rest. The length of a
phrase, calculated without taking into account repetition or other extensions,
is significant for its rhythmical character (rhythmische Beschaffenheit).
Phrases in succession create a rhythm, namely periodicity, which is most
pleasing if it is regular; Koch shows a decided preference for a succession of
four-measure phrases (Vierer). Not all units end on the fourth measure, how-
ever, and Koch describes various types of the basic (enger) phrase and ex-
tended (erweiterter) and compound (zusammen geschobener) phrases. Both
the length and the punctuation of phrases must be taken into account when
fitting a melody toa meter. The rhythm of the phrase must not be contradicted
by the more arbitrary accents of meter; rather, one should reinforce the other.

In large compositions it would be both impractical and confusing to con-
sider each phrase and its relationship to other phrases; accordingly Koch
treats another level of structure, the principal period (Hauptperiode), which
contains many phrases. Here the rules of punctuation and rhythm so crucial to
melody must still be observed but are taken for granted. In his consideration
of the principal periods, he now focuses on the harmonic area defined by each
period and the relationships among the various periods. Through the modula-
tions and repetitions contained in these periods, the form of a composition
arises, the product of harmonic-melodic thinking on a large scale.

Koch’s treatment of compositional form is unique in its detailed discussion
of the musical mechanics involved. Earlier writers such as Scheibe and Riepel
had given the harmonic outlines of a composition, but in an extremely brief
manner.” Still others, such as Kirnberger and Schulz, had gone into lengthy
descriptions which were rich in adjectives but poor in information concerning
the various forms.”4 Koch quotes extensively from Kirnberger/Schulz to pro-
vide some information on the “aesthetic nature”’ of types of pieces, which will
characterize them and indicate what sort of expression best suits them. He
always continues the descriptions, however, with a detailed outline of the
genre in question; this encompasses not only the harmonic structure but also,
to a lesser extent, melodic aspects. Although in volume 3 Koch describes cer-
tain vocal forms, his primary emphasis is on instrumental music; Sulzer’s aes-
thetic judgments as to the relative values of vocal and instrumental music are
pushed into the background as Koch deals with the instrumental genres of the
time—the symphony, the sonata, and the concerto. The symphony receives

x YO INTRODUCTION

his closest attention, for the sonata and the solo sections of the concerto differ
only in the nature of the melody rather than in the form of the principal
periods.

Koch describes several options for the various main periods of a symphony
and liberally sprinkles his treatise with examples. He has abstracted rules from
contemporary composers’ works and presents them as possible treatments of
the form, not as models to be followed. Familiar as he is with current practice,
Koch realizes that the options are innumerable and that flexibility is the only
firm rule. He simply wishes to show what is usual, das Gewohnliche; the stu-
dent can find out later about the variations in form for himself. Throughout his
treatise, Koch frequently bemoans the lack of theory; on certain matters, he
cannot state definitive rules, as there are quite permissible exceptions to these
rules. Their justification, as he says, is ‘“‘easier felt than explained.’’® In order
to compensate for the incompleteness of theory, Koch recommends the study
of the works of good composers. He gives such a rich assortment of examples
in order to insure that the beginner will become familiar with a cross-section of
contemporary practice.

Koch quotes from operas by Carl Heinrich Graun, Ignaz Holzbauer, and
Anton Schweitzer; from Singspiele of Johann Adam Hiller and Georg Benda;
from symphonies by Francesco Antonio Rosetti and Franz Joseph Haydn;
from clavier sonatas of Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach; and he pays homage to his
former teacher Scheinpflug by using his dances and vocal works as illustra-
tions.?° Since Koch does so often name the composer of his examples, it is
fairly safe to assume that where there is no attribution, they are from his own
works. The examples that can be dated were written from 1742 to 1782, the
majority in the 60s and early 70s. There appears to have been approximately a
twenty-five year time-lag between practice and theory, between the composi-
tion of the examples and the writing of the Versuch. Because of this, a few
comments that Koch makes may seem reactionary for 1793, the year in which
he completed his treatise.” Yet twenty-five years is a relatively short period
for current practice to have influenced theory, especially considering his loca-
tion in the small town of Rudolstadt. Koch was always quick to appreciate the
new music of the time. In volume 3 of the Versuch he praised the ‘‘Haydn”’
Quartets of Mozart, published but eight years before. Although the concertos

INTRODUCTION SL XXl

of C. P. E. Bach served as Koch’s ideal in 1793, he took Mozart as his model
by the time he was writing the Lexikon in 1802. More significantly, he modi-
fied his description of concerto form slightly to conform with Mozart’s compo-
sitions.?° Koch truly derived his theory from contemporary practice, and when
practice changed, he felt obliged to alter his theory; only then could his writ-
ings fulfill their pedagogical aim.

Koch was the first to present a complete system which explained the coop-
erative nature of the relationship between harmony and melody and described
it at various levels of composition. In a very thorough and logical manner, he
instructed the student in the process by which tones arise and from there built
up to the larger compositional forms, which he described in more detail than
any other theorist up to that time. Neither harmony, nor counterpoint, nor
melody was emphasized at the expense of another. Rather each was examined
as a facet of what Koch saw as a larger, unified discipline, namely the art of
composition.

The Versuch einer Anleitung zur Composition is unique in the theoretical
literature of the time; yet it was not widely disseminated nor, consequently,
appreciated. Francois Joseph Fétis (1784-1871) was one of the first to recog-
nize its worth in his Biographie universelle des musiciens; he commended par-
ticularly the portion here translated:

This book is one of the best which has been published in Germany on the sub-
ject with which it is concerned, and Koch has treated it following original
views. . . . Under the title ‘Mechanical rules of melody,” the second section
of this second volume contains perceptions which are totally new and of great
interest concerning this important branch of the art. Up to this day nothing
better has been done, and before Koch nothing had been produced that was as
satisfying. The entire third volume is devoted to the development of the theory
of melodic form. The period and its diverse combinations are treated therein
with the hand of a master. Nevertheless, the merit of this excellent book has
not been recognized in Germany. The obscurity of the author, the absence of
all means of publicity at the time that the work appeared, and the savoir-faire
of a few theorists, quite inferior to the author of the Introductory Essay on
Composition but more active, have caused this book, which was conceived in a
truly philosophical way, to remain in oblivion. Even today German musicians
and professional critics seem to be unaware of the value of this work, and
nearly all biographers limit themselves to mentioning only the title.

xxii) INTRODUCTION

The Versuch was at first better received in France than in Germany. Alexan-
dre Etienne Choron (1771-1834) was sufficiently impressed by it that he trans-
lated much of the second and third volumes into French in book 2 (1838) of his
Nouveau manuel complet. After an introductory chapter which is not de-
rived from Koch, Choron begins his translation with volume 2, part 2 of the
Versuch, ‘“‘The mechanical rules of melody” (2:135). He includes the discus-
sions of modulation, meter, melodic sections, their connection into short com-
positions, the various means of extension, and the larger structures of music in
the same orcer as had Koch. Although the translation is quite literal, Koch’s
text has been drastically abbreviated, particularly from the chapter on meter
(2:270) until the end of the translation, at the conclusion of the discussion of
the chorus (3:293). Not all of the material was written by Koch; parts of the
sections on vocal compositions as well as a second Chapitre supplémentaire are
Choron’s, which were substituted in the appropriate places in the translation.
The French version does preserve the essence of the Versuch, however, and
despite its brevity and alterations was a means by which Koch’s ideas became
better known.

Fétis’s description of the Versuch as having fallen into utter oblivion was
not quite accurate. During the nineteenth century various theorists made use
of Koch’s ideas and adapted them to their own theories, while not always ac-
knowledging their source. Anton Reicha (1770-1836), Gottfried Weber
(1779-1839), Adolph B. Marx (1795-1866), Mathis Lussy (1828-1910), and
Hugo Riemann (1849-1919) were all influenced, either directly or indirectly,
by Koch’s ideas and incorporated them into their own theories.*!

In the twentieth century, Leonard Ratner was the first to recognize the
significance of the Versuch and discussed it in his dissertation and various ar-
ticles.52 Until now, however, there has been no translation into English. It is
hoped that the present work will make this treatise more accessible to music
historians and that Koch will at last be appreciated for both his originality and
his influence on later theorists’ works.

Toutefois le mérite de cet excellent livre a été méconnu en Allemagne. L’existence obscure de
auteur, l’absence de tout moyen de publicité 4 l’époque ot l’ouvrage parut, et le savoir-faire
de quelque théoriciens, bien inférieurs en mérite a |’auteur de |’ Essai d’une introduction a la
composition, mais plus actifs, ont fait en quelque sorte rester dans l’oubli ce livre congu d’une
maniére vraiment philosophique. Aujourd’hui méme, les musiciens allemands et les critiques
de profession senblent ignorer la valeur de ce livre, et les biographes se bornent presque tous a
en indiquer le titre.”

INTRODUCTION 3 Xxill

In this translation I have tried to make Koch readable without being un-
faithful to his meaning or flavor. In general I have attempted to prune his often
wordy German and translate it into comprehensible and flowing English. Fre-
quently I have broken up very long sentences into shorter units and simplified
their grammatical structure. At times I have omitted words which did not add
to the meaning or were redundant, and often I have changed verbs from active
to passive, or vice versa, whichever seemed better in English. I have always
tried to avoid introducing ideas or concepts which are not proper to the time in
which the treatise was written.

A more challenging problem for the translator than the awkwardness and
density of the prose was that Koch’s terminology was not sufficiently precise.
He often used one word to designate several different divisions; for instance,
the word Theil may refer to one of the main sections of a movement, a phrase,
or a segment of a.phrase. One must translate by context. A related and more
serious problem is that he used the same word—for example, Grundabsatz—
to designate both a particular melodic section and the ending which articulates
it. (At one point, he equated them by saying ‘‘the period contains four main
punctuation sections, or four main resting points.’’)*? Again, I have trans-
lated by the context and added “‘ending”’ after the word where appropriate.
Partly in order to retain this ambiguity of meaning, I have translated the word
Einschnitt by the French grammatical term incise, the equivalent of the Latin
incisio, which means clause. The English equivalent of this term, incision, is
too limiting for the way that Koch used this word; it suggests only the cutting
or the point of division, rather than the entire segment preceding it. Since
Koch frequently used Einschnitt in the latter sense, I felt that a word which
had fewer connotations for the English reader would be better in this case.

Grundabsatz and Quintabsatz have been translated as I-phrase and V-
phrase, with or without “‘ending”’ appended. I have chosen to designate these
phrases by the degrees of the scale upon which their endings were based; since
Koch did not use the terms tonic phrase and dominant phrase in the Versuch, I
felt it would not be proper to introduce them. Related to this is the translation
of Haupttonart as main key, rather than tonic. Koch never used the word
Tonica in the Versuch, and in his Lexikon defined it differently from the way
we understand it.*4 The use of the cognate would therefore be misleading.

I have merged Koch’s footnotes with my own and indicated the author by
an initial; Koch’s notes are followed by [K.], my own by [B.]. They are consec-
utively numbered in each larger section of the Versuch; Koch’s method of

33. See below, p. 215.

34. Koch defines Tonica as the main note of a key to which there has been a modulation; it
can, therefore, change in the course of a composition. The tonic note as we understand it he
designates by the term Hauptton or Grundton; this remains constant throughout a given work.
The tonic key he cails Haupttonart or Grundtonart. See Koch, Musikalisches Lexikon,
“Tonica,”’ cols. 1554—SS.

xxiv ) INTRODUCTION

asterisks would have been somewhat unwieldly, especially with the addition
of my notes.

The examples were the component of this treatise which needed the most
editorial work. I have numbered them consecutively throughout the transla-
tion and numbered every fifth measure. I have retained Koch’s numbering of
measures Only in those few examples in which he illustrated either a com-
pound phrase or compound meter, where a special count was required. His
figures appear below the examples, mine always appear above. Where possi-
ble I have eliminated special signs to which Koch referred and used measure
numbers instead. I have added composers’ names above the examples when
Koch referred to them only in the text, and I have modernized their spelling.
The examples were filled with obvious inaccuracies and inconsistencies which
it would profit no one to enumerate. In the placement of slurs I was guided by
consistency within the example, the musical sense, and other examples which
gave identical music and were sometimes clearer because of better spacing.
There were innumerable typographical errors in the key signatures, dura-
tional value of the notes, presence or absence of barlines, and even, occasion-
ally, pitches (which were indicated by other examples giving the same music).
I have added time signatures to those examples for which Koch did not pro-
vide them; in the few cases in which I have disagreed with him about the time
signature, either because of his indications for phrases and/or because the
work was written in a different meter, I have noted the alteration.

Perhaps the most crucial changes I have made were in the placement of the
squares and triangles with which Koch indicated phrase and incise endings.
This was extremely inconsistent in the published version; the sign was placed
sometimes at the caesura note, sometimes at the last note of the section, or,
more often than not, straddling two notes, neither of which might be appropri-
ate. I have chosen to place it at the caesura note of the phrase or incise, deter-
mined in accordance with Koch’s theories explained on pp. 19-40. Al-
though this means that the sign is not necessarily at the last note of the section,
if the caesura note has been decorated, the caesura note is the harmonic rest-
ing point, and as such is rhythmically, melodically, and harmonically more im-
portant than any notes surrounding it.

Since Koch wrote his Versuch with such care and thoroughness, it is un-
likely that the errors and inconsistencies are due to oversights on his part.
Most likely the publisher and his rather rudimentary method of printing music
were responsible for the flaws in this otherwise excellent treatise. I hope to
have eliminated most if not all of them.

assisted in the preparation of this work. My friend and series editor Professor

SECTION 3

The Nature of Melodic Sections

§77.

Certain more or less noticeable resting points! are generally necessary in
speech and thus also in the products of those fine arts which attain their goal
through speech, namely poetry and rhetoric, if the subject that they present is
to be comprehensible. Such resting points are just as necessary in melody if it
is to affect our feelings. This 1s a fact which has never yet been called into ques-
tion and therefore requires no further proof.

By means of these more or less noticeable resting points, the products of
these fine arts can be broken up into larger and smaller sections. Speech, for
example, breaks down into various sentences [ Perioden] through the most no-
ticeable of these resting points; through the less noticeable the sentence, in
turn, breaks down into separate clauses [Sdtze] and parts of speech [Rede-
theile]. Just as in speech, the melody of a composition can be broken up into
periods by means of analogous resting points, and these, ‘again, into single
phrases [Sdtze] and melodic segments [ Theile].

In this section, we shall necessarily first become acquainted with the mate-
rial nature of the sections which form the periods of melody. Then we can
profitably study their formal nature or the periodic structure: the way in which
the smaller melodic units are connected into a principal section of the whole.
§78.

If we consider the various sections in musical works which compose their peri-
ods, then two main characteristics are found through which they distinguish
themselves as divisions of the whole. The first is the type of their endings, or
that which characterizes the resting points in the material aspect of the art.
The second is the length of these sections, together with a certain proportion

not treated in the present translation; this part of the treatise is devoted solely to the technical or
mechanical side of the art. Koch does refer the reader to taste and feeling, however, when he
cannot supply definitive rules which would cover all possibilities. See note 7, for instance. [B.]

2 THE NATURE OF MELODIC SECTIONS

or relation between them which can be found in the number of their measures
once they are reduced to their essential components.

The endings of these sections are certain formulas, which let us clearly rec-

ognize the more or less noticeable resting points. We have as yet no generally

accepted technical term which would appropriately express both the variety of
these formulas in general, as well as the particular order in which the different
kinds and types can follow one another, taking into account the structure of
melodic periods.’ For lack of a completely suitable term and on account of its
similarity to the labelling of the larger and smaller resting points in speech, we
shall call this melodic punctuation.?

The length of these melodic sections, on the other hand, and the proportion
or relationship which they have amongst themselves with regard to the num-
ber of measures will be called rhythm.

879,

Now the divisions of melody formed through resting points are of a nature that
one alone may or may not be understood as a section of a period, that is, these
divisions may contain either complete or incomplete thoughts. If such a me-
lodic section contains only an incomplete idea, another one must be added if it
is to be understood or felt as a complete section; this will be called an incise
[Einschnitt].4 However, if the thought is considered complete as a section,
then something more may still be required [to express] the idea or feeling of
the entire period. In other words, the complete thought is of a kind that the
period either cannot or can be closed with it. In the first case it will be called a
Phrase [Absatz], and in the second case, because of its characteristic cadence

2. The term “harmonic progression” [Tonordnung] can express neither the different kinds
and types of ending formulas of melodic sections nor the manner of their connection into peri-
ods. For, because of its etymology, it can do no more than either show the propriety in the
sequence of tones, which in the first section I called ‘‘melodic progression,”’ or indicate an as-
pect of modulation, namely, the order in which the different subsidiary keys of a main key of a
composition can be made to be heard. [K.]

3. Even this term is not completely suitable, for the resting points are something essential
not only in speech but also in melody. In the first, the distinctive signs in their visible presenta-
tion are nothing more than an aid to discover more quickly the more or less noticeable resting
points (which even without punctuation marks would still be there). In melody there is no need
of this aid, because its resting points affect our feeling enough that there is no need to use special
signs to indicate them. Nevertheless, these subjects have a great deal of similarity; for example,
a period ends the sentences of speech in the same way as the cadence closes the periods of mel-
ody; the phrase and incise differentiate the melodic sections of the period just as do the semico-
lon and comma the smaller divisions of the sentences in speech. It is this similarity in particular
which induced me to use the term ‘‘punctuation’”’ in connection with the differentiation of me-
lodic sections. [K.]

4. I have translated Einschnitt as ‘‘incise’’ rather than ‘‘incision,’’ the literal translation.
Since Einschnitt is used both for the point of division, the incision, and for the section preceding
it, I felt that a term which was more neutral and not so weighted towards one of the meanings
would be more appropriate. See introduction, p. xxiii [B.]

BASIC PHRASES AND THE INCISES CONTAINED IN THEM 3

formula, we shall call it a closing phrase [SchluBsatz], in order to distinguish it
from the [internal] phrase.

From this division there result three different types of melodic sections,
namely incises, phrases, and closing phrases. According to the introduction in
the preceding section [78], what is important for all of these divisions is the
formula through which they become noticeable as resting points, or, to use
our chosen term, their punctuation mark and also the length or number of
their measures.

Remark

Among the various phrases of a melody, the first usually contains the main
idea, which, as it were, defines the feeling the whole should arouse. This will
be called the theme or the main phrase. The remaining phrases, however,
which present the different expressions of this feeling, may be called dissection
Phrases | Zergliederungssdtze|, because they are broken up ina different way in
the realization.® Because both types are not essentially different with regard to
either their length or punctuation formula, however, it is not necessary to use
this special distinction in this section.

§80.

Completeness in melodic phrases manifests itself in different ways. (1) A
phrase may contain only as much as is absolutely necessary for it to be under-
stood and felt as an independent section of the whole; such a phrase I shall call
a basic phrase [enger Satz]. Or, (2) it may also contain a clarification, a more
complete definition of the feeling, and in this case the phrase is an extended
phrase [erweiterter Satz]. Or, (3) two or more phrases, complete in them-
selves, are combined so that externally they appear in the form of a single
phrase; such a phrase we wish to call a compound phrase [zusammengescho-
bener Satz].

CHAPTER 1: BASIC PHRASES AND
THE INCISES CONTAINED IN THEM

§81.

Generally speaking, only feeling can determine both the places where resting
points occur in the melody and also the nature of these resting points, if they
are more or less noticeable, that is, if they indicate whether the sections of the

4 THE NATURE OF MELODIC SECTIONS

whole thus ended may be considered complete or not. Although in the course
of this chapter the lengths and punctuation formulas of these sections must be
treated, neither of these offers a general, characteristic trait that can define
not only the place where a resting point is present in the melody, but also the
completeness or incompleteness of the sections thereby arising.’

If one could distinguish subject and predicate in melody as definitely as in
speech, it would not be necessary to let feeling determine the completeness or
incompleteness of phrases. Then an incomplete phrase or an incise would be a
melodic segment which lacked either a subject or a predicate. The complete-
ness of a basic phrase would be shown through the joining of a predicate witha
subject. An extended phrase would be either one in which more than one
predicate had been joined with a subject, or one in which the subject, the
predicate, or both had been defined more exactly through subordinate ideas.

I shall pursue this comparison of melodic phrases with the phrases of
speech for a moment. If, for example, the following phrase in example 1 were
to be considered from this logical viewpoint, then it would be a complete basic
phrase, because its main idea or the subject contained in the first two measures
would receive a certain direction, a certain definition through the two follow-
ing measures or through a predicate.

Example 1
Allegretto

sidiary thematic material as Zergliederungssdatze seems to draw a distinction which is not there.
Perhaps uncertain as to its definition, Koch uses the term very infrequently; he seems to prefer
melodischer Haupttheil, which is more straightforward in meaning. [B.]

7. No doubt to many of my readers it may be remarkable that I lead the beginner before the
tribunal of feeling for the discrimination of the sections of melody. But through what aid should
he otherwise get to know not only the resting points which are not followed by a rest, but also
the completeness or incompleteness of the sections which thereby arise? The number of mea-
sures generally cannot determine where in the melody resting points must be which divide the
whole into sections in order to let it be felt distinctly. The ending formulas of these sections are
so various and can be formed in such manifold ways that it would be very questionable to de-
cide, by means of these figures, where resting points are present in the melody; not to mention
that such figures in the melody also can be used where there is no resting point. In short, nothing
concrete can determine the places where they are in melody. If, then, feeling necessarily must
assist in ascertaining resting points, why should it not also decide whether those discovered end
complete or incomplete sections of the whole? The latter is as easily decided by feeling as the
former.

To be sure, every melodic section which takes three or four measures of a simple meter is
usually called a phrase, without distinction; the thought may or may not be complete. But the
insufficient definition of the nature of phrases makes the teaching of musical period structure
more difficult with regard to the punctuation sequence of sections and the rhythm. In this way
one creates an obstacle, for the maxims according to which the sections are joined into a whole
cannot be formed generally enough. Whether and how much the classification into basic and

4

The dissimilarity among these basic, complete phrases referred to in sec-
tion 79 shows itself when we compare them with one another. Some are of
such a nature that we necessarily expect still one or more sections to follow in
the whole to which they are joined because (usually) they cannot close the
whole themselves. Both phrases in examples 8 and 9 are of this type and are
called internal phrases [Absdtze] because they are unable to conclude the
whole. The phrases in examples 10 and 11, on the other hand, can close the
whole after other preceding sections, and because of their characteristic end-
ing formula, we call them closing phrases [Schlusatze}.

Furthermore, the essential difference between an internal phrase and a
closing phrase depends on nothing more than the essential difference of end-
ing formulas. Hence internal phrases can be transformed into closing phrases
through alteration of their ending formula, and closing phrases can be turned
into internal phrases. Thus, for example, the internal phrases found above in
examples 8 and 9 are changed into closing phrases in examples 12 and 13, and,
conversely, the closing phrases in examples 10 and 11 are changed into inter-
nal phrases in examples 14 and 15.

These resting points in the complete phrases, or these still incomplete seg-
ments of a phrase, are called’incises. They may not be revealed through any-
thing external, as in the quoted examples, or they may contain an outward sign
of a short rest, as in examples 19 through 21.

Example 19

When resting points are found in complete phrases—be they internal or
closing—which break them down into smaller sections, those incomplete seg-
ments which do not end a complete idea are called incises. Thus the incise
itself is an incomplete segment of a phrase which becomes noticeable through
a resting point.

Remark

Henceforth, to distinguish incises from phrases in the examples I shall al-
ways use the sign A to indicate the incises and the sign LJ to indicate the
phrases. We do not need to note closing phrases with a special symbol, how-
ever, because the cadential formula distinguishes them enough.

§85.

9. According to Koch, simple meters are those in which the measure has only one down-
beat [Niederschlag] or strong part [guter Tacttheil]; the remainder of the measure is the upbeat
[Aufschlag] or weak part [schlechter Tacttheil]. Examples of these meters are 2/4, 3/4, and 3/8.
Compound meters are derived from simple meters by omission of a barline, so that two mea-
sures appear as one; this occurs in 4/4, 6/4, and 6/8. Koch believed that in these compound me-
ters, the “original” measures retain their accentual relationships; each compound measure thus
has two équal downbeats. As the second of these will receive less emphasis than the first, Koch’s
equation of simple and compound meter is somewhat simplistic. See ‘‘The nature of meter in
general and the various kinds and types of it” 2:270-341. [B.]

BASIC PHRASES AND THE INCISES CONTAINED IN THEM 11

1. THE LENGTH OF BASIC PHRASES
AND THE INCISES CONTAINED IN THEM

§86.

Complete phrases need less or more length depending on whether they are
basic or somewhat extended phrases. Because in this chapter we shall first
consider the nature of basic phrases so that we can later understand more
clearly how and through what means they can be extended, we now deal only
with the length of basic phrases.

§87.

Most common, and also, on the whole, most useful and most pleasing for our
feelings are those basic phrases which are completed in the fourth measure of
simple meters. For that reason they are called four-measure phrases | Vierer].
They may actually appear as four measures in simple meters or in compound
meters in the form of only two measures.!° All of the preceding examples in

the chapter are of this nature save those of examples 4 through 7 at the end of
the 81st section.

Remark 1

If the length of phrases or the number of measures they contain is to be
ascertained, and the phrase does not start with a complete measure, the begin-
ning is made with notes which, due to the nature of the melody, belong either
to the strong or to the weak part of the measure. If the notes which begin the
phrase still belong to the strong part of the measure, the measure is taken into

account in determining the length of the phrase. Thus the following phrase in
example 24 is a four-measure unit.

Example 24
Allegretto

The accompanying voices may sound instead of the rest, as in example 25, or
they may enter after the beginning of the main part, as in example 26.

Example 25

10. See above, note 9. [B.]

Example 26

If a phrase begins with notes which have to be used in the upbeat of the mea-
sure due to the nature of the melody, the first note falls either on the upbeat
itself or after the upbeat. In the latter case, when the notes come after the up-
beat, they are never taken into account in determining length. Thus the fol-
lowing phrases in examples 27 and 28"! are actually four-measure units, al-
though their length appears to be somewhat more than four measures.

Example 28

If, on the other hand, the first note beginning the phrase falls on the upbeat
itself, one must, in order to determine its length, first try to decide whether the
phrase is formed so that the bass, or also the accompanying middle voices,
must begin in the preceding downbeat of the measure. If it does not, the notes
beginning with the entire upbeat of the measure are never considered in deter-
mining the length of a phrase. Thus, for instance, the following phrase in ex-
ample 29 is considered a four-measure phrase:

The phrase may be composed, however, so that the notes beginning on the
upbeat require the bass or the remaining voices on the downbeat in the preced-

part of the measure, must be considered a complete measure, with a rest form-
ing its strong part. In determining the length of the phrase, it must be included
in the calculation, as, for instance, in example 30.

§89.

Not all basic phrases are complete in the fourth measure; often such a phrase
becomes complete only in the fifth or sixth, occasionally not until the seventh
measure. These basic phrases of more than four measures should not be con-
fused with extended phrases of the same number of measures, because the
former require a different treatment from the latter in the joining of phrases in
a period.

If a phrase is complete in the fifth measure of a simple meter then it is called
a five-measure phrase [Fiinfer|. Such a phrase can arise in three ways:

It can arise (1) from a four-measure phrase, by means of the extension of
two metrical units to two measures. When, for example, the metrical units of
the first measure in the phrases of examples 36 through 38 are extended to
complete measures and thereby made more emphatic, this results in the five-
measure phrases in examples 39 through 41."

When a four-measure unit which is to be extended to a five-measure unit
contains a ccmplete incise of two measures, as does, for instance, the phrase in
example 38, then not only the first segment can contain the extension as in
example 41, but also the second, as in example 42.

lets, and symphonies. His most successful work was his opera Alceste (1773), to a libretto by
Christoph M. Wieland; this was the first attempt at opera in German in the second half of the
century. In 1778 he replaced Georg Benda as Kapellmeister at the court of Gotha. There he
wrote the opera Rosamund (1778), libretto again by Wieland, which was not as successful as
their first collaboration. Example 49 is from Alceste, which was published in Leipzig in 1774; it is
the opening of Hercules’ aria ‘‘O du, fiir die ich weicher Ruh und die ich alles wage’’ at the

§92.

It has been mentioned above in section 87 that the length of phrases is deter-
mined by the number of measures of simple meters and that therefore every
measure of a compound meter must be considered as two simple measures,
between which the barline is left out. Occasionally, however, a phrase which is
in a simple meter is inserted in the midst of a compound meter, and, con-
versely, a phrase which presupposes a compound meter is inserted in a simple
meter. The way that the length of these phrases is determined will be shown
when we deal with their use in the period.

2. THE PUNCTUATION SIGNS OF PHRASES AND THEIR
INCISES, OR THE ENDING FORMULAS OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§93.
That place where a resting point is shown in the melody, that is, the place
where one section of the melody can be separated from the following one, is
called a caesura (cutting).

It has been mentioned several times that the caesuras of melodic sections
should properly always fall on the strong part of the measure. The reason for
this has been explained in the preceding section.’

17. If the caesuras do not fall on the strong part of the measure, then the composer has
chosen a meter which is not appropriate to his melody. Koch’s reasoning becomes clear in the
following paragraphs. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 51
============================================================

20 THE NATURE OF MELODIC SECTIONS

At the moment of the invention of a melody certain tones that have inner
value or emphasis distinguish themselves (see section 50). These determine
the separation of measures in the presentation of the melody, provided that
other musicians can understand and perform them just as the composer imag-
ined them. Let us suppose the composer invented the following sequence of
notes in example 59, allowing that the relative duration of tones has already
been determined.

Example 59

In inventing this beginning melodic section, the composer may let the empha-
sis of the division fall either on the tones marked * or on the tones marked with
the sign °. If he conceived this melody so that the emphasis falls on the tones
marked *, then, as soon as he wishes to represent it through notes, he must
write the melody according to this conception. That is, he must bring those
tones which are emphasized in his conception into that part of the measure
which is stressed according to the nature of the meter. Thus, in this case, this
beginning melodic section must appear in the metrical division as in example
60. On the other hand, if the emphasis falls on the tones marked with °, then
he must divide the phrase into measures as in example 61.18

Example 60

From this it is apparent that simultaneously with the genesis of an idea,
those stressed tones which necessitate this or that metrical division are already
determined. Now the caesura of a phrase is a point of division in itself because
it divides larger portions of the whole into smaller sections, and it must have its
proper emphasis as a point of division, according to section 50. If the caesura
note of cadences, phrases, and incises were introduced on the weak part of the
measure, a contradiction would arise between the nature of such a caesura

18. The reason why this phrase can be conceived in two different divisions with a single me-
ter is found in the remark at the end of section 96. [K.]

PAGE 52
============================================================

note, which has an inner emphasis as a point of division, and the nature of the
measure, which is only capable of this emphasis in the downbeat. In most
cases, this offends our feelings in a very perceptible way. To demonstrate this,
we have only to form the sequence of tones in example 60 into a complete
phrase, as in example 62, and to give this complete phrase the metrical divi-
sions of example 61, thus resulting in example 63. The caesura note which fell
on the strong part of the measure in example 62 would then fall on the weak
part of the measure and the phrase would offend our feelings.’

§94.

It has already been remarked (section 82) that the ending formulas of ca-
dences and phrases must of necessity be fundamentally different, because the
former can close a whole, the latter cannot. The punctuation figures of incises,
on the other hand, are not essentially different from those of phrases, because
both can end only sections of the whole. Before considering the figures of ca-
dences more closely, however, we wish to continue with the punctuation for-
mulas of phrases and incises. Basically these consist in interrupting the conti-
nuity of the melody in the strong part of the measure by a tone of an essential
triad basic to that key in which the melody is rendered or to which it is turning,
as in example 67.

example 68, then the caesura of the first phrase is made by means of the third,

and incises can be decorated.

21 . Presto

The rule to which Koch refers in the next sentence is that in the division of a melody into
measures, the units representing beats, the metrical units, must not be presented as subdivi-
sions of the beat, submetrical units; conversely, the submetrical units must not be presented as
metrical units. To do so causes metrical confusion—in the former case, this produces a com-
pound meter instead of a simple meter, and in the latter it results in no meter at all (see 2:300).

composed in a meter, it has been treated contrary to the rule noted previously,
and that the submetrical units have been presented as metrical units. Precisely
this error against the nature of the meter would appear if, for example, the
second phrase in example 79 were placed in a meter as in example 81.

Example 81

a. This phrase can be divided most properly into four measures, that is, it has
only four main points of division according to our conception, and it affects
our feeling as a phrase of an even number of measures. Yet, if one closed it
with its caesura note without a feminine ending, as in example 82, then it
would appear as a seven-measure unit.

Example 82

b. The caesura note and its feminine ending, which together only occupy one
strong and one weak part of a measure as in example 79, are extended to two
measures. This feminine ending, which is after all nothing more than an over-
hang of the caesura carried on into the weak part of the measure, comes to be
on the strong part, albeit only apparently.

From this it is evident that when a melody is composed in a meter, it is nec-
essary to consider the length along with the ending formula of phrases, if mis-
takes which are contrary to the nature of the meter are to be avoided.

Remark 2

In the preceding illustrations in examples 70, 72, and 74, the phrase ends by
means of a weak close on the second quarter of a 3/4 measure without offend-

has been explained as wrong, also closes with the second quarter of a 3/4 mea-

ending and the preceding tone D as the proper caesura note? No, as long as the
tone C in this measure requires its own harmony and does not appear in such a
connection that it can seem a passing note with regard to the triad G B D at the
basis, the tone D cannot pass as a caesura note nor the tone G as a feminine
ending. In order to convince oneself of this, one may try to compose a correct
bass to this phrase, especially in the last measure, and then let feeling judge the
effect of it.

On the other hand, if the tone C is entirely omitted in this measure (exam-
ple 87) or is connected with the tones of the chord G B D as a passing note

§96.

The decoration of a caesura note can occur (2) by means of a suspension
[Vorhalt] or an appoggiatura [Vorschlag] through which the caesura note is
displaced from its proper position.” Instead of it, a tone not belonging to the

Example 89

22. From Koch’s text and examples, it might appear that a Vorschlag is an appoggiatura
notated by a small note and having no durational value of its own; it is accented and its value is
subtracted from the following note. A Wechselnote is an appoggiatura written as a regular note
and having its own durational value. A Vorhalt is a suspension (by implication) or an appoggia-
tura. In the first volume of the treatise, Koch designates a suspension or a delay of a note by the
term Aufhaltung and does not use Vorhalt at all; in the passage at hand, he gives examples only
of suspensions treated freely, which are, of course, appoggiaturas. Although Koch attempts to
distinguish between Vorschlag and Wechselnote, he is inconsistent in his use of these terms and
actually employs Vorhalt, Vorschlag, and Wechselnote interchangeably. All three of these de-
vices have in common that they are nonharmonic tones which displace the caesura note from its
proper position by delaying it. At times, Koch seems to use Vorhalt and Vorschlag to indicate
the displacement process in general, rather than the particular note which causes the displace-
ment. [B.]

Example 96

Example 97
Andante

From this it is apparent that in all these instances, which occur very fre-

point; this must be determined by one’s feeling.

§97.

Finally the caesura of a phrase is decorated also by this means: (3) the space
from a caesura note struck in the strong part of the measure or delayed by an
appoggiatura is filled with notes until the tone with which the following phrase
begins. In this case, the following phrase is connected more closely with the
preceding one. For instance, instead of the mere caesura in the phrase in ex-
ample 103, it is composed as in example 104.

Example 103

This connecting of two phrases by filling in the space between the caesura
note of the preceding phrase and the first tone of the following one, however,
should not be mistaken for the compounding of two phrases. This is done by
the so-called suppression of a measure [Tacterstickung], with which we shall
become acquainted in the third chapter.

In addition, it should be noted that a phrase which ends with a fermata

placed on an underlying dissonant chord is really not yet complete. Rather,
for its completion a section following the fermata is required, because the feel-
ing of dissonance demands a subsequent phrase in which its resolution occurs.
§100.
The phrasés and incises are made on an underlying essential triad of that key
in which the melody is composed or into which it is led. If the tone which
makes the caesura of a phrase calls for the triad on the keynote, as for instance
in examples 96 and 99, then the phrase itself is called a /-phrase [Grundab-
satz]. If, however, the caesura tone of a phrase requires the triad on the fifth
[degree] of the key, as in example 103, then it is called a V-phrase [Quintab-
satz].*4 If a piece which is followed by another is closed with a V-phrase, either
with or without the help of a fermata, then many composers tend to call this V-
phrase a half cadence.

Phrase-endings are seldom formed on triads on the fourth [degree], for
such triads seem to be unable to give a quality of completeness to the thoughts
which end with them. Yet phrases can appear also on this triad which our feel-
ing judges to be fairly complete, as, for instance, in example 109 in m. 4.

Example 109

phrase.”’ Those are terms which Koch does not-use in the Versuch and they therefore would
have had inappropriate connotations. His definition of Tonica in his Musikalisches Lexikon

§102.

The nature of the cadence, as the ending formula of the closing phrase, has
already been treated in section 179 of the first volume.”’ Next it must be noted
that with regard to its melodic components, three special notes properly be-
long to it. The first, which constitutes the note of preparation of the cadence,
falls on the strong part of the measure. The second note of the cadence for-
mula is the cadential note, which occurs on the weak part of the measure. It is
called the cadential note as it will imminently ‘‘fall’’ onto the keynote. After
this, the closing tone or the caesura note of the cadence follows, again in the
strong part of the measure, as, for instance, in example 115.

27. In volume 1, section 179, Koch discusses the general rules of counterpoint and thus gives

CHAPTER 2: EXTENDED PHRASES

§105.

A phrase is extended when it contains more than is absolutely necessary for its
completeness. The extension of a phrase through which the feeling it contains
is defined more precisely can be brought about by various means. The first of
these means is the repetition of a segment of a phrase, and this can occur either
in the same key which underlies the passage to be repeated or in another
key.#8

If the repetition of a segment of a phrase occurs in the same key, then either
the repetition has the same underlying harmony, or it occurs on degrees of the
scale which require a different harmony.

The repetition is made with a segment of the phrase which either does or
does not contain the ending formula of the complete phrase. In the first in-
stance, when the punctuation figure of the phrase must also be repeated, the
repetition (especially when it is varied) has the appearance of an appendix.
This instance will be separately treated later, after consideration of the repeti-
tion of those segments in which the punctuation formula of the complete
phrase is not repeated.

§106.

When a basic phrase is extended through the repetition of only a single mea-
sure, then this repetition always occurs in the same key. The measure which is
reiterated can be the first or one of the middle measures of the phrase, for
instance examples 126 and 127.

Example 126

Example 127
. Allegro

eS ee et TS

Composers usually tend to have this single repeated measure performed
more softly or more loudly and, moreover, the repetition itself can be varied
in different ways without damage, as in examples 128 and 129.

28. In pericds often entire complete phrases are repeated, sometimes in the same key,
sometimes also in another key. This does not belong in this section, which is concerned only
with the nature of melodic sections, but in the following one, where the connection of these
divisions is treated. [K.]

In this case, the repetition of a measure is not only on the same harmonic
basis, but also on the same degrees of the scale. Such a five-measure phrase
arising through the repetition of a measure retains the value of a four-measure
phrase under all circumstances relating to the connection of several melodic sec-
tions. It is considered as a four-measure unit with regard to the rhythmic rela-
tions of phrases.

A single measure of the phrase may be repeated so that the repetition does
occur on the same underlying harmony, but the upper voice is inverted with
the middle voice, whether actually present or only presumed, as in example
130. The perceptibility of the repetition is then often lost through this inver-
sion of voices. Such a four-measure phrase, changed into a five-measure one
in which the repetition of a measure is not noticeable enough with the inver-
sion of the upper voices, is usually treated as a five-measure unit in the con-
struction of periods.

In the first of these transpositions (example 142), the melody is led out of

tone higher in [the key of] the fifth. In the second (example 143), the incise
formed in the key of the sixth is repeated a tone lower in [the key of] the fifth.
Because both these types of transposition have been used to excess, almost ad
nauseam, in older compositions, they are rather avoided in modern composi-
tions unless they appear in a new form. If one wishes to use them, one changes
sometimes the beginning of the phrase to be repeated, sometimes its ending
formula, as in examples 144 and 145.

Example 144

not repeated in the key of the next degree as in example 146, there is no need
for this alteration. They are sanctioned by current taste, which in this and in
every similar case must decide what is or is not practicable.

§110.

The second means through which a phrase can be extended and the substance
of it more closely defined is the addition of an explanation, an appendix, which
further clarifies the phrase. This appendix can be a section of the phrase itself,
whose repetition makes the meaning of the phrase more emphatic, as in exam-
ples 147 and 148, or it may be an incomplete segment which is not yet present
in the phrase but which is able to define its substance more closely, as in exam-
ples 149 and 150.

Through such an appendix the extended phrase usually acquires two
phrase-endings on one and the same root. If these are not to be offensive to
the ear, as in example 151, they must be different, either in the tone which
constitutes the caesura note or at least in the decoration of one and the same
caesura note.

§111.

This multiplication of ending formulas resulting from the addition of an ap-

same underlying triad on which the phrase to which it is added itself ends (as in

of the appendix is not taken into consideration with respect to interphrase

§113.

In the appendix of a closing phrase, the cadence may or may not be repeated.
If the appendix contains a repetition of the cadence, then either: (1) only that
section of the phrase which forms the cadence is repeated, with or without
alteration, as in examples 157 and 158; or (2) asegment of the phrase itself also
is repeated, as in example 159; or (3) an incomplete idea which ends again with
a cadence is connected with the closing phrase as an appendix, as for instance
in example 160.
