Example 161

If the appendix of a closing phrase contains no cadence, then it always has a
V-phrase-ending, as in example 162. In this case such a V-phrase-ending is
called a half cadence and is usually marked with a fermata. A section which
closes with this half cadence really always presupposes another piece immedi-
ately following upon it, whose key again is C.

Example 162

If, on the other hand, another piece in a related key comes after one which
ends with a half cadence, the appendix with the half cadence tends to lead to
the key of the succeeding section. When, for example, the key of F major fol-
lows, then the appendix can be formed as in example 163. If the key of G ma-
jor follows, then it is given the form of example 164. And, in case A minor is
the main key of the next piece, it will have approximately the form of example
165.

§114.

When several cadences follow one another in a closing phrase by means of
either an appendix or the repetition of the cadence, one often places a differ-
ent tone than the caesura note in one of the last cadences and thus deceives the
ear in its expectation of the closing tone. If that occurs it is called a deceptive
cadence and can be produced not only by the upper voice but also by the bass.
The deceptive cadence arises in the upper part when, instead of the closing
tone [caesura note] of the cadence, a different interval is struck, as in exam-
ples 166, 167, and 168; or when the closing tone itself is placed an octave
higher or lower, thereby beginning an appendix to the closing phrase, as for
instance in example 169.

Example 166

On the other hand, the deceptive cadence is produced by the bass when (1)
another degree takes the place of the keynote as the closing tone of the ca-

Example 170

the minor mode in addition allows for one deceptive cadence peculiar to it
alone. This happens when the second degree of the scale, if it is a cadential
note, is lowered a semitone, as in example 172 (m. 3-4).

§118.

In practice, two or perhaps even three of these means of extension of basic
phrases are at times combined to extend one phrase. It would be far too long-

The beginning composer who has examined these separate means with atten-

by their various combinations.

CHAPTER 3: COMPOUND PHRASES

§120.

Two or more phrases, of which each is complete in itself, may be connected
with one another so that they either appear in the form of a single phrase or
ought to be considered as only a single phrase within the period. I call such a
melodic section which consists of two complete ideas a compound phrase.
§121.

The compounding of phrases can be brought about through various means.
The first and most common of these is the so-called stifling or suppression of a


============================================================
PAGE 86
============================================================

COMPOUND PHRASES 55

measure [Tacterstickung or Tactunterdriickung]. Two complete phrases in
which the caesura note of the first and the initial tone of the second phrase are
one and the same degree of the scale are connected in such a way that the
measure which contains the caesura of the first phrase is omitted, and the ini-
tial tone of the following phrase is at the same time considered as the omitted
caesura note of the preceding phrase.

If, for example, in the two complete phrases in example 181, the fourth
measure of the first phrase is omitted, as in example 182, then the initial tone
of the second phrase must also take the place of the omitted caesura note of
the first phrase. The measure which now contains both the caesura of the first
phrase and the beginning of the following phrase must be doubly taken into
account with respect to the rhythmic relations of phrases: namely, once as the
measure with which the first phrase ends, and secondly as the first measure
with which the second phrase begins.

Example 181

Allegretto —_

This process appears just as often in closing phrases as in internal phrases,
and, indeed, happens either with two closing phrases, as in example 183, or
with a closing phrase and an internal phrase, as in example 184.

Example 183
Allegro



============================================================
PAGE 87
============================================================

56 THE NATURE OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 184

By this means two successive complete phrases which have their caesura

note on the same triad (but can follow one another in the period only under
certain circumstances) may be used as a single phrase without any offense.
§122.
The compounding of two phrases complete in themselves can also be pro-
duced by removing that quality of the ending formula of the first phrase which
characterizes it as a complete phrase. Through this process the subsequent
phrase is required for the completeness of the first, and both phrases then ap-
pear in the form of a single integral phrase. This process, which I am about to
explain more clearly, is possible only with such phrases of which the first con-
sists of two segments that contain a repetition on a different harmonic basis.

In the following section, where the punctuation of incises in succession is
discussed, we shall learn that two complete incises, of which the second con-
tains a repetition of the first on a different harmonic basis, can form both a
complete and an incomplete phrase.*? The phrase always remains incomplete
in the connection of two such incises when the ending formula of the second
incise is the same as the ending formula of the first. Therefore we perceive the
phrase in example 185 as incomplete at its second incise, contained in the
fourth measure.

Example 185

On the other hand, when the punctuation formula of this second incise 1s
formed differently, and, for example, its overhang is lowered to the octave of
the keynote, as in example 186, then we recognize instinctively a complete
phrase in the connection of the segments, though both are incomplete in
themselves.

187 is made incomplete, and the second phrase, even though complete in it-
self, is yet necessary to finish the first. By this means, two complete phrases are
given the form and the value of a single, integral phrase, that is, they are com-
pounded.

I show the beginner these last two ways of compounding phrases, however,

the contents of the preceding chapter with attention.

SECTION 4

The Connection of Melodic Sections,
or the Structure of Periods

§1.

If melodic sectians are to be connected, it must be determined beforehand
whether they are even fit for joining, that is, whether they are so constituted
that nothing conflicts in their being linked together. For not all melodic sec-
tions can be properly joined into a whole.

The features by means of which they are capable of being connected with
each other may be considered from two different standpoints: first, those re-
garding the feeling to be expressed; second, those of an external or material
nature.

§2.

Observations on the suitability of connecting sections with regard to the feel-
ing to be expressed belong to the higher study of the art, since they are matters
of taste and artistic sensitivity.

Remark

Although my aim is directed solely to the mechanical part of composition,
nevertheless I have endeavoured from time to time to make the beginner at-
tentive to the importance of these higher matters of art and to recommend the
necessity of their study. I have done so especially in the first part of the second
volume.! My purpose was to encourage the beginner to use every opportunity
to form his taste and his artistic sensitivity as much as possible before he tries
to exercise his creative powers. If he has seized these opportunities he will be
in a position to invent a melody even at the first attempt and to turn his atten-
tion not only to the mechanical rules, but also to the higher requirements of
the art.

In this treatise I shall subsequently endeavour to intersperse observations
of this kind, wherever it can be done without long-windedness. The beginning
composer cannot be given enough opportunity to perceive for himself that far
more is required for composers than the knowledge and observance of the me-
chanical rules of the art.

1. Koch is referring to the section entitled ‘‘The aim and the inner nature of a composition,
and particularly the way in which it arises,” 2:15-134. [B.]
63


============================================================
PAGE 95
============================================================

64 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§3.

If, however, the suitability of connecting melodic sections is examined with
respect to their external or material nature, then the following is taken into
consideration:

1. the unity of the underlying key of these sections;

2. their equality of meter and similarity of tempo;

3. acertain likeness in the movement of beats or further subdivisions of the
measure, or a Certain similarity of melodic figures;

4. the punctuation formula of these sections, or the way in which they end;
and

5. the relationship of their length compared with one another, or their
rhythmical nature.

84.

It would be unnecessarily lengthy to give rules for the first two of the subjects
mentioned, namely, the unity of the key and the identity of the meter of
phrases suitable for joining. Through practice in vocal or instrumental music,
the beginner should already be trained well enough that he cannot violate
rules of this kind.

Thus we could immediately turn to the real subject of this section, namely,
the connection of melodic sections with regard to punctuation and rhythm,
if these phrases did not require the similarity in the motion of beats or further
subdivisions of the measure just mentioned, which is designated by the term
meter. For that reason, the first chapter of this section must treat of meter or
metrical stress | Tactgewicht].

§5.

The remaining external characteristics of melodic sections which must be ob-
served in their connection are already known from the contents of the preced-
ing sections. Subsequently, therefore, we shall only deal with the different
ways of connecting them. Thus the question is: How can melodic sections be
connected in order to form a whole, or how should the sections of a period be
constituted as to their length and their punctuation formulas? The following
chapters of this section are devoted to answering this question.

§6.

In this regard, a melodic section can be connected with another immediately
preceding if:

1. its punctuation formula compared with that of the preceding phrase has
not only the necessary conformity of underlying key but also the necessary
variety for our feeling; and

2. if its length is so constituted that it has a good rhythmical relation with the
length of the preceding one. Already‘one sees from this that the entire teach-
ing of the connection of melodic sections is relative. Thus the question raised


============================================================
PAGE 96
============================================================

THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS 65

before—how individual melodic sections are to be connected into a whole—
can be properly answered and discussed only in connection with specific cases.

87.
It is recognized as a general rule that with every skill to be acquired, one must
proceed gradually from the easier to the more difficult, from the simpler to the
more complicated. Likewise with the skill of joining melodic sections into a
whole, the beginning composer must follow this rule. For supposing that at the
first attempt to invent a melody he chose an elaborate composition, then a
thousand hindrances would stand in his way, partly real, partly only imagi-
nary. He avoids all these hindrances together with the detrimental conse-
quences of such a reversed procedure if he first learns to compose small
pieces.* If he is then given instruction in how such a small composition can be
given a greater length through various means, it will not seem difficult to him
to proceed by degrees to the largest and most complex kinds of compositions.

To give the beginner opportunity for this gradual progress, I am dividing
the various ways of connecting melodic sections into different chapters. Thus
the second chapter is to treat the diverse ways of connecting melodic sections
in short compositions.

In the third, I shall attempt to show how these small compositions can be
enlarged by different means.

The subject of the following chapters, however, will be the ways of con-
necting several melodic sections, or the arrangement of larger compositions.

In this way I hope to be able to make comprehensible to the beginner the
various methods of connecting melodic sections. Moreover, this gives me the
best opportunity to show without long-windedness the most essential matters
concerning the arrangement of each particular composition, which I promised
to do in the introduction to the first volume.

Remark

I have further divided the chapters which concern this subject into special
exercises, partly in order not to weary the beginner through the quick succes-
sion of the several ways of connecting [phrases], but especially in order to give
him more opportunity to exercise his power of invention through imitating all
particular methods of connection. Nothing will be troublesome about these exer-
cises if he is sufficiently practiced in counterpoint and has studied the contents
of the second part of the second volume with the necessary attention.

But is not genius too greatly limited or indeed even suppressed through
such imitations in which a certain form is determined? The reason for this ob-
jection is well known. From time immemorial there have been people who
wished to shake off all imagined constraints of art, who chose to concede

2. Here and afterwards in this section, by “‘composition”’ [Tonsttick] I understand not the
joining of several individual movements, for example an Allegro, Andante, and Presto into a
symphony or sonata, but I mean each of these individual movements separately. I will not use
the customary expression “‘composition”’ [Satz] because I have applied it already too precisely
to the sections of a whole. [K.]


============================================================
PAGE 97
============================================================

66 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

everything to genius, but nothing at all to craft. If young composers who wish to
use these pages for instruction should be taken in by this prejudice, I can say
nothing further here than to reassure them that their genius neither will nor
can as yet show itself to its total extent, and that no other way can probably be
devised to lead their genius in the right direction. If one has once used the
proper ways of connecting melodic sections through imitation of the correct
forms, then these fetters fall off by themselves.

By the way, the objection which one generally raises against the rules of art
with regard to genius has already been answered several times. One can look
up D. [sic] Forkel’s Allgemeine Geschichte der Musik concerning this subject,
specifically the introduction from section 123 to section 129.°

He who still cannot set his mind at rest on this account can also adopt the
following method. He may acquaint himself with the various exercises merely
as examples of the different ways of connecting melodic sections, and only at
the close of every chapter practice free imitations with no prescribed definite
form. In this way too one reaches the goal, only one most beware of overhasty
steps.

CHAPTER 1: METER OR METRICAL STRESS

§8.
It is well known that the technical term meter is borrowed from poetry, where
it defines the flow and the length of the line by help of feet.
These feet are of either two or three syllables.‘
Disyllabic are

1. the trochee, which consists of a long and a short syllable (— ~); for exam-
ple, the words Kiinste, schéne are articulated in this way:

2. the iamb, whose first syllable is short, but the second long (~-—); for ex-
ample Figur, entztickt;

3. the spondee, a succession of two long syllables (— —); for example
‘“‘Nennest du, wiirdigest du etwas Seligkeit dann”’;

4. the pyrrhic, a succession of two short syllables (v ~); for example ‘Jn
umwo6lkender Nacht rufet des Strahls Gefahrt.”’

Trisyllabic feet, on the other hand, are

1. the dactyl, a series of one long and two short syllables (- ~~); for exam-
ple ‘‘Empfangt mich heilige Schatten, thr. . . .”’;

3. Johann Nikolaus Forkel, Allgemeine Geschichte der Musik, 2 vols. (Leipzig, 1788-
1801). Only volume 1 had been published at the time that Koch was finishing his Versuch. [B.]

4. Although familiarity with meter is not absolutely necessary for the knowledge of stress
within measures, both subjects are so similar to each other that one clarifies the other. More-
over this knowledge of meter is most necessary for beginners who intend to work at vocal com-
position also. [K.]


============================================================
PAGE 98
============================================================

METER OR METRICAL STRESS 67

2. the anapaest, a reverse dactyl, or a succession of two short and one long
syllable (~ ~—); for example ‘‘Das Gestad hallet, es donnert das Meer. . . .”;

3. the tribrach, a series of three short syllables (UV vv); for example “Jn der
Entziickung”’;

4. the molossus, a succession of three long syllables (— — —); for example
‘Herr! Herr! Gott! Gerechter’’;

5. the amphibrach, which has a short, long, and short syllable (~— ~); for
example ‘‘Sie sah die junge berbende Streiterin’’;

6. the bacchius, a series of one short and two long syllables (v — —); for
example In Wettlauf;

7. the antibacchius, a series of two long and one short syllables (— — ~); for
example “‘Zweykdmpfe! Verflucht ist der Brudermord”’;

8. the cretic or amphimacer, a succession of long, short, long syllables (— ~
—); for example. Edle That!

§9.

The extraordinary similarity of these feet to the different movements of parts
of the measure is easily perceived, for already in the simplest divisions of the
measure these feet are very noticeable. Thus, for example, the division of the
measure in examples 193 and 194 is trochaic, in examples 195 and 196, on the
other hand, iambic. In examples 197 and 198 the phrase begins with a spon-
dee, in example 199, however, with a pyrrhic.

§10.

Music makes use of still more such feet or such specific movements of the divi-
sions and subdivisions of the measure; these, however, are neither arranged in
certain classes nor designated by special names.

Now, when a composition begins with such a meter, that is, with such a
fixed movement of the divisions of the measure, our feeling accepts this type
of movement very easily and, as it were, settles itself in it. It is, however, im-
mediately offended when this movement is interrupted in its flow by another
dissimilar one, because it must then accommodate itself to another movement
too quickly and too unexpectedly.

So far poetry and music completely correspond with regard to meter. As
soon, however, as those divisions or subdivisions of the measure which define
the meter are decorated in the melody with different types of melodic figures,
some of this external similarity is lost. In this case, the meter in music pos-
sesses something exclusively its own—something which is not known in po-
etry. Nevertheless, in the use of melodic figures, our feeling also demands a
similar continuation of that movement to which, as it were, it has been


============================================================
PAGE 101
============================================================

70 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

inclined. In most cases, it is offended when this movement previously used is
interrupted or exchanged with another dissimilar one.

§11.

All our modern compositions consist of more than one melody, or, more cor-
rectly, of more than one part. Therefore the correct movement of meter does
not depend on the main part alone, but on all the voices together. When the
melody of the main part does not preserve the accepted meter perceptibly
enough or deviates from its movement too much, then either the bass or one
of the subsidiary parts present can and must maintain the similarity of this
movement.

§12.

The metrical stress in the principal melody can be treated so that the accompa-
nying voices cannot rectify the mistake. I have already shown this in section
232 of volume 1 in connection with mixed counterpoint; there the maxims are
found by means of which such mistakes can be avoided.® That section thus
properly belongs here and must be reread.

§13.

Far more often, when our feeling is offended through disregard of the metrical
stress, the mistake must be attributed not only to the main melody, but even
more to the lack of movement suitable for the accompanying voices.

Thus every time the figures of a melody are constituted so that the metrical
motion is not noticeable enough to be felt, the accompanying voices must al-
ways come to tts aid, and by their motion preserve the identity of metrical
stress.

1. This must be done when the melody has a rest in that subdivision of the
measure where it should sound as a result of the accepted metrical motion; for
example at the sign + in the phrases of examples 209 and 210.

Example 209
Allegro

6. In section 232 of volume 1, Koch discusses the accompaniment of a cantus firmus by a
bass in mixed counterpoint and stresses the necessity of choosing melodic figures which pre-
serve the accepted meter. If in the accompaniment one inserts values which are dissimilar to
those upon which the meter is based—for instance, triplet eighth notes in a passage containing
mainly eighth notes—a contradiction arises amongst these figures and the accepted metrical
stress is interrupted. Such variations in melodic figures are only acceptable if the meter is based
on a note value greater than that which is being varied. [B.]

Remark 1

Because a measure is divided into a certain number of sections which deter-

can be of no greater value than the so-called metrical units. In these examples
the sounding of a beat would be omitted through the rest at the sign +, if the
bass did not compensate for this lack; without this sounding of the bass, the
metrical stress would necessarily have been interrupted.

Remark 2
The meter, however, is not always defined through beats, but also often

through further subdivisions of the measure;’ this happens mostly with
phrases of slow tempo, as in example 211.

Example 211

7. Submetrical units have already been explained on p. 297 of the second volume. [K.] A
metrical unit [ Tacttheil] is a unit which represents one beat. As Koch explains (2:297), a subme-
trical unit [ Tactglied] is a subdivision of this beat and has the value of either one half or one third
of the metrical unit. Thus in 2/4 meter, a quarter note is a metrical unit and an eighth note a
submetrical unit; in 6/8 meter, a dotted quarter serves as the metrical unit and an eighth note as
the submetrical unit. Whether the submetrical unit is a duple or triple division of the beat, the
next smaller value [Tactnote] is always a duple division of the submetrical unit. [B.]

§15.

All those tones which are necessary in the accompanying voices to maintain
the metrical stress can be multiplied without giving offense. That is, the tone
necessary to preserve the metrical stress can be struck several times through
division of its value, or this tone can be mixed with harmonic subsidiary notes
or also with passing notes. Thus, for example, the phrases in examples 215 and
217 in section 13 may also be accompanied in the way of examples 221 and 222,
without the slightest violation of the metrical stress. This is, however, already
known to us from the exercises in counterpoint.

§16.

the usual way of handling metrical stress. This occurs most frequently between
the immediate succession of two complete phrases. Thus, for example, in the

ing phrase properly requires the striking of the second quarter note of the bass

compensates for this omission. Thus it is not offended by the omission of
sounding a beat, as in example 225.

It would be superfluous to seek out more examples in which the composer
has purposely interrupted the meter. There are so many cases that they cannot
be classed in definite, precise types. The justification for such exceptions, es-
pecially in connection with purely instrumental pieces, is for the most part eas-
ier felt than explained. Thus, in this matter too, the beginning composer must
study good scores in order to compensate for the lack of theory.

CHAPTER 2: THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS
INTO PERIODS OF SHORT LENGTH, OR THE ARRANGEMENT
OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS

§17.

In this chapter we begin to know and to practice the connection of melodic
sections with regard to their rhythm and punctuation. To attain this objective
the shortest compositions common in music are chosen first, because the dif-
ferent possible ways of connecting their few melodic sections can be most eas-
ily perceived and imitated. These short compositions are:

1. the current dance melodies,

2. the melodies to odes and songs, and

3. all short pieces arbitrarily arranged with respect to the meter, the
rhythm, the length, the punctuation, and the tempo.

§18.
The dances most used are:

A. The gavotte, a dance of gay and pleasant character. It is, to be sure, not
as common in social dances as in theatrical ones. Its characteristics are that (1)
it has a duple meter which usually is 2/2 in a settled and not too rapid tempo;
(2) every melodic section begins on the upbeat of the measure with two quar-
ter notes; (3) the rhythmic sections are of an even number of measures, which,
in addition, tend towards a perceptible incise in the second measure; (4) it
consists of two sections, each containing eight measures.

If the gavotte is taken up as a separate entity in compositions, then its


============================================================
PAGE 110
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 79

length is not limited to a certain number of measures, because in this case it is
not intended for dancing. At times, the number of eight measures is retained
in the first section, but the second section is limited to no definite number of
measures at all. Consequently it is also unnecessary that all melodic sections of
this second division be of an even number of measures. Because the gavotte is
less well known than are social dances, I shall insert one as an example (exam-
ple 227).°

B. The bourrée, to which everything applies that has been said of the ga-
votte, except that its meter is formed differently: it begins with only one quar-
ter note on the upbeat of the measure, for instance see example 228.!°

C. The polonaise, or the national dance of Poland. Its proper character is
solemn gravity. This dance melody, which (1) moves in a somewhat slow 3/4
meter, has (2) the thoroughly distinctive feature that all caesuras of its ca-
dences and phrases end on the weak part of the measure of its 3/4 meter. It
requires (3) not only rhythmical sections of an even number of measures, but
in addition is inclined to have a very perceptible incise in the second measure
of every one of these melodic sections. It consists (4) of two sections or re-
prises, which are bound to no definite number of measures owing to the arbi-
trary figure of the dance. Usually the first section contains eight, the second
section twelve to sixteen measures. It is more customary than necessary to
close the first section with a cadence in the main key.

D. The anglaise or the contredanse, which is bound neither to a particular
form nor to one specific meter. ‘‘All these dances,” says Sulzer, “‘are similar in
that they are very lively and, for the most part, have a somewhat comic nature
through which they combine entertainment and prettiness. The music is gen-
erally very sprightly with a great simplicity.””!!

E. The minuet, which, above all other dance melodies, is taken up most
often in our modern compositions, moves (1) in a brisk 3/4 meter, which can
begin not only on the upbeat, but also with the downbeat. If it is arranged for
dancing, then (2) its melodic sections must have a rhythmical relation of an
even number of measures; and (3) it must consist of two sections or reprises,
each containing no more than eight measures. If, however, it is not designed
for a dance, then not only can its reprises be of quite arbitrary length, but also
its melodic sections can be of an uneven number of measures.

section 4, note 8). The manuscript is in the archives of Rudolstadt where it is designated as S.
161. [B.]

(see section 4, note 8). The manuscript is in the archives of Rudolstadt, where it is designated as
S. 149. [B.]}

82 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 228 (Continued)

Usually two of these dances occur in succession, of which the second is
given the name trio, although in most cases it does not conform to this term at
all. This so-called trio, after whose performance the minuet is repeated, is usu-
ally composed in a key which is closely related to the key of the minuet. At
times, however, it also appears in the same key as the minuet. In this case, it is
especially necessary that the punctuation form of the trio be arranged differ-
ently from that of the minuet.

F. The march, which properly pertains to martial music, has the aim of fa-
cilitating a complete equality of steps. This category has also been taken up in
larger works of the art, particularly in the opera, where it is used for proces-
sions; in this case, the march always maintains the character of the sublime
and splendid.

It requires a grave tempo in duple time and a very clear and uninterrupted
meter. Reprises are as unnecessary in it as is a definite number of measures.
Even the rhythmic ratio of sections need not necessarily be even-numbered.

The usual overhang of the caesuras of phrases and cadences in the march,
which consists of repeatedly striking the caesura note, is not really essential in


============================================================
PAGE 114
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 83

this category. However, it appears to have been introduced merely not to
leave without metrical support that step which must be made between the end
of one and the beginning of the following melodic section. This overhang,
which moreover has no good effect when used several times, can be entirely
omitted in the marches placed in compositions; but then the metrical motion
must be actively maintained by the bass or by the middle parts.

§19.

The second kind of short compositions comprises the melodies to odes and
songs. This type contains two main categories, namely,

1. The chorale, the simplest of all types of compositions. It is slow and de-
voutly solemn by nature and one of its characteristics is the avoidance of all
melodic figures. Because it is to be sung by all members of a congregation, it
should have neither a large vocal range nor difficult intonations. Because of its
specific use, it is not subject to such precise metrical performance as other
compositions.

2. The figured melodies. The character of these short pieces depends upon
the contents of the poetry, their form, however, chiefly upon the structure of
the strophes. For not only the chorale, but also the figured melody are usually
composed to odes and songs.

Remark

The chorale and the figured melody of an ode or a song are, generally
speaking, subject to the same punctuation and rhythmic rules as other short
compositions. Neither, however, will be discussed in this chapter, because
their treatment presupposes a certain knowledge which belongs to a special
branch of composition, namely vocal composition. Only when the beginning
composer has already made some progress in instrumental composition is he
advised to occupy himself with the two side-branches of composition, namely
with fugue and vocal composition.

§20.

Among the third kind of small compositions I class all occasionally used short
pieces of no defined character, whose meter, tempo, and arrangement of
rhythm and punctuation depend entirely upon the imagination of the com-
poser. To this category belong, generally speaking, all short pieces, in particu-
lar the so-called theme for variations. This requires a somewhat simple but
flowing melody, capable of many variations and permitting sufficient, un-
forced changes of harmony. In the variations themselves, the main melody
should always be recognizable, though an exception is often made in this re-
spect, especially when they are intended to show skill on the instrument for
which they are composed.

These small pieces are really the most appropriate for the first exercises of
the beginning composer, because in case he is considering a certain rhythmic
or punctuation form, he is yet not bound to any definite meter or specific
tempo of this or that meter.


============================================================
PAGE 115
============================================================

84 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§21.

Small instrumental pieces of this kind and dance melodies generally use me-
lodic sections which we have called basic phrases in the preceding section. It
has been noted above on page 11 that those phrases are complete in the
fourth measure and for that reason are called four-measure phrases. Quite
certainly these four-measure phrases are not only the most common and most
useful, but also, generally speaking, the most pleasing melodic sections for
our sensibilities.

Therefore, in this chapter none of the kinds of phrases introduced in the
preceding section will be used other than four-measure phrases. These, in
their connection with each other, are already in the most perfect rhythmic re-
lationship, on account of their even number of measures and their equal
length. Thus there is no need to consider their connection in this chapter; it
will only become necessary later in this section when talking about the connec-
tion of phrases of unequal length.

§22.
The shortest length of the previously described compositions is usually sixteen
measures, or four four-measure phrases. Thus our first exercises will deal with
the connection of four complete melodic sections with regard to the nature of
their punctuation, or, as one also says, with regard to the harmonic progres-
sion.

Remark

Although the inner nature of the melodic sections of a composition has al-
ready been treated in volume 2, part 1,!2 nevertheless, the question must occur
to the beginner: How should these four melodic sections composing such a
short piece be constituted in view of their inner contents? It is well known that
every composition, be it short or long, must have unity but also variety. Now
if, in order to give such a short piece sufficient variety, four melodic sections
entirely different from one another were connected, then this variety would be
achieved in such a way that it would destroy a still more necessary characteris-
tic of the composition, namely, its unity and symmetry. Four different melodic
sections joined into a period can indeed contain a complete plan for a larger
composition; never, however, can they make up a complete whole by them-
selves without fragmentation and manipulation of ideas. For what would pre-
serve the unity of such a composition when no section of it would be repeated
in another connection or given a new turn? And if this does not happen, the
sections are connected without any purpose and make up no self-sufficient
whole. For this whole has then no modification of ideas, no coherence, and
one cannot see the purpose of such a display of thoughts.

The unity of such a short piece thus requires a closer correspondence of
sections and at least one of them needs to be repeated in another connection or
in a different turn, etc. .

I would deviate too far from my true purpose if I were to pursue this subject
further. I hope that it will be enough for the attentive beginner when, through

12. See section 4, note 1. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 116
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 85

brief observations on the examples, I later give him the opportunity to reflect
on this matter further and to learn to study models of the masters from this
point of view.

§23.
When four melodic sections are connected into a whole, only the last should
contain the necessary close of the whole, that is, these sections should be
formed so that only one of them contains a cadence; or two of these sections
may close with a cadence.

In the latter case, either the first cadence is also in the main key, or the
melody is led into a related key and cadences therein.

This distinction induces me to explain the various ways of connecting me-
lodic sections in different exercises, so that the beginner will not be over-
whelmed with too many forms all at once.

EXERCISE 1, INWHICH FOUR MELODIC SECTIONS ARE
CONNECTED, OF WHICH TWO CONTAIN A CADENCE IN THE
MAIN KEY

§24.

In this case the two sections which contain the cadence are usually the second
and fourth phrase; that is, they divide the whole into two small periods or sec-
tions, which are either repeated as a reprise or performed without repetition.
Now, with regard to both those melodic sections which close not with ca-
dences, but merely with phrase-endings, four different situations or punctua-
tion forms are possible here. For with our accepted condition that the second
and fourth section make a cadence in the main key, either (1) the first melodic
section can close with a phrase-ending on the triad of the main key, that is as a
I-phrase, the third section with a phrase-ending on the triad of the fifth, that is
as a V-phrase. Example 229 is such a case.}3

Remark 1

In connection with this example, the opportunity immediately arises to
make a remark which had occurred to me in section 22.

This short composition has the most perfect unity. It consists of four me-
lodic sections and contains only a single main idea, which, however, is modi-
fied in various ways. This is the first four-measure phrase, which initially ap-
pears as a I-phrase, but immediately afterwards has been repeated and
changed into a closing phrase. In the second section, the phrase which is a V-
phrase and with its repetition is the closing phrase is essentially the very same
phrase; it has merely been given a different turn. The phrase has been played

in contrary motion and through a passing modulation has been given more va-

From this it is apparent that a single phrase can indeed be sufficient for such
a short composition if the composer knows how to give it a different direction
and connection so that the whole, despite its unity, obtains nevertheless the
necessary variety. :

But one must not believe that in such short compositions of four phrases the
three last phrases always have to arise through modification of the first. No! In
most such compositions two truly different melodic sections are connected;
the remaining two then arise partly through alteration and partly also through
repetition of the preceding sections, as in example 230.

Compositions of this kind are found in which even three different melodic
sections have been connected, without the piece being deprived of all unity.
But in this case the fourth phrase necessarily must be a clear repetition of the
first or second phrase, otherwise the piece would lose its unity. When the sec-
ond section of the preceding minuet is composed in the manner of example
231, it is a short piece of sixteen measures which contains three different
phrases.

In most cases, however, the composition is better when the connected
phrases are more similar to each other, as in examples 229 and 230.

14. Koch believes that the most organically unified compositions are those for which both
the melody and its accompanying harmonies were conceived simultaneously. A composer may
have taste, genius, and a sure knowledge of the mechanical rules of composition, but he is truly
gifted only if he has the ability to conceive melody harmonically. This Koch considers the high-
est attainment of the creative spirit. The concept of key as a source of both harmony and melody
is an integral part of Koch’s theory, and he stresses their close relationship throughout his trea-
tise. [B.] °

Remark

As long as the second period of a short composition consists of no more
than two phrases, those forms in which the first period cadences in the main
key are not capable of much variety in modulation, since at most only a passing
modulation can take place in the first phrase of the second section.!® This is
because with the beginning of the closing phrase the melody must again be in
the main key. Thus pieces of such short length make very little use of these
forms and instead use one of those with which we will become acquainted in
the second and third exercises. They are frequently found only in some dance
melodies, such as the minuet and the contredanse, and in the melodies to odes
and songs. But if the second period consists of more than two melodic sections,
then, as we shall see later, forms are created which are capable of more variety
in modulation and are common to all kinds of short compositions.

§26.

Not to omit a form recurrent in short compositions of sixteen measures, I must
add that the second period, instead of having two complete melodic sections,
sometimes consists of a compound phrase. In this type of composition, the
compound phrase is usually one whose method of compounding has been de-
scribed in section 122 of the second volume. I shall therefore insert here only
one example in which the second period is composed in this manner (example
235).1”

That the second period of this minuet is compounded as previously de-
scribed can be seen from the following dissection of it in example 236; here the
first melodic section, by which the second period has been compounded, has
been given back its proper completeness.

§27.

Because in the punctuation forms described so far the second and fourth
phrases close with a cadence in the main key, though both the remaining sec-
tions are I- or V-phrases, nothing remains to be noted in connection with the
use of these forms in the minor mode. It is also unnecessary to cite examples of
these forms in the minor mode, because they would be different from the pre-
ceding ones not in punctuation, but only in mode.

§28.

In the examples concerning these punctuation forms, the melodic sections
were for the most part formed so that there were no perceptible incises. If,
however, melodic sections which contain incises are occasionally used, they
are either complete or incomplete incises. 8

§29.

Until now it has been shown how the punctuation can be formed with a com-
position of four phrases when the first cadence is in the main key at the end of
the second melodic section, and thus divides the composition into two small
periods of equal length. But one may ask whether the two remaining possible
cases cannot also occur, namely, that,either the first or the third melodic sec-
tion may contain the first of the two cadences in the main key.


============================================================
PAGE 124
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 93

When among four melodic sections the first is closed with a cadence in the
main key, then the second and third phrases usually do not alternate with a I-
and V-phrase of the underlying key, but modulate to one of the most closely
related keys. This case belongs to the exercise immediately following.

The second case, wherein not only the third but also the fourth phrase
closes with a cadence in the main key, is not usual with short compositions.
Only in larger compositions does the case occur that two different melodic
sections form a cadence immediately after one another in one and the same
key. But when two cadences are found at the close of short compositions, then
either the last phrase is not a complete section but only an appendix or a repe-
tition of a segment of the preceding closing phrase,!? or the entire preceding
closing phrase is repeated, and then there is no difference in the melodic sec-
tions.

Remark

In odes and songs often only three melodic sections—indeed, with short
stanzas at times only two—are connected into a whole.

When three phrases are connected, the first usually closes with the cadence
in the main key, but the second is a V-phrase, so that not all the caesuras of the
sections fall on the main key. In this case, the last phrase must necessarily be a
clear repetition of the first, if the whole is to have the feature described in the
remark in section 22. The ariette of example 240 is such a short composition of
three phrases.

If the melody consists of only two melodic sections, then the first of these
must necessarily be a V-phrase, so that with the repetition of the melody for
the remaining verses of the song, not all resting points fall on one and the same
root and become offensive through the complete lack of harmonic variety.

The melody to the song by Weisse ‘“‘Ich weiB ein Madchen, schoéner ist” (exam-
ple 241) is a whole in which only two melodic sections are connected.”°

Because I am dealing here with compositions which contain less than four
melodic sections, I shall immediately bring up a form which properly belongs
to the following exercise. Occasionally melodies to odes and songs of three
melodic sections are found in which all three phrases close with cadences. If
they do, the first cadence must always fall on the main key, the second has to
be in the most closely related key. The third phrase is then always a repetition
of the first phrase, for reasons already familiar to us. An example of this form

EXERCISE 2, INWHICH FOUR MELODIC SECTIONS
ARE CONNECTED, OF WHICH ONE CONTAINS
A CADENCE IN A SECONDARY KEY

§30.

Here, too, the first cadence, which closes in a subsidiary key, is usually made
by means of the second phrase, and the whole is divided into two periods of
equal size.

When using the major mode, this secondary key in which the second phrase
closes is the major key of the fifth; but when the minor mode is used, it can be
either the minor key of the fifth or the major key of the third.

§31.

In the major mode, when the second phrase closes with a cadence in the fifth,
again four different punctuation forms arise with regard to the first and third
phrases. (1) The first melodic section can be a I-phrase, the third a V-phrase.
This is the most usual form for four melodic sections of which the second
closes with a cadence in the fifth (example 243) .”

(2) Not only the first, but also the third melodic section can be a V-phrase.

When the first phrase of the allegretto of example 243 is altered, the punctua-
tion form shown is obtained (example 244).

21. Johann Adam Hiller (1728-1804) was a German conductor, pedagogue, and composer.
He founded and conducted a Concert Spirituel in Leipzig, which later became the
Gewandhaus-Konzerte. He taught and wrote numerous pedagogical treatises on both singing
and violin playing. As a composer his fame rests primarily upon his Singspiele, a genre which he
developed and which achieved great popularity. This song is taken from his most successful
Singspiel, Die Jagd, composed in 1770 to words by C. F. Weisse (see note 20); it is sung by
Hannchen in Act II, p. 49. Koch transposed it from A down to G. The year after this Singspiel
was produced, it was published as Die Jagd, eine comische Oper in drey Acten (Leipzig, 1771).

Poco allegro

(4) Finally one can also close not only the first but also the third melodic sec-
tion as a I-phrase, for example when the first phrase is modified as in this poco

allegro (example 246).

If the arrival of this closing phrase is not to sound flat, some caution is nec-
essary with the use of both these last forms, because the third melodic section

is a -phrase. The main point here is what kind of melodic section from the first


============================================================
PAGE 129
============================================================

98 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 246

period forms the closing phrase, and with which harmonic basis the melody of
the closirig phrase begins. If it is the main phrase or the so-called theme which
is repeated, then the following maxim must be remembered: instead of a I-
phrase, it is always better that a V-phrase precede the main phrase when it is
repeated in the last period of a composition. The reason is easy to understand.
The melody of the main phrase requires at its beginning the triad of the main
key for its harmonic basis. Now, when the preceding melodic section closes as
a I-phrase, then there is no change of harmony between the end of this phrase
and the beginning of the closing phrase. The entrance of the main phrase is
necessarily rather weakened through the repetition of exactly the same har-
mony, instead of arousing the greatest attention at once as a main phrase and,
in Our present case, as a closing phrase.

If instead of the main phrase another melodic section is chosen for the clos-
ing phrase, then the harmonic basis with which the melody begins has to be
taken into account. If it uses another harmony than that of the main key, then
a I-phrase can always precede without the slightest disadvantage. But if the
closing phrase begins with the harmony of the underlying key, then either the
preceding I-phrase is changed into a V-phrase, or the beginning of the closing
phrase is formed so that it can have a harmony different from that of the main
key.

These maxims will be tested forthwith on our poco allegro. Its third me-
lodic section closes without ill effect as a I-phrase because the following clos-
ing phrase begins with the harmonic basis of the triad on the fourth degree of
the scale. But supposing it was felt that the short composition would gain if the
main phrase itself, formed as a closing phrase, were repeated. Then its en-
trance will always make a better éffect if the preceding I-phrase is changed


============================================================
PAGE 130
============================================================

99

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS

phrase appears again with just the same harmony with which the preceding

into a V-phrase, as in example 247, than if the I-phrase remains and the main
section ended, as in example 248.

Remark

The third melodic section of our short composition can make a passing
modulation in all those keys which are closely related to the main key. Only
this passing modulation must be managed in such a way that, in anticipation of
the repetition of the main phrase, either this section can close as a V-phrase in
the main key, or it must be formed so that it loses its completeness and is
bound more closely with the following fourth melodic section.

In the allegretto which is found in section 31 (example 243) as an example
of the first form, at the beginning of the third phrase there is immediately a
modulation back into the main key, without a passing modulation in another
key. Since, however, the melody is in [the key of] the fifth before the beginning
of this phrase, it can also begin in this key and modulate back to the main key
afterwards, as, for instance, in examples 250 and 251.

Example 250

Example 251

But if a passing modulation is used in this third melodic section, it can lead
(1) to the minor key of the second degree, either as in example 252, where the
phrase becomes complete with the V-phrase-ending because the caesuras of
both segments of this phrase are different in their decorations; or as in exam-
ple 253, where on account of the similarity of their decorations, the phrase is
not complete, but immediately attaches itself to the following closing phrase.
But there can also be a modulation (2) to the minor key of the sixth, as in ex-
ample 254.

If it is desired to take away the completeness of this third phrase and com-
pose the entire second segment a tone lower than the first, as in example 255,
then the melody would be in [the key of] the fifth at the close of the second
segment, and the entrance of the repeated theme directly following in the
main key would have a bad effect. On the other hand, if the incomplete phrase
in example 255 is made complete by means of an appendix which closes with a
V-phrase [in the main key], as in example 256, then nothing is objectionable in
the entire period. In this case, however, the third melodic section has grown
through the appendix to an extended phrase of six measures.

The passing modulation of the third melodic section can (3) also be into the
major key of the fourth, for instance in example 257.

When the first segment of this phrase is transposed a tone higher and if, as
in example 258, it has lost its completeness through the similarity of the caesu-
ras of its segments, nothing else is objectionable in this phrase except that the
taste of our time does not approve of it. Such phrases transposed upwards step
by step are called rosalien.~ They were used so frequently in older composi-
tions that they have become disagreeable to some extent.

23. The term comes from an Italian song, ‘Rosalia, mia cara,”” which begins with such a
literal repetition, one tone higher. The rosalia always entails a (passing) modulation since the
intervals are unchanged in the repeated portion. The German word for rosalia is Schusterfleck,
cobbler’s patch. [B.]

EXERCISE 3, INWHICH FOUR MELODIC SECTIONS ARE
CONNECTED, AMONG WHICH IS FOUND ONLY A SINGLE
CLOSING PHRASE

§34.

This exercise would abound with different punctuation forms if the melodic
sections which close with a phrase-ending could follow one another without
restriction. But the sequence of phrase-endings is very limited considering
their harmonic basis: for neither two I-phrases nor two V-phrases in one and the
same key may be composed immediately after one another with melodic sec-
tions which differ from each other, if the second of these sections is to have no
unpleasant effect on us.

§35.

The rule seldom permits an exception with I-phrases. When two really differ-
ent phrases which are not in the form of a single compound phrase are closed
as I-phrases immediately after one another in the same key, the second phrase
almost always produces an unpleasant effect. In the decoration of its caesura,
it may be the same as or similar to the preceding one, as in example 267, or it
may be different from it, as in example 268.”

Example 267

be discussed in the second subdivision of the fourth chapter. [K.] It is interesting that examples
267, 268, 273, 274, 278, and 281 share the same incipit, transposed, as example 235 which Koch
attributed to Stamitz. [B.]}

the melodic sections are different. Consequently a section closing as a I-

without alteration of the figures, as in example 269. Very often in the repeti-
tion the decoration of the caesura is changed, as in example 270.

ples, or the I-phrase is changed into a V-phrase, as we shall see presently.

The rule also prohibits the immediate sequence of two V-phrases. But be-

Therefore the rule applies only to the sequence of two V-phrases in differ-

There are very many cases in which the immediate sequence of two V-

nature.

Example 276

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 115

But there are also many instances in which two V-phrases following one
another produce no such unpleasant effect, for example, if the second V-
phrase of example 277 is formed in the way shown.

Example 277

Nevertheless these latter cases must be regarded as exceptions to the rule in
view of what has already been said. Only what a pity that I am not in a position
to reduce these exceptions to certain definite types in order to assist the begin-
ner. For the correct use of the V-phrase in all its connections is a most impor-
tant matter in the structure of melodic periods. Indeed, I have made an at-
tempt to determine the cases in which two V-phrases follow each other
without producing an unpleasant effect, but I have failed, for I found in the
end as I compared the effect of several examples which necessarily must be
considered to be of one and the same type that they did not conform to each
other in the expected way. What is certain, however, is that the rule permits
most exceptions in cases when both the successive V-phrases appear not at the
beginning of a period, but in the continuation of it and when the decoration of
the caesura of the second V-phrase is entirely different from the caesura of the
preceding one.

Only these characteristics are too insufficient to determine the appropri-
ateness or inappropriateness of the sequence of two V-phrases. So here, too,
taste must be the judge and decide, should the case arise, whether both the V-
phrases to be composed successively are or are not proper.

Remark

In the second subdivision of the fourth chapter, the succession of two V-
phrases in one and the same key will be treated somewhat more specifically in
connection with the joining of melodic sections in periods of greater length.
The rule permits several exceptions when these sections have been extended
or compounded.

§37.

When at the beginning of a composition the first melodic section is a V-phrase,
the second should not be a I-phrase, else a connection results which also pro-
duces an unpleasant effect. The V-phrase must be either followed by a phrase
with a cadence, or the melody in the following phrase must be led into another


============================================================
PAGE 147
============================================================

116 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

key and again form a V-phrase. Examples of the first case are already familiar
to us from the preceding exercises, but the application of the second case be-
longs to later sections.

§38.

From all that has been said so far about the sequence of phrases, one might
easily tend to conclude that the exercise before us does not permit of many
useful punctuation forms. And one would be right, too. For the connection of
four melodic sections with only a single closing phrase allows only two satisfy-
ing punctuation forms for short compositions.

In the first form the first melodic section closes as a I-phrase, the second as a
V-phrase; the third melodic section is closed again as a I-phrase, and the
fourth necessarily with a cadence in the main key.

What has been said in section 31 about the third melodic section when it
closes as a I-phrase applies here too. In the following example of this form
(example 278), not the main phrase but the second melodic section has there-
fore been chosen for the closing phrase.

Example 278
Menuet

The second form is distinct from the first in that the third melodic section is
closed as a V-phrase instead of as a I-phrase. Here we have the case where, in
going from the first to the second reprise, two V-phrases are heard immedi-
ately one after the other and where we have to take care that their sequence
does not produce an unpleasant effect. A minuet by Haydn (example 279)
gives us an example of this form.”

IN WHICH MORE THAN FOUR MELODIC

b)

EXERCISE 4
SECTIONS ARE CONNECTED IN SHORT COMPOSITIONS

§39.

In the preceding exercises we have tried to explore all forms of punctuation on
small compositions made up of four melodic sections. This was partly in order
to get to know these forms themselves, but partly also to present all possible


============================================================
PAGE 149
============================================================

118 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

ways in which I-phrases, V-phrases and cadences can alternate appropriately
with each other, because the entire structure of periods depends on this alter-
nation. The following maxims have become known by means of these exer-
cises, namely,

1. After a I-phrase can follow either (a) a V-phrase, or (b) a cadence.

2. A V-phrase can be followed either (a) by a cadence, or (b) by a V-phrase
in another key, or (c) by a I-phrase if it does not stand at the beginning of a
composition, or also (d) at times by another V-phrase in the same key.

3. After a cadence we may have either (a) a I-phrase, or (6) a V-phrase, or
also (c) a cadence in a different key.

Although a short composition consisting of more than four melodic sec-
tions is arranged according to these maxims, I yet hope that it will not be
deemed to be too long-winded when I now point out the most useful forms in
the following exercise and present even more examples. The knowledge of
these forms is useful to the beginning composer not only in itself but also with
regard to the larger products of the art; for these forms are at the same time
representations in miniature of larger compositions.

In the next chapters, devoted to larger compositions, the description of
every possible form together with each and every possible phrase is omitted
because of the extraordinary length which this would necessarily entail. The
different arrangements of the main periods of compositions will be our only
concern.

§40.

Among short compositions in which more than four melodic sections are con-
nected, the dance melodies are distinguished from others in that generally not
more than two melodic sections are connected in the first reprise. To be sure, a
dance melody also can contain more than eight measures in the first reprise,
when it is not specifically intended for dances. But this number of measures is
usually achieved by those means of melodic extension which will be discussed
in the following chapter. The melodies to odes and songs, and the small pieces
of arbitrary meter and tempo can, to be sure, have the same characteristics in
their first period; but also more than two different melodic sections may be
connected as well.

In the second reprise, dance melodies can contain just as many melodic sec-
tions as every other kind of short composition. Therefore, because there is no
difference in the second reprise between dance melodies and the other types
of brief pieces with regard to the number of connected melodic sections, I shall
in this exercise again choose dance melodies as the punctuation model for
them all. Only at the close of the exercise shall I show how the first period of


============================================================
PAGE 150
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF SHORT COMPOSITIONS 119

the other kinds of works can be arranged when it contains more than two me-
lodic sections.

841.

When in the second reprise of a short composition more than two melodic
sections are connected, they always tend to have the closest possible con-
nection with each other in pairs. Therefore it is rare to find only three me-
lodic sections connected in the second reprise, perhaps because one of them
would be too isolated considering this close relationship, or because these
three melodic sections would have no good symmetrical relation with the two
in the first reprise. In short, instead of three phrases, four or more phrases are
always used for the formation of the second reprise, if it consists of more than
two phrases.”’

§42.

The type of modulation in the two phrases beginning the second reprise de-
pends mostly on the form of the first reprise. For it is easy to see that it must
modulate entirely differently when the first period closes in the key of the fifth
from when it closes in the main key.

If the first reprise has closed with a cadence or with a V-phrase in the main
key, there may or may not be a formal modulation in the first two phrases of
the second reprise.”

In the second case, when there is no formal modulation in these two
phrases, either (1) the first of them closes as a I-phrase without a passing mod-
ulation and the second as a V-phrase, after which the first period is then re-
peated and therewith the entire composition closed, as in example 280.”

27. Every melodic section from the first period which is repeated at the end of the second
must necessarily also be counted. [K.]

28. Here a formal modulation means one which is closed with a cadence in the key to which
the melody has been led. See section 28 of [the second division in] the second volume. [K.] Koch
distinguishes three types of modulation, which are ostensibly based upon the degree to which
the related key is established. An incidental [zufallig] modulation is one in which a few notes are
harmonized as if they were in another key; no modulation takes place, however. A passing
[durchgehend] modulation does involve a change of key and use of the leading tone of the new
key, but no close in it. In section 28, Koch outlines three possible types of formal [férmlich]
modulation. A composition may stay in a new key for a while without closing in it, and then
return to the main key or one closely related to it; it may close immediately in the new key; or,
the case that he cites here, it may remain for a while in the new key before closing in it. It must
be noted that his first option for a formal modulation does not differ from a passing modulation,
and his distinctions between incidental and passing modulations are actually quite ambiguous.
In his attempt to set up a hierarchy of modulation, from the most transitory to the most perma-
nent, Koch is somewhat inconsistent and uncharacteristically confusing. [B.]

Edition of the Complete Symphonies of Joseph Haydn, 12 vols. (Vienna, 1965-68), 1:240. [B.]

THE USE OF MELODIC MEANS OF EXTENSION 129

CHAPTER 3: THE USE OF MELODIC MEANS OF EXTENSION

§46.

The means by which the sections of melody can be given a greater length are
already familiar from the contents of the third section of the second volume
[sections 105-19]. Therefore in this chapter it only remains for us to get to
know their application. Because of the diversity of these means, this will be
done in different exercises.

EXERCISE 1, INWHICH THE MELODY IS EXTENDED BY
MEANS OF REPETITION

847.

The immediate repetition of a melodic section or of one of its segments is
doubtless that means of extension of melody most frequently used. But before
I continue and show how to use this means, I must first observe that even a
basic phrase may contain a repetition. In this way, for example, the first mea-
sure is repeated in the phrase in example 293, and in example 294 there is a
repetition of two measures.

Example 293

Here the discussion is neither about such repetitions which belong to the
completeness of a phrase and have their basis in its inner arrangement nor
about the repetition of entire periods. We are now concerned with the repeti-
tion of segments of phrases which are already complete in themselves.

Remark

In this chapter the repetition of melodic sections will be considered, with-
out regard to their aesthetic purposes and effects, merely as a technical means
to the extension of melody. But if the beginning composer uses this means in
his elaborations, he should nevertheless not disregard the fact that a melodic
section worthy of repetition either already contains much of the feeling to be


============================================================
PAGE 161
============================================================

130 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

expressed or that in the repetition new material must be given for the expres-
sion of the feeling. The latter can occur through various means, namely,
1. through increase or decrease in the strength of the tone in the perform-

ance of the repeated section,

2. through alteration of the figures by which the main melodic notes are
decorated,

3. through a new turn in the accompanying voices,

4. through increase or decrease [in the number] of the accompanying in-
struments,

5. through the combination of several of these means.

When and where each of these means should be used, either singly or in
combination, cannot be generally defined; rather the best use of them must be
learnec: from masters of the art through diligent study of their works.

§48.
The extension of melody occurs through the repetition of either entire com>
plete phrases or only a segment of a complete phrase.

This repetition of a melodic section or a segment of it occurs either on the
same underlying harmony or by means of tones which require a different har-
monic basis. If it is done on the same harmonic basis, either the melody is
played by means of the same main notes (with or without alteration of the
subsidiary notes), as in example 295, or the upper part is exchanged for the
middle part, which is actually present or even only assumed, as in example
296.

Example 295



============================================================
PAGE 162
============================================================

THE USE OF MELODIC MEANS OF EXTENSION 131

Example 296

That repetition of a melodic section which necessitates a different har-
monic basis is to be dealt with in the third exercise.
§49.

the first, second or third measure can be repeated without any restriction. This
is possible if the passage of melody at the end of that measure which is to be
repeated is constituted so that the repetition can take place without melodic
and harmonic ill effect, as in examples 297 through 299.

But if the phrase in which a repetition is to be made contains incises, then what

If the phrase contains incomplete incises and the first of these is repeated,

phrase in example 310, there are two incomplete incises in the first half; if the
first of them is repeated but the second is not, as in example 311, then the
unpleasart effect of their unequal treatment becomes very noticeable. Thus if
our feeling is not to be offended, the second segment must necessarily be re-
peated too, as in example 312.

§51.

If, on the contrary, the incises contained in the phrase are complete, that is,
take up two measures, then, as we shall see hereafter, in the case of a repeti-

But at times such a complete incise of two measures is changed by the ex-
tension of metrical units into a segment of three measures, and the rhythmic
relationship of sections is thereby made unequal. This can happen not only in
the first, but also in the second segment, without restricting the length of the
section immediately following.

Thus in both phrases in example 313, which consist of segments of an even
number of measures, either the first segment of the first phrase can be ex-
tended, as in example 314,”’ or the extension can be contained in the second
segment, as in example 315. In both cases, the second phrase consisting of four
measures can follow just as it could before a segment of the first phrase was
extended.

Still more usual in practice, especially in instrumental pieces, is the case

§52.

The repetition of two measures which make up an incise or of whole complete
phrases is used far more often than the repetition of a single measure or the
extension of metrical units into segments of an uneven number of measures
which have been described thus far.

A complete incise can always be repeated with or without variation regard-
less of the incises of the remaining melodic sections of the period. That is, the
repetition of such a complete incise can occur without necessitating a repeti-
tion in another phrase of the period. Thus in the following period in which the
complete incise of the second melodic section has been repeated, a repetition
can take place in the third phrase, as in example 320, but it can also be omitted
and the third phrase can be performed without repetition, as in example 321.

EXERCISE 2, INWHICH THE MELODY IS EXTENDED
THROUGH MULTIPLICATION OF THE PHRASE-ENDINGS AND
CADENCES

§54. .

An appendix is often attached to a complete phrase, through which its content
is either more strengthened or more closely defined. This appendix is found
not only with those melodic sections which close with a phrase-ending, but
also with those which end with a cadence. In this way, phrases show a multipli-
cation of phrase-endings on one and the same triad, but closing phrases show
many cadences in the same key. Considered technically, this multiplication of
phrase-endings and cadences by means of such an appendix will be a way of
extending the melody.

§55.

If the content of a phrase which concludes with a phrase-ending is to be
merely strengthened by the appendix, this appendix tends to be nothing more
than a repetition of the second segment of the phrase. It is performed either
without alteration of the subsidiary melodic notes, as in example 325, or with
alteration of them, as in example 326.

If, on the other hand, the content of the phrase is to be more precisely
defined through the appendix, then this appendix consists of a segment com-

Example 325



============================================================
PAGE 180
============================================================

THE USE OF MELODIC MEANS OF EXTENSION 149

plete in itself which is not contained in the phrase, as for instance in example
327; or indeed even two such clarifying segments are attached, as in example
328. In both cases, however, one should heed what has been said concerning
the decoration of caesuras on pp. 45—48.

Example 327

It should be noted that, in case such an appendix or several of them are
made, the decoration of the caesura or its overhang should not reach the oc-
tave of the root of the underlying triad until the phrase has attained its maxi-
mum completeness. In example 327, the caesura of the phrase-ending only
reaches the third B, but the caesura of the appendix with which the phrase is to
close is brought down to the octave of the root, that is, to the tone G. Indeed,
in example 328 all three caesuras are made with the third of the triad; only the
first is decorated by means of a preceding appoggiatura. In the second caesura
the third sounds without a suspension and moves up to the fifth of the underly-
ing triad in the overhang. On the other hand, in the third caesura, with which
the phrase attains its maximum completeness, the overhang or its decoration
goes down to the octave of the root. And this is the customary sign that the
phrase should not be extended further through an appendix.

§56.
If one bears in mind all that has been said in the preceding section, this appen-
dix may also occur with a V—phrase, as in example 329.


============================================================
PAGE 181
============================================================

150 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 329

§57.

At times the space between the caesura of the phrase-ending and the begin-
ning of the appendix is filled with notes; this can happen not only with a I-
phrase, as in example 330, but also with a V-phrase, as in example 331.

Example 330

Through this filling, the phrase very often (as in example 331) has the ap-
pearance of an extension which will become more familiar in the following
exercise.

§58.

In the comparison of the punctuation of phrases, only that phrase-ending with
which the phrase attains its maximum ,coffipleteness is taken into consider-
ation. The preceding phrase-endings on exactly the same triad are not taken


============================================================
PAGE 182
============================================================

THE USE OF MELODIC MEANS OF EXTENSION 151

into account, but are considered as mere incises. Thus this multiplication of
phrase-endings by means of an appendix takes place without transgressing the
rule given in section 34.

§59.

It is not necessary that the appendix of a I-phrase take its caesura again on the
triad of the keynote or the appendix of a V-phrase make its caesura on the
triad of the fifth. The appendix of the I-phrase also can close with its caesura
on the triad of the fifth, as in example 332, and the appendix of the V-phrase
can modulate to one of the most closely related keys and make its caesura on
the triad of the fifth of this key, as for instance in example 333.

Example 332

In this case, the phrase in example 332 has the value of a V-phrase as re-
gards the punctuation sequence of phrases, and the phrase in example 333 is
taken as a V-phrase in the key of the fifth.

§60.

Such an appendix which consists of a segment of an even number of measures
is never taken into account from the standpoint of the rhythmic relations of
melodic sections; the phrase is considered just as if this appendix were not
present.

This appendix can even consist of a segment of an uneven number of mea-
sures, which thus makes the rhythm uneven, as in example 334, without neces-
sitating that the following phrase also be of an uneven number of measures.


============================================================
PAGE 183
============================================================

152 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 334

§61.

If an appendix is added to a closing phrase, then either it is only a repetition of
its second segment, as with example 335, or the appendix consists of an unfa-
miliar segment not contained in the phrase, which closes again with a cadence
in the same key, as in example 336.

EXERCISE 3, IN WHICH THE MELODY IS LENGTHENED BY
MEANS OF THE EXTENSION OF COMPLETE MELODIC
SECTIONS

§63.

The extension of a complete phrase occurs either so that a technical means of
extension can be specified without doubt or in such a way that no specific
means can be perceived. Nothing definite can be said concerning this latter
type of extension of a complete phrase; rather it must be studied in the works
of composers. Usually the phrases extended in this way are so constituted that
they cannot actually be reduced into basic phrases. When such a phrase
consists—as it often does—of unequal segments, only taste can decide
whether this inequality of segments is or is not offensive to our feeling. In ex-
ample 173 there is a phrase extended in this way; also the andante by Haydn in
the preceding first exercise (example 324) gives examples of this type of exten-
sion from mm. 38—49 of the first,°? and from mm. 104-110 of the second sec-
tion.

39. Koch is evidently taking these measures out of the context in which they appear, for m.

began in m. 34. This theme begins with an incise which is immediately repeated; these four
measures cannot be considered a self-sufficient,phtdse and dépend on what follows in m. 38ff
for their completion. [B.]
