§64.

The means through which a phrase can be extended in a specific way are di-
verse. The first of them is displacement or the similar repetition of a segment
of the phrase on other degrees of the scale.

If such a segment is repeated on other degrees of the scale only once, then it
is usually not less than two measures in length.”

In the case that such a segment is repeated on other degrees of the scale so
that the melody remains in the same key, for example if the phrase in example
340 is extended as in example 341, then the displacement of this segment or
this extension of the phrase is not generally designated by a specific technical
term.

Example 340

But if there is a modulation with this displacement of the segment, as in
examples 342 and 343, then, as we already know, it is called a transposition.

§66.

With the extension of a phrase by means of sequence, one must be especially
careful to maintain the equality of segments until the end of the phrase. The
sequence in example 346 in the preceding section consists of two-measure seg-
ments. If this phrase were closed as in example 347, then the reason for its bad
effect would not be the uneven number of measures of which the phrase con-
sists, but rather that a section of a segment is lacking.

Example 347



============================================================
PAGE 189

158 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

In the first six measures of the phrase the listener is mainly aware of the succes-
sion of two-measure segments; thus his feeling again expects a segment of two
measures. If the phrase is closed unexpectedly with a segment which contains
only a single measure, then with so closely connected a melody, his sensibility
must necessarily be shocked by this incomplete segment. The unpleasant ef-
fect of the inequality of these segments would be felt still more strongly if the
sequence found in the following section were formed in the manner just de-
scribed as incorrect (see examples 349 and 350).

But if the sequence consists of only single-measure segments, one cannot
fall so easily into the error pointed out, because then one measure is usually
sufficient to form that segment which ends the phrase. Thus in the Haydn an-
dante the sequence could end without ill effect with a single-measure segment
in the manner of example 348, even though the phrase would be of an uneven
number of measures.

§71.

There are in addition two special types of interpolation, but the beginning
composer must proceed very cautiously with their use if good taste is not to be
offended. The first type consists in the interpolation of an entire melodic sec-

The second special kind of parenthesis is the insertion of melodic sections
of a simple meter in a piece composed in a compound meter.

Now with the interpolated phrase one must be careful not to take sections
of an even number of measures for those of an uneven number, nor simple
meters for compound, nor incomplete segments or incises for phrases. In ex-

through the sixth measure.*! Thus, with respect to the rhythmic relations of
phrases, each of these measures must be counted twice. But with the seventh
measure, a phrase has been inserted which is based in 2/2 meter and which,
with its repetition, lasts until the fourteenth measure, when the compound
meter again begins. Feeling convinces us that the phrase beginning with the

CHAPTER 4: THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS INTO
PERIODS OF GREATER LENGTH, OR THE ARRANGEMENT OF
LARGER COMPOSITIONS

§73.

Melodic sections are connected in compositions of greater length according to
the punctuation and rhythmical rules and maxims which were introduced in
the preceding chapters. Nevertheless, much yet remains to be said of their
connection in periods of greater length, the most necessary of which is to make
up the contents of this chapter.

42. In the section entitled “‘The aim and the inner nature of a composition, and particularly
the way in which it arises” (2:15—134), Koch outlines three levels of skill in conceiving melody
harmonically. In the first, the composer places all the expression of the feeling in the melody;
thus the accompanying parts need have only the appropriate variety in harmony, but not any
particular melodic features which help portray the feeling. Often they merely double the mel-
ody in thirds or sixths, or sound the tones of the underlying harmony. Koch cites as an example
of this level the aria ‘“Ein Gebeth um neue Starke” from Der Tod Jesu (1755) of Carl Heinrich
Graun (see section 3, note 14). The second level demands more skill in thinking harmonically.
The feeling is expressed in the melody, but the accompanying parts greatly strengthen this feel-
ing by particular melodic characteristics, usually by a continued metrical pattern. The highest
level of this ability is manifested when the composer invents two or more melodies which to-
gether express the feeling, as in a duet. This method of composition presumes both much expe-
rience and a lively imagination on the part of the composer. As Koch states on the page to which
he refers (2:91), these three approaches to conceiving melody harmonically can all be united in
the mind of a composer and he can seize on the one that is appropriate to the situation at hand,
guided by his genius and the feeling to be expressed. Koch presents these levels as separate
entities merely for pedagogical purposes; a practicing composer would not consciously distin-
guish between them. See also section 4, note 14. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 197
============================================================

166 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§74.

Even a slight attention to larger compositions shows that their various main
periods are very different in their manner of connection and treatment.*? Thus
not only the arrangement of the larger compositions with regard to their main
periods, but also the ways of connecting melodic sections in these various
main periods must be examined more closely in this chapter. This is to be done
in what follows.

1. THE NATURE AND ARRANGEMENT
OF THE MOST COMMON COMPOSITIONS

875.

If music expresses feelings, it occurs either by means of human voices which
combine the articulated tones of speech with the unarticulated tones of art, or
solely through the unarticulated tones of art, which are produced by musical
instruments. Thus arise two main types of the art, namely vocal and instru-
mental music.

§76.

In vocal music, the recitative, the aria in its various species, and the chorus
differ as special kinds of a whole. According to the content and treatment of
the poetry, the cantata, the oratorio, and the opera arise from their combina-
tion.

I. Recitative

877.

When combining music with poetry, the singing (or also acting) person ren-
ders those ideas through which he has gradually reached a high level of a spe-
cific feeling. In the description of these ideas, there is still no level of feeling so
high that, according to our nature, it could turn into an overflowing of the
heart and a sensual, passionate outburst, for example in song or gesticulation.
Thus for this type of poetic utterance which is still not passionate enough for
real song, a special kind of musical form has been invented that holds the
mean between declamation and formal song and which is called recitative.
§78.

As an entirely special type of composition deviating completely from all
others, recitative differs from ordinary declamation through the following char-

43. By a main period I mean the connection of several phrases, of which the last closes with a
formal cadence either in the main key or in one closely related to it. [K.]


============================================================
PAGE 198
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 167

acteristics. First, its articulated tones are not the usual ones of speech, whose
height and depth are entirely undetermined; the articulation results from
tones whose height and depth are fixed most precisely by a given keynote, that
is, its tones are sounded in a definite key. Secondly, these tones are arranged
so that they are fit for a harmonic support of different chords; and thirdly, they
are not performed by speaking, as is usual in declamation, but rather by singing.

But recitative differs from real song through the following characteristics.
First, it is bound to no definite movement within the measure. Only those syl-
lables which also stand out in declamation are somewhat extended. The re-
mainder are just treated curtly; they may be notated with quarter, eighth, or
sixteenth notes. The fitting of recitative in the measure therefore occurs
mainly so that both the larger and smaller resting points through which the
poetry is comprehensible and also those syllables which should have more em-
phasis than others can be presented according to the correct expression. Sec-
ondly, recitative has neither a definite meter nor a definite relationship be-
tween the lengths of its sections. Its larger and smaller resting points are
subject to no other rules than those which ordinary declamation also follows.
Because it is only a declamation, limited in keys and performed in a singing
tone, thirdly, neither a real continuous melody nor melismatic concatenation
of tones takes place. Fourthly, recitative is limited to no main key to which it
must return again and close after modulation into subsidiary keys. In addition,
several liberties of modulation are used which do not occur in proper song.

“It differs,” says Sulzer,” ‘‘from the aria, the song, and other texts serving
for formal song in that it is not lyrical. The verse is free, sometimes short,
sometimes long, without a constant meter. To be sure, this appears to define
only its outer character; but it has given rise to just this special type of song.”’
§79.
Now and then, passages are found in recitative which require a special type of
treatment. “‘No one imagines,” continues Sulzer in the article quoted, “that
the poet reserves only the weakest and most unimportant passages of his work
for the recitative, but places the strongest outburst of passions in arias or other
songs. For quite often, the opposite occurs, and of course must occur. The
very lively passions, rage, despair, grief, also joy and admiration, can seldom
be expressed naturally in arias when they have reached a high level. The ex-
pression of such passions will usually be uneven and discontinuous, which is
utterly contrary to the flowing nature of true song.”’

Composers tend to use the so-called accompanied recitative [accompagne-
ment] with such passages of recitative. They let the singer sing these passages
of the text in the proper manner of recitative style from incise to incise, but

44. In the A!lgemeine Theorie der schénen Kuinste, under the article “Recitative.” [K.] This
quotation and its continuation at the beginning of section 79 are found in Sulzer, ed., Allge-
meine Theorie, ‘‘Recitativ,”’ 4:482-84. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 199
============================================================

168 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

they portray the actual feeling by means of instrumental music, through pow-
erful and suitable phrases which are inserted between the incises of the vocal
part.

§80.

In recitative, when such tender, plaintive, or solemn, eloquent passages ap-
pear which continue through one or several verses in the same emotional
state, then the composer changes from ordinary recitative style to formal
song. These changes are performed by the vocal part either supported only by
the bass or with the accompaniment of several instruments. This formal me-
lodic section, inserted in the recitative style, which is subject to strict tempo
and the rules of metrical stress and rhythm is called arioso.

This arioso must possess all the characteristics of expressive song in a very
high degree, because the presentation of feeling is not continuous. It is not
capable of such modification as in arias, but must concentrate, as it were, on
one single point in its presentation of the feeling.

§81.

In addition, there is a special way of treating recitative which is also sometimes
called accompanied recitative [accompagnement] and which is used only in the
sacred cantata or oratorio. The intervals of the chords which lie at the basis in
ordinary recitative and which the general-bass player sounds in performance
are prolonged quite softly by the main parts of the orchestra in four-part har-
mony, in order to facilitate for the singer the pure intonation of the intervals.
If now the series of chords is chosen so that with each of their changes a few
intervals of the preceding chord can remain as intervals of the following one,
then this immutable soft continuation produces a very melting harmony. Be-
cause it is supported by no further metrical motion except that of the vocal
part (indefinite in meter), it creates an entirely unique solemnity, which can be
used with the best effect to accompany a prayer in the text, a vow, or any other
passage requiring a high degree of devotion.

If this manner of accompanying a recitative is to have its proper effect, then
the soft prolonging of tones must be connected with much refinement and con-
tinuity of the bowing technique. For example, if several instrumentalists
change the bowing simultaneously with not too strong a distribution of parts,
and if this does not happen unnoticeably and with the greatest refinement,
then a large part of the good effect of the passage is lost.

§82.

The technical treatment or the handling of a recitative requires quite specific
rules, most of which seem to be derived from the art of good declamation. It
has its wholly characteristic cadence and phrase-ending formulas, which devi-
ate completely from those typical of formal song. Moreover, modulation into
other keys is used with such freedom as can be employed in no other musical
composition. In short, an altogether special instruction is required for tne


============================================================
PAGE 200
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 169

treatment of recitative which necessarily is entirely different from the instruc-
tion to compose formal melody.

Moreover, he who wishes to study vocal composition must have attained
first a certain degree of skill in instrumental composition. One can thus under-
stand the reason why I have not taken up vocal composition (in which the
treatment of recitative makes up by far the lengthiest subject) in the plan of
this treatise.

Those beginning composers who wish to familiarize themselves with tech-
nical treatment of text in vocal music, especially the recitative style, find op-
portunity for that in Marpurg’s Anleitung zur Singcomposition.”

IT. The aria and its species

§83.

The aria, as it appears in the cantata and in drama also, consists of a worked-
out and very developed song accompanied by instruments. Through it a per-
son portrays in various modifications that feeling induced by the subject of the
vocal work.

The poetry of the aria consists of different verses, arbitrary in number and
meter, but always lyrical.

If these verses contain only one complete sentence of speech, then it is
called an ariette, in contradistinction to the usual aria, which always contains
two complete sentences.

§84.

Formerly not so many musical forms of the aria were used as now. The most
common form in which it appeared and which is still used frequently now with
little change has two main periods in the first section of the aria. A short instru-
mental interlude separates it from the second section, which in this case is only
a single period, and after the second section the first is repeated either entirely
or only in part.

The first main period or the first singing of the poem is preceded by a period
which is performed by the instruments alone and is called the ritornello. First,
this ritornello allows the singer, after he has sung the preceding recitative, to
recover and prepare himself for the performance of the aria. But secondly and
principally, it serves to prepare the listener for the enjoyment of the feeling

45. Friedrich Wilhelm Marpurg, Anleitung zur Singcomposition (Berlin, 1758). Koch’s two
types of accompanied recitative are distinguished by different names in the writings of Jean
Jacques Rousseau. He designates as ‘‘récitatif obligé”’ that type in which expressive instrumen-
tal sections alternate with the vocal phrases, and calls ‘‘récitatif accompagné”’ the type in which
sustained notes accompany the singer. See his Dictionnaire de la musique (Paris, 1768), “Récitatif
obligé,”’ pp. 404-05 and “‘Récitatif accompagné,”’ pp. 403-04. Marpurg, however, classifies
beth of these styles of recitative as accompagnement in his treatise on vocal music (see p. 81),
and Koch evidently saw no need for more precise terminology. [B.]

which the aria is to arouse.“ It consists mainly of such phrases which belong to
the plan of the aria, performed without special fragmentation and in the main
key, in which this ritornello always closes with a formal cadence.*’

After the ritornello the vocal part begins the first main period of the aria.
The verses of poetry which belong to the first section of the aria are first per-
formed without repetition and fragmentation, if their content is strongly con-
nected, so that the listeners can easily grasp their sense. But immediately af-
terwards, the most prominent passages are repeated and fragmented. At the
same time, there is a modulation to the key of the fifth (or with the foundation
of a minor key, also to the third), in which this first main period closes.

46. Sometimes, however, the vocal part begins the aria immediately after a recitative with-
out a preceding ritornello. This occurs when in the poetry the end of the recitative is so closely
connected with the beginning of the aria that the two cannot be separated through the ritornello
without inappropriateness, as a result of the prevailing feeling. A lovely example of such an
entrance of the aria without a ritornello is found in Schweitzer’s Alceste, after the monologue of
Admetus “‘O Jugendzeit, o goldne Wonnetage der Liebe”’ [‘‘O youth, O golden blissful days of
love’’].

Admetus, absorbed in sweet remembrance of his former possession of Alceste, in imagina-
tion sees her lose her way around Lethe’s banks; ‘‘liebreich drangen sich die Schatten um sie
her; sie bieten ihr aus Lethes Fluth gefiillte Schalen an. O hiite dich, Geliebte,’’ (sings Adme-
tus) ‘‘koste nicht von ihrem Zaubertrank! ziehe nicht mit ihm ein schreckliches Vergessen un-
serer Liebe ein!” [‘‘lovingly the shadows crowd around her; they offer her vessels filled from
Lethe’s stream. O take care, Beloved,” (sings Admetus) “‘taste not of its magic drink! enter not
with it into a terrible forgetting of our love’’}.

(Now he immediately begins the aria:)

“O flieh’ ge,iebter Schatten, fliehe! [‘‘O flee beloved shadow, flee!

Ich unterlage dem Gewicht I could not bear the weight

Von diesem schrecklichsten der Schmerzen! Of this most horrible of sorrows!

Noch lebt Admet in deinem Herzen, Admetus still lives in your heart,

O entziehe O do not take away

Dies einzige, dies lezte Guth ihm nicht!” From him this single, this last blessing!’’]

How much the anxious cry of Admetus “‘O flieh’ geliebter Schatten,”’ etc. would have lost if
the composer had separated these closely connected ideas through a ritornello after the last
words of the recitative: ‘‘Ziehe nicht mit diesem Zaubertranke ein schreckliches Vergessen un-
serer Liebe ein”! [K.] The passage discussed is from Act 4 of Alceste (1773) by Anton Schweit-
zer (see section 3, note 16), pp. 95-106. [B.]

47. Koch defines the plan of a musical work as ‘‘the main ideas of the composition already
connected with one another, which present themselves together to the composer as a complete
whole, along with its main harmonic features” (“die schon mit einander in Verbindung gebrach-
ten Hauptgedanken des Satzes, die sich zusammen dem Tonsetzer als ein vollkommnes Ganzes
darstellen, nebst den harmonischen Hauptziigen desselben. . . . ’” 2:53). In his treatise, Koch
attempts to explain the way in which a work arises in the mind of a composer, how he invents it
and how he works it out. To distinguish the stages of the creative process, Koch borrows three
terms which Sulzer used in his Allgemeine Theorie der schénen Kiinste: plan [Anlage]; realiza-
tion [Ausfiihrung]; and elaboration [Ausarbeitung]. Sulzer described these three stages of in-
vention, which trace their origin to the art of rhetoric, as common to the fine arts in general, and
he frequently illustrated his ideas with reference to painting. Although Koch relies heavily on
Sulzer for both his terminology and his definitions, he was original in his attempt to apply these


============================================================
PAGE 202
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 171

With texts which do not consist of many verses, the modulation usually
occurs in the third melodic section,* so that the real fragmentation of phrases
and the first main modification of the feeling happen in this related key, which
is intended chiefly for the working-out of the first period. The working-out and
fragmentation of phrases in the main key is reserved for the second half of the
second period.

After the first main period makes a full cadence in this most closely related
key, the instruments play a short interlude which, according to whether it is.
longer or shorter, closes either with a cadence or only with a V-phrase in this
key.

The second main period either begins in this key and modulates back to the
main key with the first or second melodic section, or it enters immediately in
the main key itself.

The first half of this second main period is reserved for the fragmentation of
the most important phrases and short modulations in other related keys, with-
out closing in them, however. Then the main phrase with its principal subsid-
iary phrases [Zergliederungssatze| may be played in the main key in a new way
and the period ended.*? Or only the second half of the first period is repeated
and thus this second period closes in the main key with exactly the sequence of
phrases with which the first period had closed in the fifth.

After this period also, the instruments play a short ritornello which ca-
dences in the main key, because with the repetition of the first section, the
entire aria ends with it.

As a result of the contents of the text, the second section is performed ei-
ther by means of a few phrases of the first section in just the same meter and
tempo or by means of new melodic sections in another meter and tempo. In
this form, it always contains only a single main period, in which moreover
there are few repetitions and fragmentations. Usually it has its own key either
beginning immediately with it or modulating to it when the period starts in the
main key. If, for example, the aria is set in a major key, then this second sec-
tion modulates to the minor key of the sixth or the major key of the fourth; it
either closes in one of the two or it modulates to the minor key of the second or
third in order to close therein. But very often with the entry of the second
section, the minor of the main key is interchanged with the major.

After this second section there was formerly a modulation into the main
key again by means of a short ritornello, and the entire first section was always

concepts to actual musical composition, and in so doing he elaborated on and modified their
meanings. [B.]

48. But if the first portion of the text consists of many verses, the modulation can also take
place in one of the following melodic sections, so that enough room is left over in the key of the
fifth for the remaining melodic sections and their fragmentation. Moreover I must note that I
talk here of modulation only in the technical sense. [K.]

49. See section 3, note 6 concerning Zergliederungssdtze. [B.]

repeated. But now more often the transition is made so that only the second
main period or a section of it is repeated, or the most important material of the
entire first section is gathered into a single period in the main key and written
out completely.

This form is indeed the most appropriate when the poet has portrayed the
essentials of the feeling in the first section of the aria and only a special modi-
fication of it in the second, which immediately leads back to the representation
of the first section.

Remark

Usually the ariette is different from this form only in that it has no second
section and the working-out and fragmentation of phrases tends not to be as
extensive as in the aria.

§85.

The second form of the aria used in modern vocal pieces is the rondo. If this is
to be used with the aria, then it requires a text of tender feeling suitable to this
form in its versification. The first section of such a text most appropriately con-
tains four short lines, of which the first and third, and the second and fourth
are similar or rhyme with each other. The second section can indeed be of
greater or lesser length according to the number of lines, but above all, it must
make the repetition of the first section if not necessary, yet most appropriate.
With such texts the different episodes always contain the second section of the
text, and the first section is reserved for the return of the rondo theme. It is still
better if the form of the text conforms with the arrangement of the rondo, that
is to say, if after the main sentence it also contains several complete sentences
which lead to the return of the principal one.

§86.

In music the rondo differs from all other compositions mainly in that its differ-
ent periods or episodes have no such similarity of melodic sections in common
as do the periods of other compositions; for its every period has a special com-
bination of specific melodic sections. The first of these, or the so-called rondo
theme, consists of only one single complete melodic section, which is pre-
sented first as a V-phrase, but in its immediate repetition is transformed into a
closing phrase of the main key.

In the aria this rondo theme is first presented by the instruments as a ri-
tornello,” and very often an appendix is added to its cadence. With the return
of the rondo theme, this appendix is often used instead of the entire ritornello
(as shown in example 363).>!

50. With concertos, however, it is more usual that the solo part performs the rondo theme
first, before it is repeated as a ritornello by the orchestra. [K.]

The second or third episode of the rondo, which usually begins immedi-

rondo. That is, its first characteristic melodic section is performed in the key
which has been chosen, first as a V-phrase, but afterwards as a closing phrase.
This is immediately followed by a further modulation into the key closest to
the chosen key, in which likewise a formal cadence is made. The rondo theme
of this period is then repeated in its proper key before the return of the main
theme, as in example 365, where the third episode of our rondo (without re-

It is evident that this form can be used only when the episodes are to be

§87.

In the third form of the aria which is frequently used in modern vocal pieces,
the first main period is composed of phrases of slow or moderate tempo, but
the remaining periods are formed in phrases of faster tempo.

This form cannot be as definitely analyzed and described as the two preced-
ing Ones since it requires here and there considerable deviations, partly be-
cause of the special nature of the text and partly also because of the way the
composer modifies the feeling prevailing in the aria. But just because it is not
used in so one-sided and fixed a manner as both the preceding forms, it is in
more than one respect the most excellent one, provided that the change to the
faster tempo does not contradict the prevailing mood.

Mostly this form has a modulation to the fifth in the slow first main period,
which is usually preceded by a ritornello of the same tempo. After the per-
formance of the different phrases, the most outstanding of these are frag-
mented and the period ends with a formal cadence in the fifth (but also at
times in the main key); then the allegro movement immediately begins with-
out a ritornello. This allegro ordinarily has two main periods which, without


============================================================
PAGE 210
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 179

particular variation, are of the same nature as the first two main periods of the
form described in section 84.

This form is very useful when the poet has given the stronger expression of
feeling to the second section of the aria.

Remark

The given text and its special turns of expression compel the composer at
times to use certain deviations from this form, as well as unusual forms of the
aria in general. The beginner must try to make himself familiar with these de-
partures through diligent study of the scores of good masters.

§88.
When two people portray their feelings in such a composition, each in his indi-
vidual way, then it is called a duet.

The duet, to be.sure, has just the same main periods and the same course of
modulation as the aria. The single technical difference seems to be that the
melodic sections are divided between now this, now that singer, or that some-
times both sing on together for some time with equally strong, prominent mel-
odies. But similar as the external arrangements of the duet and the aria ap-
pear, their actual treatment is very different. For the duet is polyphonic,” that
is, it must be composed in the way which I have described in the second vol-
ume.

One of the most difficult tasks of the composer, one that presupposes the
utmost knowledge of the art, is to have two people describe their feelings in
one period, each according to his individual way. Equally difficult is to invent
two melodies neither of which predominates and which, with every difference
of mood they must often express, make up a well-connected whole. Thus the
duet is a piece to be treated only by the composer who is already completely
trained. ;

Graun” has produced altogether first-rate masterpieces in this type of com-
position. Hiller,°’ even if he had not accomplished much himself in music,
would have earned great credit merely by saving these masterpieces of art
from oblivion and, by arranging their publication, making them more widely
known.

of the composer is contained in only a single voice, and which are called homophonic. See p.
83ff of the second volume. [K.] See section 4, note 42. [B.]

55. Koch refers again to the portion of ‘‘The aim and the inner.nature of a composition, and
particularly the way in which it arises”’ (2:87) in which he describes the different levels of ability
to conceive melody harmonically. The duet requires the highest such level, because it is poly-
phonic, or has two equal melodies which together express a feeling. See section 4, note 42 for a
more detailed discussion. [B.]

Il. The chorus

§89.

The chorus differs from the aria in that the expression of a specific feeling is
presented by many people at the same time, who represent either a whole
people or a special class of it, an entire congregation, or such-like. It also dif-
fers in that its melodic sections must be constituted totally differently from
those of the aria. The phrases of the aria necessarily must be completely and
most precisely developed, because they contain the feeling of a single person
who expresses it in his own individual way. In a chorus, on the other hand, the
phrases must not be highly developed, because they should present the feeling
of an entire crowd. Thus the melodic sections of a chorus must distinguish
themselves more through inner power than through the highest development
or refinement of expression.

890.

The chorus is one of the most difficult tasks of the composer. This is not, as
many beginners believe, merely because he is thereby tied to a strict four-
voice composition which he cannot vary with two- or three-voice passages as
arbitrarily as in instrumental pieces; nor is it because the text of each one of
the four voices must be underlaid without breaking its continuity and without
logical errors. The greatest difficulty is that in the invention of the plan of his
chorus, the composer must proceed according to the polyphonic art already
mentioned before and described in the second volume.

§91.

In the sacred cantata, the chorus appears in two different forms, for this kind
of vocal piece is fashioned either in the strict or fugal style or in the free and
galant style. In the opera and in cantatas which are not particularly devoted to
edification, usually only the latter style is used.

§92.

The choruses of the free style are rather similar to the aria in the number,
form, and mcdulation of their periods. The choruses which are in the middle
and at the end of vocal works, however, do not use a ritornello at the begin-
ning as generally as in the aria; very often the vocal parts enter directly after
the recitative. As to the internal arrangement, on the other hand, there are
two different modes of treatment: either these are separated so that only one
of them prevails throughout the entire chorus, or they are combined.

The first treatment of a chorus in the free style is that the vocal parts are
very undeveloped with regard to the melodic figures and only contain the
tones of the harmony without melodic embellishments. Simultaneously the
instruments move on by means of such melodic figures as correspond to the
expression of the prevailing feeling, as in example 366.°°

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 187

§93.

If the chorus is composed in the bound or strict style, then it either is a formal
fugue or contains only such imitations and contrapuntal passages as are cus-
tomary in a fugue but which are used without following its strict form.

§94.

The fugue is a composition in which a passage or a melodic section which is
formed especially so that it is capable of different kinds of imitations is worked
out according to certain rules that are quite specific to this type of piece. The
melodic section is transferred from one voice to the other amid continual vari-
ations throughout the entire work.

The text to this type of chorus is usually a passage from the Bible which can
be sung appropriately by many people together, as in the fugue on the words
“Christus hat uns ein Vorbild gelassen”’ in Graun’s Tod Jesu.©

The fugue requires an arrangement completely differing from all other
compositions and is treated according to rules which pertain to it exclusively.
The beginning composer who wishes to penetrate the intrinsic nature of com-
position must, after he has made some progress in the free style, seek to attain
a thorough knowledge of fugue. For this, the best introduction is in Marpurg’s
Abhandlung von der Fuge.®©!

§95.

But the chorus can also be composed in the strict style without appearing in
the proper form of the fugue. In this case, it contains passages similar to the
fugue and imitations in double counterpoint, without a single main phrase
prevailing and without the phrases being bound to a certain definite order in
which they must enter or follow one another. But, just as for the fugue itself, a
basic knowledge of all types of imitations and of double counterpoint is
needed for the working-out of this kind of chorus in which metrical texts are
set also.

Because this type of chorus does not appear too frequently, I shall insert
one brief passage as an example in order to make the beginner familiar with its
composition (example 369).

194 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§96.
Three main types of composition are used in instrumental music, namely, the
opening or preparatory pieces, the sonatas, and the concertos.

To the opening pieces which are used for the introduction to a play or a
concert belong the overture and the symphony.

The sonatas are of either two or more parts. The two-part sonata may con-
tain only one main voice which is supported by an accompanying bass, or both
parts may participate equally in the melody and must rightfully be considered
main voices. This kind of sonata in particular is called a duet. If the sonata
contains only one main part supported by a bass part, the general designation
‘sonata’ is retained if the work is composed for an instrument on which both
can be performed at the same time, as for example, the clavier or the harmon-
ica. If, however, it is composed for instruments on which only the main voice
alone can be performed, as for example the flute, violin, and the like, then it 1s
usually called a solo. The sonatas of many parts get their specific names from
the number of parts which they contain; thus the three-, four-, and more-
voiced sonatas are called a trio, quartet, and quintet.

The concerto may be the customary chamber concerto with which a musi-
cian can be heard on his instrument with the accompaniment of an entire or-
chestra; it has gotten its name from the chamber music usual at the courts of
reigning princes. Or it may be a concerto grosso, so-called by the Italians, in
which several virtuosi may be heard on their instruments, sometimes alter-
nately, sometimes together.

IV. The overture

§97.

The overture is, as its name indicates, of French origin, and has obtained a
definite form from Lully® in particular. It differs from other compositions
through a formal fugue, which is always preceded by a short movement of
great seriousness and pathos, written in a particular style. The fugue, which
makes up the main movement of the overture and usually is of a brisk tempo,
can appear in any meter. The preceding earnest movement, however, is com-
posed in 4/4 meter and contains many dotted notes and imitations in melodic
figures which are characteristic of it. These mainly consist of a dotted quarter
or dotted eighth note followed by three, four, or more rapid notes which re-
quire a powerful and staccato articulation. An example of such a movement
will be able to show the beginner its nature more clearly than a lengthy de-
scription (example 370). This movement always ends with a cadence in the
fifth, after which the fugue immediately begins, again in the main key. After
the fugue, usually a section of this movement is repeated in the main key.
§98.

Since the overture is a piece which serves as introduction to an opera, orato-

Allegro
>

rio, or cantata, the fugue as its main movement has no strongly defined charac-
ter. Rather it conforms to the character of that piece which it introduces. If it is
composed not for preparation of a particular vocal work, but as an opening
piece of chamber music or of a concert, an allegro or one or several character-
istic dance melodies follow the fugue in addition.

§99.

Nowadays the overture has been almost entirely supplanted by the symphony.
It may be, as Sulzer thinks, because its composition and manner of perfor-
mance are so difficult.© Or it may be because the strict fugue is too austere
nourishment for the fashionable taste of our time, which likes fugal composi-
tion and double counterpoint in an instrumental work only when it is com-
bined with comic ornaments in one and the same movement to provoke laugh-
ter. Now so many amateurs of the art wish to have such compositions, many
composers, indeed, serve them up. Whether this or that is the reason for the
decline of the overture will be set forth in its place.

V. The symphony

§100.

The symphony is an instrumental piece of many parts, of which the four main
Ones, namely the first and second violin, viola, and bass, are strongly rein-
forced. It is used not only for the introduction of a play and a cantata, but also
for the opening of chamber music or concerts. In the first case, it often consists
of only a single allegro; but in the latter case, it usually contains three move-
ments of different character.“ For the most part, the character of magnifi-

63. Sulzer, ed., Allgerneine Theorie, ““Ouvertiire,” 3:388-89. [B.]

64. Koch’s description of a symphony as having only three movements seems reactionary in
a treatise completed in 1793. His symphonic illustrations which can be identified, however,
were written from 1762 to 1782, and at the beginning of this period the four-movement sym-
phony was certainly not the norm. In addition, although Koch was forward-thinking in his the-
ories, in his own compositions he favored the three-movement form. Yet, of the three Haydn
symphonies that he quotes in the treatise, two of them are four-movement works (nos. 20 and
42), and in the remark following section 107, he does mention that many composers tended to
add a minuet and trio to the symphony. Koch obviously was aware of this trend; it is likely that
he simply feit it unnecessary to describe the minuet in connection with the symphony because he
had already treated it in such detail in chapter 2, ‘“The connection of melodic sections into peri-


============================================================
PAGE 229
============================================================

198 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

cence and grandeur belongs to the first allegro, the character of pleasantness
to the andante, and of gaiety to the last allegro.

Because the symphony is one of the most important compositions for those
composers who wish to occupy themselves only with instrumental pieces, a
closer description of its characteristics is not out of place here. Thus I shall
insert an extract concerning the aesthetic nature of this composition from Sul-
zer’s Allgemeine Theorie der schonen Kiinste.

One can compare the symphony with an instrumental chorus just as the so-
nata with an instrumental cantata. In the latter, the melody of the main part,
which is only set simply, can be composed so that it bears and often even re-
quires decoration. In the symphony, on the other hand, where every voice is
set more simply, the melody must contain the maximum force already in the
notes written out, and no voice can bear the least decoration or coloratura.
Because it is not an exercise like the sonata but must be sightread, there must
be no difficulties which cannot be confronted and clearly played at once by
many.

The symphony is particularly suited for the expression of greatness, solem-
nity, and grandeur. Its goal is to prepare the listener for important music or, in
a chamber concert, to summon all the magnificence of instrumental music. If it
is to satisfy this goal completely and be an integral part of the opera or church
music which it precedes, then, besides expressing greatness and solemnity, it
must put the listener into the frame of mind which the following piece re-
quires. It must distinguish itself by the distinct style proper for either church or
theater.

The chamber symphony, which is an independent whole, aiming at no fol-
lowing music, attains its purpose only through a sonorous, brilliant, and fiery
style. The allegros of the best chamber symphonies contain great and bold
ideas—powerful bass melodies and unisons; concerting middle parts; free imi-
tations; often a fugally treated theme; sudden transitions and digressions from
one key to another, which are more striking the weaker the connection is; and
considerable gradations of loud and soft, especially the crescendo, which is of
the greatest effect when it is used together with an ascending melody which
increases in expression.

The andante or largo between the first and last allegro has, to be sure, no
such closely defined character, but often is of pleasant, or somber, or melan-
choly expression; yet it must have a style which is in keeping with the dignity of
the symphony.

Opera symphonies are like the chamber symphony more or less, but
adapted to the character of the opera to be presented. Yet it seems that they
tolerate less extravagance and need not be so elaborate, because the listener is
more attentive to what follows than to the symphony itself.©

ods of short length, or the arrangement of short compositions.”” Also he made this comment
directly before quoting Sulzer’s description of the symphony as having three movements, and
he probably wished to avoid any inconsistency. [B.]

65. Sulzer, ed., Allgerneine Theorie, “Symphonie,” 4:724-26. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 230
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 199

§101.

The first allegro of the symphony, to which the description above particularly
applies, has two sections which may be performed with or without repetition.
The first of these consists only of a single main period and contains the plan of
the symphony; that is, the main melodic phrases are presented in their original
order and afterwards a few of them are fragmented. Following the cadence a
clarifying period is often appended that continues and closes in the same key
in which the preceding one also had closed. Thus it is nothing else than an
appendix to the first period and both united may quite properly be considered
a single main period.©

The structure of this period, as also of the other periods of the symphony,
differs from that of the sonata and the concerto not through modulations to
other keys, nor through a specific succession or alternation of I- or V-phrases.
Rather it differs in that (1) its melodic sections tend to be more extended al-
ready with their first presentation than in other compositions, and especially
(2) these melodic sections usually are more attached to each other and flow
more forcefully than in the periods of other pieces, that is, they are linked so
that their phrase-endings are less perceptible. For the most part, a melodic
section is directly connected with the caesura tone of the preceding phrase.
Very often no formal phrase-ending is written until the rushing and sonorous
phrases are exchanged for a more singing phrase, usually to be played with less
force. Thus many such periods are found in which a formal phrase-ending is
not heard until there has been a modulation into the most closely related key.
For the main melodic sections are all presented in the main key just as infre-
quently in the symphony as in other compositions. Rather, after the theme has
been heard with another main phrase, the third such phrase usually modulates
to the key of the fifth—in the minor mode also towards the third—in which the
remaining sections are presented, because the second and larger half of this
first period is devoted particularly to this key.

In the newer symphonies, the first allegro tends to be preceded by a brief
introductory passage which is slow and serious. This introduction differs from
the so-called grave of the overture in that it requires neither characteristic
figures nor a special meter. Rather it can appear in all meters and use all
figures which have an earnest character. Discounting passing modulations,
this passage remains in the main key in which it closes either with a V-phrase-
ending or with a cadence. Often a seventh with a fermata is added to the triad
ending the V-phrase, or the cadence runs over into the following allegro, that
is, the caesura tone of the cadence is at the same time the first note of the
allegro.

§102.
The second section of the first allegro consists of two main periods, of which
the first tends to have greatly diverse structures. Nevertheless, if one discounts

66. In the andante by Haydn, which is at the end of section 53 [example 324], there is such a
period from the 49th measure until the end of the first reprise. [K.]


============================================================
PAGE 231

the smaller variations, they may be reduced to the following two main types of
treatment.”

The first and most usual construction of the first period of the second sec-
tion begins in the key of the fifth® with the theme, occasionally also with an-
other main melodic idea, either note for note, in inversion, or also with other
more or less considerable alterations. After that it either modulates back into
the main key by means of another melodic idea, and from this to the minor key
of the sixth, or also to the minor key of the second or third. Or it may not first
return to the main key; rather the phrase that goes from the fifth into one of
the keys mentioned may be led there by means of a sequence or another type
of extension, with which generally one or several passing modulations are
used. Then a few of those melodic sections that are best suited for presenta-
tion in one of these keys® are repeated or dissected in another form or combi-
nation than they had in the first period, whereupon the period ends in this
key.”

Usually a short passage is connected with this second main period of the
symphony, which consists of a segment of a main melodic idea drawn out in a
sequential way. By this means the modulation is carried back to the main key,
in which the last main period begins.

The other method of building this [second] period frequently used in mod-
ern symphonies is to continue, dissect, or transpose a phrase contained in the
first section—often only a segment of it—that is especially suitable for such
treatment. This is done either in the upper part alone or also alternately in
other parts. There may be passing modulations in several keys, some closely
and some distantly related, before the modulation into that key in which the
period is to end. This happens either only until the ending of the V-phrase in
this key, or the phrase is continued in a similar manner until the close of the
entire period. An example of this latter type of treatment will be given later,
when we consider the connection of periods in particular. However, if the
fragmentation of such a phrase is carried only to the end of the V-phrase in
that kev in which this period will close, then a few melodic ideas of the first
period, usually changed somewhat, are presented in that [closing] key before

the cadence arrives. Examples of this may be found in many Haydn and nearly
all Dittersdorf”! symphonies.

In this case too the period usually acquires the appendix mentioned before,
which modulates back to the main key for the beginning of the last period.

By the way, modern symphonies do not always start this second period in
the key of the fifth; often it begins in an entirely unexpected key, either with-
out any preparation or by means of only a few introductory tones that follow
the cadence in the fifth.

§103.

The last period of our first allegro, which is devoted above all to the main key,
most frequently again begins with the theme in this key, but occasionally may
also start with another main melodic idea. The most prominent phrases are
now compressed, as it were, during which the melody usually shifts to the key
of the fourth, but, without making a cadence in it, soon again returns to the
main key. Finally the second half of the first period, or those melodic ideas of
the first period which followed the V-phrase in the fifth, is repeated in the
main key and with this the allegro ends.

§104.

The andante or adagio of the symphony is found in three different forms. In
the first of these, already used in the older symphonies, the andante has two
main sections, which are presented with or without repetition. The first sec-
tion always consists of only one main period, as in the allegro; when based in a
major key, it modulates to the key of the fifth, but, based in a minor key, it
modulates either to the major key of the third or the minor key of the fifth, and
closes therein.

With the second section, the question is whether or not the andante is to be
greatly extended. If the movement is to be very long, then it tends to have two
main periods, which in their external structure are very similar to both those
periods of the second section of the allegro described above in section 102.
The most important external difference is that in the andante the melodic
ideas are less extended and not so often compounded; thus more formal
phrase-endings are used than in the allegro. This accords with the nature of
the feelings which tend to be expressed in slow movements. The andante by
Haydn in section 53 (example 324) gives an example of an andante in which
the second section contains two periods.

On the other hand, if the andante is not to be very long, then these two
periods are contracted into a single one. This happens if the working-out of

71. Karl Ditters von Dittersdorf (1739-1799) was a popular Austrian violinist and com-
poser. Employed as a violinist in the court opera of Vienna, Dittersdorf became acquainted not
only with Italian opera, but also with Gluck and Haydn. In 1769 he entered the service of Count
Schaffgotsch, Prince Bishop of Breslau at Johannisberg, where he remained for twenty-six
years. Most famous for his operas and Singspiele, such as his Doktor und Apotheker (1786),
Dittersdorf also wrote many symphonies, concertos, and chamber works. [B.]

the melodic ideas in the minor key of the sixth or second and the cadence in
this key are omitted. After the theme has been presented in the fifth and there
has been a modulation back to the main key, the minor key of the sixth, sec-
ond, or third is touched either not at all or only in passing. Then the theme is
repeated again or, without that repetition, those phrases which followed the
V-phrase in the first period are presented again in the main key. Examples of
this sort are not hard to find, for nearly every short, complete andante or alle-
gretto shows the use of this form.

§105.

The second form which the andante of the symphony takes is the rondo. Noth-
ing else important remains to be said of this here, because it already has been
described above in connection with the aria.

§106.

The third design or form of the andante shows the use of variations on a short
andante or adagio passage. This usually consists of two sections of eight to ten
measures each and often has an appendix, which is presented between every
variation as a ritornello. The variations of the principal melody are performed
either by the first violin alone, or in alternation with other parts. Examples of
this form are found in very many symphonies by Haydn, who not only was the
first one to make use of it in an andante movement but also has produced first-
rate masterpieces in it.

§107.

According to the character which it assumes, the last allegro of the symphony
appears either in the form of the first allegro or as a rondo. Sometimes it also
contains variations on a typical dance melody or on a short allegro passage;
these variations are, however, usually mixed with brief episodes in closely re-
lated keys, after the manner of the rondo.

Remark

Many composers like to add to the symphony a minuet with a so-called trio,
which comes sometimes before, but mostly after the andante. Because such a
minuet'is not intended for the dance, not only can its length be arbitrary, but it
can also contain sections of an uneven number of measures.

VI. The sonata

§108.

The sonata, with its various species—the duet, trio, and quartet—has no
definite character, but its main sections, namely, its adagio and both allegros,
can assume every character, every expression which music is capable of de-
scribing. ‘‘In a sonata,” says Sulzer, ‘“‘the composer can aim at expressing ei-
ther a monologue in tones of sadness, of lamentation, or affection, or of plea-
sure and of cheerfulness; or he can try to sustain purely in sentiment-laden
tones a dialogue among similar or contrasting characters; or he may merely


============================================================
PAGE 234
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 203

depict passionate, violent, contradictory, or mild and placid emotions, pleas-
antly flowing on.’”’”

Because in the composition of sonatas the main parts are only set simply,
the melody of the sonata must stand in relation to the melody of the symphony
just as the melody of the aria does to the melody of the chorus. That is, be-
cause it depicts the feelings of single people, the melody of a sonata must be
extremely developed and must present the finest nuances of feelings, whereas
the melody of the symphony must distinguish itself not through such refine-
ment of expression, but through force and energy. In short, the feelings must
be presented and modified differently in the sonata and symphony.

§109.

The two-voice sonata or the solo, because it expresses the individual feelings
of a single person, necessarily requires the greatest refinement of expression
and of the modifications of the feelings to be portrayed. Thus the melody must
be most highly developed. Because every instrument is capable of different
refinements of expression which it alone can produce, the composition of a
sonata requires the most exact knowledge of that instrument for which such a
piece is to be composed. Thus good sonatas can only be expected from those
composers who also are virtuosi on the instrument for which they write such
pieces.

Among the Germans, C. P. E. Bach’ has distinguished himself particu-
larly in this type of composition through his clavier sonatas. Only his highly
developed, personal style of playing, combined with the most profound
knowledge of composition, could bring about what he has achieved in this
line.

For the violin and flute Franz Benda and Quantz™ have composed sonatas
of which many completely correspond to the ideal one inevitably forms of a
good sonata. It is only unfortunate that the path broken by these men was
subsequently followed by far too few. Much too often a more refined and cul-
tivated expression was replaced by empty noises with many difficulties, which
left the heart the more unstirred the more the fingers moved.

Noisy, overcomplicated sonatas are, to be sure, a necessity for those solo-

204 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

ists who wish to display mere technical skill on the instrument, and not expres-
sion of feelings. But more thought should be given to the general usefulness of
sonatas designed for the public. For not only amateurs but also most artists
are concerned more about expressive pieces than about difficult works of this
kind. Proof of this is given by the clavier sonatas of Tiirk,’> which are generally
loved because along with the suitable presentation of pleasant feelings they do
not frighten off the amateur by too many difficulties. Moreover, they are writ-
ten in a style which is very affecting for any feeling not yet overindulged—all
qualities which can rightly be required of compositions of this kind which are
intended for the public.

§110.

The external arrangement of the sonata, namely, the different forms of both
its allegros and its adagio, need not be examined in particular here, for the
sonata assumes all the forms which already have been described before in con-
nection with the symphony. Thus, for example, the first allegro has two sec-
tions which are usually repeated. The first of these sections contains one main
period, the second section has two, and all follow the same course of modula-
tion as the main periods of the symphony. The last allegro also can have either
the form just indicated, or the form of the rondo, or also variations on a short
passage such as has been described on p. 83.

But as similar to one another as the forms of the sonata and the symphony
may be in the number of periods and the course of modulation, as different,
conversely, is the inner nature of the melody in the two. This difference, how-
ever, can be better felt than described; only this can be observed for the most
part as an external characteristic: in the sonata the melodic sections are not
connected as continuously as in the symphony, but more often are separated
through formal phrase-endings. They are not usually extended through the
continuation of a segment of this or that melodic section or through se-
quences, but more often by clarifying additions, defining the feeling most ac-
curately.|

VII. The duet

§111.

The duet is a composition for two similar or two different concerting instru-
ments which, like the sonata, consists of three movements of different charac-
ter. It is treated in two entirely different ways, which should rightly be distin-
guished through special names, because they are so very dissimilar. For as the
solo consists of one main part which is accompanied and supported by a bass

75. Daniel Gottlob Tiirk (1750-1813) was a very successful and popular German composer
and theorist. He wrote numerous works for piano, among them the sonatas that Koch praises, a
few symphonies, and cantatas. His theoretical works include the Clavierschule (1789) and the
Kurze Anweisung zum GeneralbafBspielen (1791), both of which went through numerous edi-
tions and earned him great renown. In 1779 he became musical director of the University of
Halle, and, in 1806, professor of music theory there. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 236
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 205

part, so on the other hand the true duet consists of two main parts which are
both quite indispensable throughout the entire piece. That is, they make up
two equally prominent melodies which, in their combination, are themselves
already so rich in changing harmony that they do not need a bass part for sup-
port, indeed, do not appropriately permit one. This kind of composition must
be treated according to the so-called polyphonic method, described in the sec-
ond volume, and is subject to the same difficulties already described above in
connection with the vocal duet.”

This kind of duet is entirely neglected by the more modern composers, at
least I know of none which deserve the name duet since the flute duets of Tele-
mann” and Quantz.

§112.

Those duets which are known by a few modern composers involve a two-part
composition which is completely homophonic. It differs from the sonata only
in that both parts alternately carry the melody and alternately accompany
each other in the form of an ordinary bass voice, which only at times is mixed
with more passing notes and appoggiaturas than the bass voice in other many-
voice compositions.

Since this type of two-voice composition does attain the true purpose of art,
it would be absurd to want to reject it entirely because it does not completely
conform to the conception of the proper duet. But it would be right to differ-
entiate both these kinds of duets through special names, so one could at least
judge in advance which type of treatment to expect.

§113.

All that has been mentioned in Section 110 concerning the form of the sonata
applies also to the form of duets. For in this type of composition, no other
difference in the external arrangement of the main periods is found than that
the melodic sections tend to be repeated more often than in the proper sonata.
After they have been played by one instrument they are usually immediately
repeated by the other.

VII. The trio

§114.

The trio, an instrumental work of three parts, also consists of three move-
ments of different character. With every instrument the most use is made of
the middle tones of its range; thus if three instruments of similar height or
depth were to be chosen for a trio, the different parts would too often get in

each other’s way, and the range of its tones would be too narrow for a three-
voice piece. Therefore, in these compositions, to two high parts, for example
to two violins or two flutes, or to a flute and violin, a violoncello tends to be
added as a third voice.

§115.

The trio, too, is treated in many different ways. ‘“The proper trio,” says Sul-
zer, “‘contains three main parts which concert with one another and, as it
were, maintain a dialogue in tones. Each voice must be involved in it and while
contributing to the harmony, it must, at the same time, let us hear a melody
which agrees with the character of the whole and furthers its expression. This
is one of the most difficult categories of composition. To be successful in it,
one must not only understand three-part composition, but also have total
command of everything that belongs to fugue and double counterpoint, in ad-
dition to a flowing and expressive melody.’’”8

Trios of this kind are composed no longer, and those which are still surviv-
ing in copies from older composers unfortunately sink more and more into
oblivion.

§116.

More usual are those three-part sonatas which contain only two concerting
voices with an accompanying bass and which, considering their treatment and
the nature of the melody, rather resemble the vocal duet.

Approximately at the middle of this century, this type of sonata was culti-
vated most intensely and many composers produced very worthy works of this
kind. But for the most part they are known only here and there through cop-
ies, which fall into decay unused, partly on account of the now more popular
quartet and also because of the general inclination of modern virtuosi to play
concertos.

Among the sonatas of this type the flute trios of the late musical director
Graun” especially distinguish themselves, because the greatest richness of
harmony and the most agreeable and persuasive melodies are combined with
the most beautiful imitations of all kinds. This not only delights the ear but
also at the same time touches the heart.

Beginners can study the expression of pleasant feelings and also the art of
combining the greatest variety of harmony with the most pleasing melody no-
where better than in Graun’s flute sonatas.

§117.

Finally there is a type of three-voice sonata which contains only one main part;
the second part accompanies merely as a filling voice where it cannot proceed
with the main part in thirds or sixths. If it is to hold its own, this type requires a
very charming and expressive melody in the main voice.

79. As Kapellmeister for Frederick the Great, Carl Heinrich Graun wrote numerous cham-

IX. The quartet

§118.
The quartet, currently the favorite piece of small musical societies, is culti-
vated very assiduously by the more modern composers.

If it really is to consist of four obbligato voices of which none has priority
over the others, then it must be treated according to fugal method.

But because the modern quartets are composed in the galant style, there
are four main voices which alternately predominate and sometimes this one,
sometimes that one forms the customary bass.

While one of these parts concerns itself with the delivery of the main mel-
ody, the other two [melodic voices] must proceed in connected melodies
which promote the expression without obscuring the main melody. From this
it is evident that the quartet is one of the most difficult of all kinds of composi-
tions, which only the composer who is completely trained and experienced
through many compositions may attempt.

Among the more modern composers, Haydn, Pleyel, and Hoffmeister ™
have enriched the public the most with this type of sonata. The late Mozart
also had engravings made in Vienna of six quartets for two violins, viola, and
violoncello with a dedication to Haydn.®! Among all modern four-part sonatas
these most closely correspond to the concept of a true quartet and are unique
on account of their special mixture of the strict and free styles and the treat-
ment of harmony.

X. The concerto

§119.
The concerto, an instrumental piece in which a main part is accompanied by an
entire orchestra, also has no definite nature. But its three main sections,

80. Ignaz Joseph Pleyel (1757-1831) was an Austrian composer, publisher, and piano manu-
facturer. A pupil of Haydn, he became Kapellmeister to Count Erdédy in 1777 and subse-
quently Kapellmeister at the Strasbourg Cathedral. After directing the Professional Concerts in
London in 1792, he eventually became a music publisher in Paris, where he brought out many of
Haydn’s quartets and symphonies. In 1807, he established the piano factory Pleyel et Cie.
Pleyel concentrated on instrumental music and composed symphonies and particularly cham-
ber music in great quantity. At the time the Versuch was written, his string quartets were very
highly regarded and Mozart himself suggested Pleyel as a possible successor to Haydn in the
musical world. Franz Anton Hoffmeister (1754-1812) was a German publisher and a prolific
composer. He wrote some dramatic works and orchestral pieces, but his favored genre was
chamber music. He concentrated on ensembles which included the flute and wrote dozens of
string quartets. Hoffmeister established a printing house in Vienna in 1783, which printed many
of the works of Mozart and Beethoven for the first time. [B.]

81. Koch is referring to the ‘““Haydn” Quartets of Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart (1756-1791),
K. 387, 421, 428, 458, 464, and 465, which were published in 1785. [B.]}


============================================================
PAGE 239
============================================================

208 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

namely its two allegros and its adagio, can assume every mood which music is
capable of expressing.

It is that piece with which the virtuosos usually may be heard on their in-
strument; thus there are concertos for all of the standard instruments of an
orchestra.

For reasons shown in connection with the solo, the composer can, how-
ever, write a good concerto only for that instrument which he himself plays
with a certain degree of skill. Not only did many a concerto player formerly
lack a sufficient number of such pieces, but also he often could not use those
which were obtainable because of the special way of playing which they re-
quired. Thus nothing else remained for many concerto players, especially
those who played an instrument for which not many concertos had yet been
composed or become known, than to compose their concerto parts and their
accompaniment themselves, as best they could, or to let them be composed by
others. After several instrumentalists of all kinds concerned themselves with
composition, this product of music is so assiduously cultivated that there is
now no reason to complain about the lack of concertos for any instrument.
Nevertheless, the procedure referred to before remains the story of many a
concerto which appears in public. Moreover, there are musicians who make a
commercial business of adapting concertos by once popular masters for every
instrument through transpositions in other keys and through alteration of pas-
sages without compunction. Indeed it is certain that composers even write
concertos in advance for a possible commission without yet knowing for which
instrument the passages still lacking should be suited. But these are only spe-
cial cases. The concerto is subject to a far more detrimental abuse, however,
in that the expression “‘to play in company” all too often is understood as noth-
ing more than to demonstrate skill in a clean and plain performance of the
melodic figures and in overcoming well-chosen difficulties. Instead the skill
attained should be used only to present the expression of certain feelings
through an extremely perfected performance appropriate to them.

If concertos were composed and performed more generally according to a
better model, then many men of taste would be more satisfied with this type of
composition. Sulzer, for example, whose judgments concerning the other
kinds of compositions we so readily defer to, says of the concerto that it is
basically nothing but an exercise for composer and player and an entirely inde-
terminate pleasure for the ear, aiming at nothing more.®

But is this verdict perhaps too harsh? Should not this judgment be directed
towards only such concertos as are condemned by discerning concerto players
themselves?

If one disregards the abuses of the concerto, shown before, which do not
yet generally prevail, why should the concerto alone be merely exercise for

82. Sulzer, ed., Allgemeine Theorie, ‘‘Concert,”’ 1:299. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 240
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 209

composer and player? Why should this composition alone be a mere pleasure
for the ear, aiming at nothing further?

Sulzer concedes that in the sonata music can show its ability to portray feel-
ings without words. Why should music have this ability less in the concerto,
since the composer possesses more means in the richer accompaniment to in-
crease the expression of the main part than in the sonata? That which is partic-
ularly offensive to him seems to be the form of the concerto, for he says in the
article “‘Sonata’’: ‘“The form of a concerto appears to have more the purpose
of giving a skillful player the opportunity to be heard accompanied by many
instruments than to be used for the rendering of passions.”

If one discounts the ritornellos in a concerto, which after all can be as little
detrimental to the expression of feelings in this composition as they are in the
aria, what difference remains then between the form of the sonata and that of
the concerto which promotes the expression of feelings in the sonata but could
be detrimental to it in the concerto? Perhaps Sulzer understands here by the
form of the concerto merely that usage by which long passages are composed
in certain places in the periods of a concerto which are nothing more than an
exercise for the player, because in most cases they have so little in common
with the expression of a definite feeling that they may be transferred to any
concertos? This seems to be the most probable. Only the presence of these
passages is anything but essential in the form of the concerto.

It appears to me that the concerto must be judged from an entirely different
point of view than the solo. The expression of feeling by the solo player is like
a monologue in passionate tones, in which the solo player is, as it were, com-
muning with himself; nothing external has the slightest influence on the ex-
pression of his feeling. But consider a well-worked-out concerto in which, dur-
ing the solo, the accompanying voices are not merely there to sound this or
that missing interval of the chords between the soprano and bass. There is a
passionate dialogue between the concerto player and the accompanying or-
chestra. He expresses his feelings to the orchestra, and it signals him through
short interspersed phrases sometimes approval, sometimes acceptance of his
expression, as it were. Now in the allegro it tries to stimulate his noble feelings
still more; now it commiserates, now it comforts him in the adagio. In short,
by a concerto I imagine something similar to the tragedy of the ancients,
where the actor expressed his feelings not towards the pit, but to the chorus.
The chorus was involved most closely with the action and was at the same time
justified in participating in the expression of feelings. Then the listener, with-
out losing anything, is just the third person, who can take part in the passion-
ate performance of the concerto player and the accompanying orchestra. Pon-
der these ideas further, listen to most of C. P. E. Bach’s concertos, which so
completely correspond to this ideal, or better, from which this ideal is derived,

83. Ibid., “‘Sonate,”’ 4:688. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 241
============================================================

210 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

and then judge whether the concerto is no more than mere exercise for com-
poser and player, no more than mere pleasure for the ear, aiming at nothing
else.

§120.

The first allegro of the concerto contains three main periods performed by the
soloist, which are enclosed by four subsidiary periods performed by the or-
chestra as ritornellos.

In modein concertos, the first ritornello is generally worked out at length.
It consists of the principal melodic sections of the plan of the allegro, which are
brought into a different connection and extended through other means than in
the solo of the concerto part.® With these sections a few suitable subsidiary
ideas are connected in the ritornello which again lead to a main idea.

At present this ritornello takes three different forms. It either (1) makes up
only one period in which the melody stays in the main key throughout the en-
tire passage (with the exception of short passing modulations); or (2) consists
of two periods connected with one another. In this case, when based in a ma-
jor key, there is a modulation to the key of the fifth; ® after the V-phrase of this
key a cantabile phrase from the solo is played, and then the first period of the
ritornello is closed with a formal cadence in this key. With the caesura note of
this cadence, a phrase begins which leads the melody back into the main key in
one of those ways described in section 31. After the return to the main key, a
melodic section of the first period is usually repeated, in order to give the entire
ritornello a certain kind of completion and to maintain the unity of this passage
before the close follows with the principal cadence in the main key. However
the first ritornello is also formed so that (3) there is indeed a formal modulation
into the key of the fifth, and after the V-phrase in it a principal melodic section
is played in this key. But immediately afterwards, without a formal close in this
key, there is a modulation back to the main key and the ritornello concludes.
This last form is the most usual in more modern concertos.

abuses to which it is subjected entirely useless and unnecessary. [K. ]

The first main period of the solo part will contain the plan of the work; as the composition of the
plan constitutes the first phase of the creative process, then it follows that the solo period will be
composed first. Koch regards the opening ritornello which introduces the solo as part of the
realization of the work, a product of the second phase of the creative process in which the ideas
contained in the plan are modified and manipulated. The content of the solo part must be deter-
mined before it can be introduced by a ritornello; hence Koch’s order of composition. See 2:67
ff. [B.]
