§121.

Lately a short introductory passage of slow tempo and earnest character has
preceded the first allegro of a concerto.*’ It is used in modern symphonies and
has already been described above in connection with the symphony. In the
concerto also, this passage usually closes with the so-called half cadence, after
which the ritornello of the first allegro begins without interruption.

§122.

With the entry of the first main period, or the first solo part, the cadence of the
ritornello generally comes to rest completely before the solo begins. That is,
the solo does not enter with the caesura note, but first begins its period after a
fully completed close of the ritornello. On the other hand, the second solo and
also the entrance of the [second] ritornello do not leave the cadence com-
pletely at rest but enter with its caesura tone.

§123. :

Nothing remains to be noted in connection with the three main periods of the
solo part, for they have the same external arrangement and the same course of
modulation as the three main periods in the first allegro of the symphony. The
type of melody, on the other hand, is very similar to that of the sonata. It is just
as developed as in the sonata, but usually is more protracted through the
means of extension already known to us, and the melodic sections are more
connected by omitting the ending formulas of phrases. At the caesura-note of
phrases or incises, the melody of the main part is sometimes interrupted by the
orchestra with short passages, which consist either of repeated segments of the
principal melody or of phrases which occurred only in the ritornello.

In well-composed concertos, every single part of the accompanying orches-
tra makes its contribution to the main part, according to the ideal described
before. As a segment of the whole, it is involved in the passionate dialogue
and has the right to show its feelings concerning the main part through short
phrases. To this end, these voices do not always wait for the conclusion of the
incise or phrase in the principal part, but throughout its performance may be
heard alternately in brief imitations; yet they must be placed and arranged so
that they do not obscure the performance of the main part.

The beginning composer can get to know the treatment of the accompani-
ment to the concerto just described nowhere better than in the clavier concer-
tos of C. Ph. E. Bach. Besides forming the melody so that it permits such

imitations in the accompaniment, much practical experience and artistic feel-
ing are required not to harm the main voice.

§124.

At the caesura tone of the first main period which closes in the key of the fifth,
the second ritornello begins again with the main phrase. It repeats a few me-
lodic sections which already were contained in the first ritornello and closes
likewise with a formal cadence in the key of the fifth. With the caesura-tone of
this cadence, the second solo part begins in this key again. In fact, the second
solo usually starts with a melodic section which was not contained in the first
period, but is a powerful, conspicuous, yet suitable subsidiary idea, which
again leads very appropriately to a main idea.

This period is treated like the second main period of the first allegro of the
symphony; therefore it is closed in the minor key of the sixth, at times also in
the minor key of the second or third. With its caesura-note, a short ritornello
begins which forms the subsidiary period already described in connection with
the symphony. By means of a melodic section that is extended through se-
quence or the continuation of a metrical formula, the ritornello modulates
back into the main key, in which it closes with a V-phrase; thus the third solo
of the principal part can again begin in the main key.

§125.

In its form the third solo part in the concerto again resembles the third main
period of the first allegro of the symphony. At the caesura tone of this period,
the ripieno parts usually introduce, by means of a few measures, a fermata on
the six-four chord of the keynote. The soloist sustains this longer than the
other instruments and connects with it either a free fantasy or a capriccio,
which is mistakenly called a cadenza because it is made at the close of the com-
position. With the caesura tone of this so-called cadenza, which always ends
with a formal cadence, the last ritornello begins. This generally consists of the
last melodic sections of the initial ritornello, with which the entire first allegro
concludes.

§126.

The adagio of the concerto usually takes the form already described in section
84 in connection with the aria. In the more modern concertos, however, in-
stead of the customary adagio, often a so-called romance is composed. This
has a definite character, which one can best get to know from Sulzer’s descrip-
tion of the romance in poetry.

‘“‘Nowadays,”’ says Sulzer, ‘“‘the name romance is given to short narrative
songs in the extremely simple and somewhat antiquated tone of the old
rhymed romances. Their content may be a passionate, tragic, amorous, or
even merely entertaining narrative.® . . . Ideas and expression must be of the
utmost simplicity and very naive.”

88. In music this last type of romance is not used, because now it is composed only in slow
tempo. [K.] Sulzer, ed., Allgemeine Theorie, ‘‘Romanze,”’ 4:544. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 244
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 213

This composition usually takes the form of the rondo, which already has
been described at length.
§127.
The last section of the concerto is either an allegro or presto. It may take the
form of the first allegro, or an ordinary rondo with very amplified episodes, or
variations on a short melody consisting of two sections. The essentials of the
variation form have also been mentioned in connection with the last allegro of
the symphony.

2. THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS IN THE FIRST
MAIN PERIODS OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS

§128.

It has been shown in section 45 how three melodic sections can be formed in
their punctuation when they make up the first period of a composition. Be-
cause more than three sections tend to be connected in the first period of
larger compositions, we must now examine how several sections can be con-
nected in regard to their punctuation.

§129.

It follows from the preceding section that after the first main periods of all
compositions composed in the major mode have modulated from the main
key to that of the fifth, they also close in this key.

This first period thus divides into two sections, namely, one in which the
main key prevails, and one in which the key of the fifth prevails. Since no more
than two phrases take place in the main key, namely, a I- anda V-phrase, and
the melodic section which modulates into the fifth must be concluded with a
V-phrase in this key, thus a certain principal form of this first period arises
which, excepting a few deviations, is common to all first periods of larger com-
positions. It consists in this: the period contains four main punctuation sec-
tions, or four main resting points. Two of these belong to the main key and are
made by the first two melodic sections with a I- and V-phrase. The third, how-
ever, modulates towards the key of the fifth, in which it concludes with a V-
phrase, whereupon the fourth main punctuation section closes the period with
a cadence in this key. The passage used to demonstrate melodic means of ex-
tension in section 72 is an example of this form in which the main punctuation
sections are mixed with no subsidiary melodic sections.

§130.

If the composition is in a minor key and the first period is to close in the minor
key of its fifth, the four main punctuation sections have just the same nature as
in a major key, with the only difference that the caesuras of the I-phrase and
the cadence fall on minor triads. The caesuras of the V-phrases, on the other
hand, are based on the major harmonic triad in this minor key also, for rea-


============================================================
PAGE 245
============================================================

214 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

sons already known to us.®? With the use of the minor key, however, espe-
cially in larger compositions, it is more usual that the first main period modu-
lates to the major Key of its third, and that the period closes in it. In this case,
the first two melodic sections conclude with the I- and V-phrase of the minor
key; the third usually begins still in this key, but modulates to the third, in
which it closes with a V-phrase. After this the fourth main section ends the
period with a cadence in this major key; see example 371.

Example 371

The third melodic section can also enter in the major key immediately after
the V-phrase of the minor key, for instance, when the eighth, ninth, and tenth
measures of the preceding example are composed as in example 372.

Example 372

89. Koch explains in ‘“‘The consonant combinations of tones”’ (1:52-53) that there is a major
triad on the fifth degree in the minor mode; the seventh degree of the scale has been altered so
that it may function as a leading tone. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 246
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 215

§131.

Still other subsidiary melodic sections are connected with these four main
punctuation sections of the period through which the feeling to be expressed is
either more closely defined or modified. Now (if no passing modulation is
made), no other resting points take place in this period with which these sub-
sidiary melodic sections can be concluded, and, according to the rule in sec-
tion 34, two melodic sections immediately following one another may not end
in the very same key with similar phrase-endings. Thus the way in which sev-
eral melodic sections can be combined with the four main punctuation sec-
tions of the period must be examined or, in other words, how more melodic
sections must be formed with regard to their punctuation if they are to be con-
nected in the first period of a composition.

§132.

If more melodic sections are to be connected with the four main resting points
of the period, that is with its I- and V-phrase in the main key and with its V-
phrase and cadence in the subsidiary key to which it modulates, this connec-
tion is generally accomplished through the following four means, namely:

1. by taking away the completeness of the first of two melodic sections im-
mediately following one another, which together can make up only a single
main resting point of the period, and connecting it with the following melodic
section so that the second becomes a mere appendix, a section clarifying the
first; thus together they make up only a single main resting point of the period;

2. by the compounding of two melodic sections;

3. by an appropriate repetition of a phrase-ending by means of another me-
lodic section; and

4. by an appendix which is connected with the main period.

§133.

The first means of combining several phrases in one period is by connecting
two complete melodic sections which cannot properly follow one another be-
cause of the similarity of their phrase-endings so that they both can be used
only as a single extended phrase. This is done either (1) by entirely taking
away the completeness of the first melodic section; or (2) by forming the
phrase-ending of the first melodic section less completely than that of the sec-
ond. (See section 55.)

§134.

The first alternative (technically speaking) can be used only if the first of the
two melodic sections consists of two similar segments. Supposing the two com-
plete phrases of example 373 were directly connected in the period, the closing
formula of the second would have an unpleasant effect on our feeling because
another complete melodic section had already made its caesura on the same
triad of that key immediately before.


============================================================
PAGE 247
============================================================

216 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Example 373
Allegretto

However the first phrase does consist of two similar segments and, accord-
ing to section 122 of the second volume, its completeness can be removed by
making the caesura of the second segment similar to the caesura of the first.
Thus, through the use of this means, it attaches itself directly to the following
melodic section, and then both together make up only a single complete
phrase in which there is no unpleasant effect, as in example 374.

Example 374
Allegretto

§135.

In the second case, the first melodic section does not consist of two similar
segments. Both phrases which end on the same triad are to be more closely
connected by expressing the caesura of the first melodic section less com-
pletely than the caesura of the second, according to section 55. This means is
very limited when it is considered only technically, for both melodic sections
must already have a certain inner relation to one another, if they are to be
combined so that the second can make up an appendix clarifying the first. The
passage of example 375 is of this nature.”

through this means.

§136.

The second means of connecting more melodic sections in a period is by com-
pounding two or more complete phrases, that is, combining them with one
another so that they either (1) appear in the outer form of a single phrase, or
(2) indeed affect our feeling as two different phrases, but in the structure of
the period can only be treated as a single main punctuation section.

§137.

In the first case, when the two successive melodic sections are compounded so
that they both appear in the form of a single phrase, the second segment of the
first phrase must always be omitted. The first segment of it is generally either
repeated or extended in another way with this process.

Both the V-phrases contained in example 376, which immediately follow
one another, are of the type that offends our feelings.

The second of these phrases is unpleasant partly because they have the
same ending formulas and partly because the second phrase begins with the
same tones and figure of notes already present immediately before the caesura
in the first and different melodic section. Now if both these phrases are con-
nected in one period, their unpleasant effect can easily be eliminated by omit-

218 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

ting the second segment of the first phrase, which moreover is contained

nearly completely in the beginning of the second phrase, and by directly con-

necting its first segment with the following [second] phrase. The melodic suc-

cession favors this connection between the first segment and the beginning of
‘the second phrase, as in example 377.

Example 377

Through the omission of this second segment, both the repetition of the
same figure in the fifth and seventh measures contained in the preceding ex-
ample (example 376) and also the bad effect made by two ending formulas
immediately following one another on the triad of the fifth are avoided. The
two melodic sections now appear in the period as a single phrase in a suitable
and useful manner.

§138.

The second way of compounding phrases has already been described in sec-
tion 121 of the second volume. To summarize, two melodic sections, of which
the caesura of the first and the initial tone of the second have the same har-
monic basis, are connected so that the second of these phrases is already be-
gun on the caesura tone of the first. Through this process, the measure con-
taining the proper caesura of the first melodic section disappears, and the
initial tone of the second phrase at the same time serves as the caesura of the
first. Thus the measure in which this process occurs is also doubly counted with
respect to the rhythmic relations of phrases, namely once as that measure in
which the first phrase makes its caesura and also as that one with which the
following phrase begins.

For example, if the melodic sections in example 378 are connected with the
initial phrase in section 135 (example 375, of which I shall here place only the
last half for the sake of brevity), after the first of these phrases, completely
concluded in the fourth measure, the second, which also is a I-phrase, would
have a bad effect. This second phrase cannot be ended as a V-phrase because
it is the following melodic units (given in section 137 [example 376]) which


============================================================
PAGE 250
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 219

should close in this way. Now if this second phrase is brought into the period, it
should most appropriately begin at once on the caesura note contained in the
fourth measure of the preceding phrase. The reason for this placement is that
not only is the second phrase thereby closely connected with the first, but also
both I-phrase endings are avoided which made an unpleasant effect when im-
mediately following each other; this can be seen from the following com-
pounding of these phrases at the sign + in m. 4 of example 379.

Example 378

Indeed, when the melody is to be arranged very flowingly, the formal I-
phrase can be entirely omitted. The third phrase, entering in the tenth mea-
sure of example 378 and ending with a V-phrase (as seen in section 137
[example 376]), can be connected with the preceding phrase through just
this means. This phrase begins in the tenth measure (for in this process the
upbeat is not taken into consideration) with the tone G on the harmonic basis
of the triad G B D, the same chord on which the preceding phrase made its
caesura in the ninth measure. Only the initial tone G need be changed into the
third B, lying six degrees lower, and this phrase also can begin on the caesura
tone of the preceding phrase, as seen in example 379 at + +, m. 8.

§140.
The repetition of phrase-endings is, in accordance with section 132, the third
means through which several melodic sections can be combined in our period.

To be sure, in section 34 the rule was given that two successive melodic
sections in one key should not be closed with the same phrase-endings, but it
was also mentioned there that very often the rule permits an exception.

If at the beginning of a period two complete sections are closed as I-phrases
and the last section cannot be regarded as an appendix to the first, then in most
cases the second of these sections offends our feeling with its I-phrase. Never-
theless, from time to time two phrases which begin a period are connected in
this way and yet please our feelings completely; example 381 is such a case.”!

Example 381

§141.

With the use of V-phrases, however, it happens more often that when two of
them are composed successively in the same key, our feeling is not shocked.
They are used with good effect for the most part when the first V-phrase 1s
somewhat rushing and extended or connected with more melodic sections,
but the following V-phrase is cantabile. Thus through the repetition of the V-
phrase, often several melodic sections are connected in the period. In example
382, which is the continuation of the main period begun in section 139 (exam-
ple 380), the V-phrase in the main key, mm. 5-10, is repeated by means of
different melodic sections as a V-phrase in the key of the fifth, mm. 22-25.”

§142.

Finally the fourth and last means of connecting several melodic sections in a
period is to present some of these after the cadence as an appendix or a subsid-
lary period which continues in the same key.

This means, however, can be applied without restriction only in instrumen-
tal pieces; in vocal movements it can be used solely in the working-out, that is,
after all the verses of the poetry contained in the first period have been pre-
sented. For example, in an aria in which the first section contains many verses,
it would be exceedingly improper to break up their continuity through a ca-
dence before the conclusion of their period and to bring forward the remain-
ing verses as an appendix after the cadence.

In instrumental movements, on the other hand, this appendix can be used
without any limitation in two different ways. Either another melodic section
can be connected with the closing phrase by omitting a measure, as in example
383 at m. 3, or the cadence can first come to rest and afterwards a I- or V-
phrase can follow. With the succeeding closing phrase can be connected still
another melodic section also formed as a closing phrase. The passage in exam-
ple 383, which finally ends the period begun in section 139 (example 380) and
continued in section 141 (example 382), illustrates all these methods of con-
necting phrases.

Remark 2

It would be too lengthy and also superfluous to show the beginning com-

give the period the necessary length. In the period presented piecemeal above,
they were brought together only for the sake of illustration.

§143.
Before leaviig this first principal period, the most important and frequent de-
viations from its main form must still be examined.

First, it is not necessary that the first phrase of a period always be a I-phrase
or that the modulation be led into the key of the fifth in the third punctuation
section. The first phrase, or the so-called theme, can also be a V-phrase in
compositions of greater length. The first melodic section having ended as a I-
or V-phrase, the second main punctuation section may already modulate to
the key of the fifth and form a V-phrase in it. In this case, if the period is to
contain four main melodic sections, either two V-phrases must be composed
in succession, as in example 385; or, in case both melodic sections are of the

second V-phrase must be connected with the closing phrase following it,
through the means shown in section 136, as in example 386; or, if the offensive
V-phrase is not to be omitted from the period, it must be changed into a clos-
ing phrase and brought-forward as an appendix after the cadence, as in exam-
ple 387.

Remark

Occasionally with a cadence in the main key replacing a V-phrase, the pre-
ceding melodic section, which in this case can be a I- as well as a V-phrase, is
much extended or is connected with another melodic section. This phrase
thereby often takes on the character of a regular introduction, but has the
Same meter and tempo as the main movement. If the phrase is formed in this
way, usually the actual theme to be worked out in the main movement is set
forth with its subsidiary phrases just after this introduction.

§146.
In another deviation from the main form of the first period it often happens

that, after the V-phrase in the key of the fifth yet before the cadence, a me-

lodic section is inserted which is a I-phrase of this key, as in example 391.
Example 391

§147.
This is the form with its most usual deviations® in first main periods of those

compositions which have not the special form of the rondo or of variations on

a short me‘ody.
According to first impression, for example, the first period of an aria often

96. A brief survey of musical compositions will show many a deviation from this form not
discussed here. My aim here was merely to show the usual. For where would there be room
enough to describe and to illustrate with examples all deviations from the usual which some-
times appear and are of little importance? [K.]


============================================================
PAGE 260
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 229

appears to be in a form entirely different from that of the symphony. But this
noteworthy difference of both compositions is caused not through the punctu-
ation form, but partly through the inner nature of the melodic sections, partly
through the way in which they are brought into this form, and partly also
through their accompanmment. For example, in this form an adagio is different
from an allegro. A composition which has only one main part arrives at this
form differently from one in which all parts are allowed to present the most
outstanding phrases.

Fewer melodic sections are used in the adagio, for example, which, because
of its slow tempo, cannot be as long as an allegro. Therefore usually a I- and V-
phrase of the main key and a V-phrase and cadence in the fifth [degree] are
indeed sufficient resting points, and the means of connecting several melodic
sections are used far more seldom. Indeed, those very feelings which are ex-
pressed in an adagio.favor the presence of many resting points because they
are more lingering than swift. The first main period of the andante by Haydn
in section 53 (example 324), for example, contains no more than five different
melodic sections although it is very long. Yet, until the first cadence, no other
melodic means of extension is used than repetition. All these melodic sections
have formal phrase-endings, and not until after the cadence are a few com-
pound phrases found in the appendix of the period.”

The melodic sections in the allegro of a sonata or a concerto are treated
entirely differently with regard to the punctuation form. Here several of the
means pointed out before must be applied in order to bring a greater number
of melodic sections into the period. Also several means of extension are used
here. The more frequent compounding of melodic sections through which the
melody becomes more continuous completely suits those feelings which are
usually expressed in the allegro of these compositions. The first period con-
tained in the preceding sections, beginning in the 139th section (example 380),
gives an example of melodic sections taking the form of a first period of an
allegro, specifically in a composition which has only one main part. This peri-
od also can serve as the first solo of an opening allegro of a concerto. Still more
different from the adagio is the arrangement of melodic sections in pieces in
which all parts may present outstanding phrases or are main parts for a time.
Thus in the first period, the melodic sections of the first allegro of a symphony
must necessarily be treated entirely differently from those of the adagio, the
allegro of the concerto, or the sonata.

In the first allegro of the symphony, for reasons already explained in the
first section [of chapter 4], the melodic sections are less developed than in
compositions which have only one main part performed by a single person.
Rather, they must distinguish themselves through inner power and emphasis,
and the feeling must be presented with momentum and not in extreme detail.

97. The closing phrase which ends in m. 49 is elided with the beginning of the appendix; thus
there is a compound phrase at the cadence, and not after the cadence as Koch says. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 261
============================================================

230 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

Now because in the first allegro of this composition a noble or, more often,
forceful feeling prevails, most I- and V-phrases are not formally left at rest
(even if this could take place without offending the feeling); rather they are
passed over by means of suppression of a measure, so that the melody be-
comes all the more continuous. The necessity of frequently passing over the
formal phrase endings in such a movement is also due to the fact that the sub-
sidiary parts, for example the second violin or the bass, often play phrases per-
formed by the first violin when it is occupied with a new melodic section, or
they imitate each other while the first violin only functions to fill out the
chords. For in a symphony the subsidiary parts would not make much of an
impression if the second violin were only complementary to or in constant uni-
son with the first violin, and the bass produced nothing but the uninteresting
roots of the chords.

It has already been mentioned in the description of the symphony that of-
ten, in the first period of its allegro, a formal phrase-ending is not heard until
the V-phrase in the key of the fifth presents itself. This is seldom passed over,
particularly because it is usually followed by a cantabile phrase. Now the
avoidance of most remaining formal phrase-endings is no less than a standard
by which the worth of the entire movement can be measured; this much is
certain: the first allegro of a symphony in which the I- ‘and V-phrase-endings
are presented as formally as in an andante or allegretto can never have that
effect which we expect from it.

In order to make the beginning composer as familiar as possible with the
treatment and method of connecting melodic sections in the first allegro of the
symphony, I shall here insert an example of such a piece. The means of con-
nection and extension of the melodic sections which are used will be indicated
by the number of that section in which each has been explained (example
392).

Example 392
Sinfonia

3. THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS IN THE
REMAINING PERIODS OF COMPOSITIONS

§148.

When many melodic sections are to be connected in the remaining periods of
compositions, the same means are used which have been described in the pre-
ceding section. Thus in this portion there only remains a study of the most
usual punctuation forms of these periods.

§149.

The second section or second reprise of a composition may contain only a sin-
gle main period, that is, in this section no cadence is made in a key related to
the main key. If this is the case, the entire period breaks down into two halves
with regard to the course of its modulation. The first half is devoted to one or
two related keys; no cadence is made in them and instead there is a modula-
tion back again in a V-phrase of the main key, with which the first half closes as
its main resting point. In the second half of the period, the principal melodic
sections are repeated in the main key.


============================================================
PAGE 265
============================================================

234 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

8150.
No specific punctuation form of the first half of this period can be given be-
cause it may modulate very arbitrarily, sometimes into this, sometimes into
that related key. There is no difficulty with the sequence of melodic sections
regarding their punctuation formulas, because the melody remains not long in
any one key and because, according to sections 35 and 36, the order of I- and
V-phrases is not limited by a change in key.

The main melodic phrase or the theme” in the key of the fifth! begins this
first half of the second period, and in the continuation of the movement three
different situations are common.

1. The theme may be presented until its phrase-ending in the key of the
fifth; afterwards, without first returning again to the main key, a more or less
extended modulation in the minor key of the sixth or also in the minor key of
the second or third is made by means of another melodic section. There is no
cadence, but instead there is a modulation back into a V-phrase of the main
key. An outline of this form is found in the movement in section 45, example
292.

2. After it has begun in the key of the fifth, the theme may be modified so
that it ends again in the main key. In this instance, there is a modulation from
the main key into one of the minor keys mentioned before and the harmonic
progression is different from the preceding case. For example, the movement
quoted before may be formed as in example 393.

Example 393

99. At times the second section begins with either a subsidiary phrase or a melodic section
not even contained in the first period, but which necessarily must lead again to a main phrase.
The last case is common only in concertos; on the other hand, the first can be found in all kinds
of compositions. [K.]

100. The application to the minor key has already been made several times and will be famil-
iar to the beginner from the first section of [the second division in] volume 2. [K.] In 2:237-38,
Koch gives a very brief summary of the modulations to be found in a composition in the minor
mode. In the first period, it modulates from the main key to the major key of the third; the
second period features the minor key of the fifth. This order may be reversed as well. The third
period is devoted to the main key; the key of the fourth may be heard temporarily, but there is
no close made in it. [B.]


============================================================
PAGE 266
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 235

Example 393 (Continued)

3. The theme may be repeated in the main key after it has first been given in
the key of the fifth. Then the following phrase, which modulates to another
related key, can keep the same form as in the case immediately preceding, as
in example 394.

Example 394

Remark

The melodic sections contained in these examples, for the sake of space
given here as basic phrases, not only can be extended through all the means
already known, but also can be combined with other phrases by compounding,
if the movement is to be of greater length.

§151.

In the first half of the [second] period, there is a modulation back into the main
key, and it is closed with a V-phrase of this key. Then either the theme is re-
peated once again in the main key or, if omitted, the second half of the first pe-
riod, transposed into the main key, is immediately played.

It is unnecessary to give extended examples of the three different forms of
this second period which have been pointed out. The beginner will find them
in nearly every adagio or andante as also in every second period of those arias
which are in the older form.

in example 396. }%!

102. Examples 397 and 398 are from the first movement of a harpsichord sonata in F major,
the first of the six ‘‘Prussian”’ sonatas (W. 48, no. 1), written by C. P. E. Bachin 1742. It is found

Example 397 (Continued)

C.P.E. Bach

§152.

In compositions which consist of three main periods, the second one ends with
a formal cadence in a closely related key. The following three distinct methods
of treatment of this second main period are used in modern compositions,
which, however, allow a few variations from time to time.!%

The first and most usual treatment is that, after the first phrase has been
played in the key of the fifth (it may be the real theme or, as is usual in the
concerto, another appropriate phrase), one or two melodic sections are re-
peated again in the main key asa I- and V-phrase. After this, a melodic section
follows which modulates to the minor key of the sixth or third. This usually
tends to be either much extended or compounded with other phrases, and
concludes in the fifth of the key in which the period is to end.

After the V-phrase, that section which preceded the closing phrase in the
first period is usually repeated in this minor key. Then the closing phrase is
played, either with or without its appendix, and thus concludes this period.

If a second period in this form were to follow the first period which was
begun in section 139 (example 380), it would be treated approximately as in
example 399.

in Die preussischen Sonaten; Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach, ed. Rudolf Steglich (New York,
1966-67). [B.]

102. [B.]

Remark

After the theme has been played in the fifth, two phrases in the main key
need not always be placed before that phrase which modulates into the key of
the sixth or third. This phrase can also begin after a single phrase in the main
key or else immediately after the first melodic section of the period (according
to section 150). Two phrases were placed in this example in order that the be-
ginner could become familiar with as many cases as possible where I- and V-
phrases can alternate with one another without detriment.

§153.

In the second common treatment of this period the theme in the key of the
fifth is immediately connected with a phrase which is extended either in the
undefined way described in section 63, or by means of the continuation of a
segment of it. This extension is carried on until the V-phrase of that key in
which the period closes (see example 400).

Example 400
Allegro

After this V-phrase, the remaining portion of the period is treated just as
indicated in the preceding section.


============================================================
PAGE 271
============================================================

240 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

§154.
The third treatment of this second period is found mainly in the symphony. It
differs from those preceding in that the period is begun with a melodic section
of the first period, often only with a segment of it. This segment is then contin-
ued without a formal phrase-ending, so that by and by there are passing modu-
lations in several keys, some closely related, some distantly. This process goes
on either until the V-phrase of that key is reached in which the period is to
close, or it is continued to the very end of the period. The first case is more
usual than the second, and, as noted already in section 102, examples of this
form are found in many symphonies by Haydn and Dittersdorf. The second
case, however, does not appear as often and requires much skill to lead the
modulation into several keys in a good way. I shall insert a movement here
which demonstrates the latter of these ways.'™

The passage in example 401 is the theme of a symphony by Pleyel; the ap-
pendix of the closing phrase of the first period is found in example 402.!%

Example 403 (Continued)

Remark

This passage also illustrates what was already noted at the end of section
102, namely, that the second period of an allegro in the symphony is some-
times begun in a related key other than the fifth.

8155.
In the third period, most melodic sections of the first period are presented ina
somewhat altered connection in the main key.

By means of a melodic section which is connected with the cadence of the
second period, there is first a modulation back into the main key in which the
third period begins, usually with the theme. After the repetition of the theme,
a few melodic sections from the first half of the first period are either put in
another combination or given a new shape, in the course of which there is usu-
ally a brief modulation to the key of the fourth. Finally the second half of the
first period is repeated in the main key and with that the movement concludes.

In the concerto and sonata usually a passage [passaggio] is connected with
one or the other of the last melodic sections. It generally enters on the caesura-
note of a phrase by means of the suppression of a measure, and ends just as the
phrase also would have if the passage had not been introduced; thus it gives
rise to no important deviation from the usual form of this last period.

The andante by Haydn in section 53 (example 324), from m. 105 of the sec-
ond section until its close, is an example of such a last period in its usual form,
together with the preceding return of the modulation to the main key.

8156.
Before leaving entirely the form of periods, we must still talk of ritornellos
which are connected with the aria, the chorus, and the concerto.

The first ritornello of these compositions has its characteristic forms as an
introductory or preparatory passage, which already have been described
above in the first section of this chapter.

The difference between the form of an initial ritornello and the following
first main period of the composition is principally that it closes in the main key,
and not in the most closely related key as does the period.


============================================================
PAGE 276
============================================================

THE ARRANGEMENT OF LARGER COMPOSITIONS 245

Although this ritornello includes sections of the main period, it does not
(particularly in the concerto) contain each and every phrase of it, but only
those which appropriately can be played by several instrumentalists simulta-
neously with ease and the necessary precision of execution. Other melodic
sections which are non-essential but suited to the main period are connected
with these phrases. Indeed, in the ritornello of the concerto, even the main
phrase of the concerto part is not always used. Often another fitting idea is
chosen which can be combined as a principal phrase with those melodic sec-
tions which are carried over from the main period into the ritornello.

§157.

In the ritornellos of arias, choruses, and the adagios of concertos, which usu-
ally tend not to be very long, no formal modulations are used, but only occa-
sional passing ones. After the playing of the I- and V-phrases, another phrase
is often compounded with the latter one. Two possibilities may occur: either
this is followed by a closing phrase with or without an appendix; or, after the
V-phrase, another melodic section from the main period that closes again with
a V-phrase or that is compounded with the closing phrase is used.

§158.

Sometimes the ritornello of the aria is longer than usual, especially when the
vocal part is accompanied by one or more obbligato instruments. Then it as-
sumes one of those forms described in connection with the ritornello of the
concerto, of which examples will follow.

Because these examples will only concern the punctuation forms of the [ini-
tial] ritornellos, however, the preceding main period begun in section 139 (ex-
ample 380) will be made to fit these forms, in order not to have to insert three
different entire ritornellos.

The first of the punctuation forms of the first ritornello described in section
120 is obtained when the passage in section 139 (example 380) is taken as the
first half of the ritornello but connected with a few of the succeeding melodic
sections in the manner shown in example 404.

Example 404

The third punctuation form of the ritornello can be constructed very easily

§159.

The remaining ritornellos between the main periods of the compositions re-

ferred to above are formed from the melodic sections of the first ritornello.
In a concluding ritornello, either only the closing phrase of the first ri-

tornello with its appendix is used, or the theme once again precedes the clos-

ing phrase.


============================================================
PAGE 279
============================================================

248 THE CONNECTION OF MELODIC SECTIONS

The second ritornello is played between the first and second main period in
the same key in which the first period has closed. When the ritornello has its
own main phrase, it always begins with it in this key. Afterwards it presents
one or two melodic sections of the principal period and uses the same closing
phrase in this key with which the first ritornello had ended in the main key. If
the first ritornello had a long appendix after the cadence, it is usually omitted
in the second ritornello.

This treatise, already very long on account of the many necessary exam-
ples, need not be extended even more by illustrating the arrangements of the
punctuation in these ritornellos. Their forms will present no difficulties to the
beginning composer.

End of the third and last volume.
